THE RED PAGE.
* I notice you never buy theological books,’ said
A bookseller the other day. That is true: I have
no use for religious dogmas. Historically, scien-
tifically, they interest me, as showing the evolu-
tion of that curious thing, the human brain :
otherwise they seem a mere beating of the air.
Religion itself, as an aid—not to salvation but—
to aspiration, is another matter. The solemn
hush of a cathedral,
I don’t say ‘church,’ tor 4 churota' has suoh stiff,
narrow, barren, whitewashed, Nonconformist associations
—it’s almost as"bad as 4 chapel.’
broken by slow music; the
stateliness of the dim, decorated spaces; the
other-worldliness, as it were—not the worship, but
the atmosphere of worship ; the occasional earnest
souls 4 wistfully rounding vacuous arms to the
shape of embrape ’ of they know not what —
stretching yearning hands in love from this in-
sufficing ‘ bank and shoal of time ’ to that ‘ other
bank’ which reason denies and hereditary-instinct
O how pitifully craves for ;
Generally speaking, I agree that the religious yearning,
in a commonplace mind, is apt to bring evil by daunting
effort, by making the yearners neglect Ingersoll’s skim-
milk on earth (a fact) while they dream of butter beyond
the clouds (a fantasy). But I think that sometimes even
the commonplace mind draws from its craving for Heaven
a force in aid of the better life on Earth—that the reli-
gious ecstasy is not all wasted, but that some, a great
deal, of it is converted (literally according to Joule’s law,*
for whether thought-energy is or is not physical, in the
ordinary sense of * physical,’ there is no doubt that it
springs front, depends on, is created by, the physical
brain ; and its ‘ waves ’ re-act to produce changes in the
brain-substance) into earth-useful deed and endeavour.
It is to be argued, of course, that in so far as they are
capable of such response to the religious stimulus, ‘com-
monplace ’ minds are not commonplace. That is so.
Given any sensation or idea, the use that wili be
made of it depends on the nature which it stirs—
good soil yields a good crop, bad or unsuitable soil a bad
crop or none. Thu 9 my attitude to the perennial con-
troversy whether historically and on the whole religion
has proved'a bane or a blessing is . that controversy has
no locus loquendi—had the religious instinct been wiped
out altogether (which oouldn’t he did without wiping
out all the preliminaries of the universe) the blood of
wars and martyrdoms, for example, would possibly have
been shed just the same—had the passion which led
Calvin to burn Servetus been wanting, the very want of
it might have led somebody else to do something even
more dreadful to somebody else. That is, supposing
there was a-hale, a gap, in humanity in the place where
superstition and dogma are now and have been ensconced
—or supposing humanity had been ‘kinder’ born without
superstition and dogma, and never knew the use or
meaning of them—it is impossible to say whether
humanity would have been happier or otherwise. Thus
dispute is fruitless, for the only issue is a cul-de-sac.
And it must be fruitless—it is labelled fruitless a
priori, before it is born, even—because you can-
not conceive humanity with one of its essential
attributes knocked out or lopped off. Relying on
natural law* you can reason with some confidence from'
the known to the unknown ; but to attempt to reason
from humanity as any man sees it to an imaginary
humanity is to attempt to reason from unknown to un-
known—for what man knows humanity? What man
even knows himself?
these things are
worth knowing and clinging to. You should go
to church for stimulation. Bernard Shaw agree-
ably puts it in his essay on the subject. ‘Lot a
man go and renew himself for half-an-hour occa-
sionally in San Zeno at Verona, and he need eat
no corpses, nor drink any drugs or drams to
sustain him.’ But going to church need not
imply vegetarianism or any other do-without
habit —a plus does not demand a minus.
* * • •
The two books whioh, absorbed by an impres-
sionable brain of fifteen, laid the foundation for
this set of opinions, were Edward Maitland’s
4 The Pilgrim and the Shrine ’ and M. J, Savage’s
‘Beliefs about Man.’ There is not much that is
absolutely original in either, but they present
data and inferences in very lucid and sugges-
tive fashion; and even to-day are well worth
reading. * The Pilgrim and the Shrine’ makes a
special appeal to Australians, for much of it is
occupied by an Australian love-story, and the
author, Edward Maitland, seems to have b < n a
N S.W. Govt, official, and took some share in the
foundation of Sydney University. He represents
his imaginary hero (an approximate likeness of
himself?) as aiding to establish theS.U. ‘on a basis
broad as Truth itself, and capable of enduring to
all tune.’ ‘And the sectarians consoled themselves
with the prospect of being permitted to have their
own exclusive colleges, which, being affiliated to the
University, might perchance in time come to
overshadow and stifle it with their noxious
growth.’ Maitland represents hishero, too, as accu-
mulating ammunition ‘ for battering our colonial
school system’ (in the denominational fifties).
Some of liis.bullets, are adngirably moulded —
The-most perfect legislation is'that whioh combines the
greatest personal liberty with the greatest personal
security.
It is an act of injustice to apply funds derived from the
whole community to the promotion of the opinions of any
portion of it.
The admission of any distinctive tenet is a sentence of
exclusion against all who do not hold that tenet.
The first duty of a State is equal justice to all its mem-
bers, without reference to their opinions, religious or
political.
Maitland’s death was only the other day re-
corded. For the last few years his name has
been closely connected with that of Anna Kings-
ford, the curious religious-spiritualist whose life he
wrote. In view of his earlier work, one would
be glad if any reminiscences of his Australian
career oan be supplied.
* • * *
* The Pilgrim and the Shrine ’ is a personal
narrative of impressions and ideas ; always inte-
resting, often fascinating. There are some charm-
ing travel - pictures—South America, South Sea
Islands, Australia ; the running notes - and - com-
mentary deals chiefly with religious dogmas. At
half-way the idyll of Mary Travers, of Yarra-
dale and Bondi, is interwoven, and this remains
the leading skein at the close. An anecdote —
My host [in San Leon, Nicaragua] had a notice of
indulgence posted on his door, promising the remittance
of a third part of his sins to the purchaser. I asked why,
if he believed in the promise, he did not get three and so
have all his sins remitted. He answered with a shrug
that every good Christian is expected to buy one—it
costs little and does no harm. A sharp Yankee fellow-
traveller, hearing this conversation, observed that there
would still remain a residuum of sin no matter how many
indulgences were bought, for that each remits only a
third of the remainder. I had to tell the host, therefore,
that to get rid of all his sins it was necessary to buy the
three indulgences all exactly at the same moment. But,
alasl this emergency also has been seen and provided
against. The sinner can buy indulgences as often as he
pleases, but only one at a time 1 His liabilities may by
repeated purchases become ‘ small by degrees and beauti-
fully fine,’ but there is no place here below for one
altogether sinless.
• • • •
An argument—
A. 'God’s creation goes wrong. He has to struggle
long with difficulties, and strive by schemes and contri-
vances to save the countless host of his children from the
deadly malice of one whom he hath himself made, and
made too in full fore-knowledge (on the Maker's part) of
all the mischief he would do, and what it would cost to
subdue him. And after all He succeeds in rescuing but a
small fraction from the Evil One’s hands. For you hold
the gate of hell to be wide, so that many walk into it un-
awares, and that of heaven to be narrow and easily missed.
Thus far I am unable to recognise either the wisdom, the
power, the benefioence, or the justice of your God.’
B. —‘You forget that, having made man free, He could
not control his use of his freedom.’
A. — 4 I remember that, having made his children weak
and ignorant of what was best for them, you hold that
He placed them in a garden of forbidden delights, and re-
quired of them an obedience which, by your deification
of Christ, you admit could not be rendered by mere man,
however perfect, and when they yielded to the first
temptation to transgress a little, He condemned them
and their unborn offspring to unspeakable tortures for
evermore. That is, having made them finite and imper-
fect, He damned them for not being infinite and perfect,
and would only be propitiated towards them by the
blood and agony of the only innocent one, the only one
who had never offended Him in his life. No human
being requires a sacrifice or compensation before he can
pardon a repentant child. Is man more tender than God,
and is the thing made an unfaithful index to the charac-
ter of the Maker ?
‘Again, when man punishes he has in view the reforma-
tion of the offender as well as self-defence, whereas the
punishment inflicted by your God, having for its end, not
the benefit, but the increasing reprobation of the sufferer,
can proceed only from the bitterest feelings of revenge,
worthy the most malignant fiend the imagination can
conceive.’
B. What, does not the glory of a pure God demand
the infinite punishment of sin ?’
A. ‘Do as you please with the sin. lam speaking of
the sinner. According to your doctrine he perpetuates,
not destroys it. If He be so infinitely pure as to detest
that which you call sin, how came He to admit its defile-
ment into His work? If so infinitely just, how comes Be
to make the work of His own hands responsible for the
flaws of its construction? If so infinitely merciful and
loving, why so averse to pardon His errjng children ? If
so infinitely powerful, why allow an evil demon to de-
vastate the fair domains of ‘us creation ? Why, your
doctrine deposes God from His high place, and makes the
devil triumphant to all eternity. Calvinism, in spite of
your calling it “Evangelical Christianity,” is devil-
worship. Calvinism is Atheism. It is thus precisely be-
cause I accept your definition of God, that I cannot accept
your account of His dealings.’
B. ‘But the revelation He has given us in His word ? ’
A. ‘ A man’s meaning is ever better known by his
deeds than his words. Were an angel from heaven to
come and tell me such things of a being whom I loved and
respected, I should reject it as a slander and a lie. But
what you Call a revelation is an impossibility, for it
necessitates a prior Knowledge of the diviae in order to
know that anything is divinely predicated. All that a
man oan know is that he has a strong, an over-powering
impression, it-may be : but of-the source of that impres-
sion he can know nothing. And of its nature he can only
judge by comparing it with his ordinary experience.
An infallible revelation requires an infallible interpreter,
and botli are useless without an infallible understanding
to comprehend the interpretation.’
A fragment of the idyll—
From Herbert’s letter to Arnold.—Dear Old Friend,—
My happiness does not choke me now. I have grown
with the occasion, and instead of being overwhelmed in
the flood of my joy I oan touch the ground and keep my
head above water.
I have a son ; and the mother is safe. I thought I
knew what happiness was before.
Then it was the intensity of hope and longing. Now it
is the intensity of satisfaction.
How well says Emerson, Love must create. Lovers do
not know it, but they unconsciously love, not each other,
or their own present pleasure, but the future that shall
grow out of their affection ; the future in which each
appears ever more blended with the other, and they oan
no more imagine themselves separately.
It makes one so orthodox having this first child. Our
little trio fills infinity for us. It is an epitome of the
Trinity. Never more let them say ‘ Three are bad com-
pany.’ I find myself blandly regarding the dogmatists.
The nativity of Jesus is no fiction, no miracle, but true
of all mankind who love. Even the latest dogma of
Rome is a fact in nature. Who dare say that purity is
incompatible with maternity ? Woe to the blasphemer
of nature for whom these things are not sol- Poor,
sensitive, great-hearted David was indeed in low spirits,
even to hypochondria, when he fancied himself‘conceived
in sin.’ To attach a stain to any natural act is to stigma-
tise the Author of Nature. There is sin in the cold-
blooded bartering of youth and beauty ; in unions where
there is no mutual pervading heartfelt necessity for each
other’s companionship. But in the universal poem, * The
Divine Drama’ of Nature, there is no more beautiful,
canto than that in which a pure, noble-hearted girl yields
herself to the man she lqves.
I used always to look up to you as my senior, but now
I feel that lam yours. Our places are reversed. Hus-
band and father, am I not doubly promoted over your
head?
You should see the devotion of the mother to the child
—her child, for she almost grudges me any property in it,
while regarding me with a kind of puzzled gratitude. I
verily believe she looks upon the babe as the paragon of
mankind, destined to be the patriot reformer of his race.
Already he is the real master of the house. Everything
must give way to ‘ baby.’ lam made of as little account
as Joseph himself, as if I were only the reputed father.
And dearly do I enjoy the poetry of all this.
Nay, it seems to be in some sense true ; for the father’s
share in the matter really seems to be nothing compared
with the mother’s. Who dare limit the drama of the
* Holy Family ’ to one single representation ? . . .
Is not a book full of such passages worth pre-
serving from oblivion? ‘The Westminster Re-
view ’ justly ealled it ‘one of the wisest and most
charming of books.’
• * * o
Two other of Maitland’s works had some vogue
in their day—and the dust covers them. The
author says himself: ‘ln “The Pilgrim and the
Shrine’’was represented a youth escaping from
the trammels of traditional belief, and laying
himself wholly open to the influences of the living
Universe, so as to allow his entire system of re-
ligious faith to evolve itself freely from the contact
of external nature with his own soul. In “ Higher
Law” a similar method is applied to Morals.’ But
4 Higher Law ’ lacks the freshness and charm of
the eailier book, 1 hough it is still worthy and in-
teresting (it contains that shining epigram : The
edifice of the orthodox religion is founded on a
Misconception). ‘ By-and By ’is another attempt
at the imagination of a social utopia, and is as
tedious as most of its kind. Maitland’s youthful
fervour had left him when he wrote it, and his
theories want the breath of life. • Its chief interest
comes as an illustration of the author’s personal
history—it shows his brain on the downward path
to the region of emotional myths and figments
where so many tired old brains find rest which
Reason denies them. But in his Australian days
Edward Maitland must have baen singularly able
and lovable.
Writes Arab H. Purcell—
Your lengthy notice of Dr. Roth’s book would have
been highly valued had Dr. Roth treated of his subject
correctly—which unfortunately he did not. His vocabu-
lary is wrong a jumbling together of many dialects
from the east, north, south, west, and even South Sea
Islands, caused by the congregating together at Boulia of
remnants —and mighty small remnants —of the various
local .tribes that don't happen to be extinct—for the
author has written (as living entities) about some which
are extinct l Machoomba, Dr. Roth's word for ‘kanga-
roo, is a Diamantina river word from the Miorli tribe ;
Matuniba is the correct Bitta Bitta (not Pitta Pitta as
Dr. R. has it). Dingo should be Punamya, not Malalea;
and emu Goolburri, not Walcaje. The latter word is
from the Moorooboola tribe, down the Georgiana River,
a hundred miles away. Dr. Roth makes other similar
blunders.
R. B. Cunninghame-Graham, in ■ The Saturday
Review ’ —
The world is to the weak. The weak are the majority.
The weak of brain, of body, the knock-kneed and flat-
footed, muddle-minded, loose-jointed, ill-put-together,
baboon-faced, the white-eye-lashed, slow of wit. the prac-
tical, the unimaginative, forgetful, selfish, dense, the
stupid, fatuous, the “candle-moulded,” give us our laws,
impose theirstandard on us, their ethics, their philosophy,
canon of att, literary style, their jingling music,
vapid plays, their dock-tailed horses, coats with buttons
in the middle: of the back, their hideous fashions,
aniline colors, their Leaders, Leightons, Logsdails, their
false morality,, their supplemented monogamic marriage,
social injustice done to women, legal injustioe that men
endure, making them fearful of the law, even with*
good oase, when the opponent is a woman ; in sum, the
immense ineptitude of modern life with all its inequali-
ties, its meannesses, its petty miseries, contagious
diseases, its drink, its gambling, Grundy, Stock Ex-
change, and terror of itself, we owe to those, our pug
nosed brothers in the Lord, under whose rule we live.
‘ Ponte ’ writes re bush songs :
4 Ben Hali ’ is certainly not Australian. I’ve heard it
sung by new-chum cookies. ‘ Ry’s ’ picture some weeks
back sums up bush singing— 4 I don’t want any music 'cos
I know any gorsquantity of toons.’ The most used 4 toon ’
is ‘ The Wild Colonial Boy ’ —the ‘ terror to Orstraylians.’
There are numerous variations of the words, and the tune
readily adapts itself to the peculiarities of the singer.
Another popular ballad is descriptive of shearing life
You can get better poetry but worse realism than—
‘ Then when the shearin’s over they get their bits of
papers;
They to the nearest shanty go and cut such pretty
capers;
They call for one and then for two in a style that’s very
funny,
Till the landlord says, " Oh, this won’t do—you got
no money.”
With my bow-wow-wow,’ &c.
Another well-known song reminiscent of the ‘carrying
days is sung to ‘The Wild Colonial Boy ’ tune. It starts,
1 Come all ye jolly bull-punchers and listen to my rhyme.’
No bush-song is genuine unless prefaced by 4 Come all
ye.’
Writes T.D. —
Victor Daley is naturally puzzled over the injustice of
that sentence in his quoted game-rhyme,
* Off to prison you must go,
My fair Lady.’
English youngsters, however, sing
* Off to prison he must go,’
which makes all the difference. I don’t think words or
sense count so greatly with children as the facility with
which the rhymes wed themselves to their primitive
tunes. Else, however would we get such a corruption as
that contained in the last two of these lines—
‘Green gravel, green gravel, .
The grass is so green.
The fairest young damsel
That ever was seen.
* Oh, Mary 1 Oh, Mary !
Your true love is dead ;
He’s sent you a letter
To turn round your head.’
The young man again ; but all the pathos of his untimely
end is lost through the youngsters of generations paying
more regard to sound than to sense. Music is an instinct,
but poetry is an art.

than any other kind of trouble that affects the human frame.

hacking cough, falling out of the hair, &c., &c., should consult him, for he is now enabled

wonderful influence. Sufferers from any form of bron-
chitis, distressing' cough, difficulty in breathing, hoarse-

colds on the chest it is invaluable, asTTbffects a Complete
cure. It is most comforting in allaying tickling and irri-
tation in the throat, and giving strength to the voice, and
it never allows a cough or asthma to become chronic. No
house should be without it, as, taken at the beginning, a
dose is generally sufficient, and a complete cure is certain.
• “Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir,—l had a bad attafc
of Bronchitis for six months, with dreadful hacking cough
day and night and great quantity of phlegm. Night
sweats came on and pains in my chest, and I got so weak

nounced to be in consumption. I had a lot of medicines

reading in the Age of your ‘ Bronchitis Cure,’ I got a

shall recommend your Medicine to all my friends.—Yours
truly, E. SMITH, Halpin Park, Nhill. Victoria.”

Bards of the Backblocks .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE OLD HAND.
If you mention any billet, he is just ]the man to
fill it,
Though a blackleg and a grumbler, a waster
and a shirk.
As a working-man he poses, though a trial soon
discloses
He’ll malinger, sham, and —well, in fact, do any-
thing but work.
At snake-juice and chain-lightning you will see
his visage bright’n.ing—
At painkiller or balsam, or eke at Wor’ster
sauce.
Is tobacco in the liquor, he will gulp it all the
quicker;
He has drunk sulphuric acid—enough to kill a
horse !
He will close each altercation with a garnished
allegation—
He has been a “ crimson bruiser ” —he has never
been knocked out.
Then perhaps you drop the question, and put forth
a mild suggestion
(The very thing he’s looking for) that you
desire to “ shout.”
But just you prepare for riot! He will suddenly
grow quiet,
Though he’s boasted of his prowess, of his “true
and trusty right.”
He will “ hope the thing is ended ” —he is not the
least offended —
It is only stupid blowers who are always talking
fight.
And his yarns when “on the tonic ” of his esca-
pades Byronic
With dusky hunted damsels would your decency
appal—
So loathly and disgusting are his tales (if worth
the trusting)
You will wonder why the heavens on such brutes
refuse to fall.
Yet his yarns are circumstantial—date, name and
place substantial—
Imagination lacking, but in detail most minute ;
You will wonder (though a bagman) if the early
western swagman
Was thus wholly lost to decency—a satyr and a
brute ?
JUMBUCK.
"SCHOOL’S IN!”
(Saturday Night.)
When all the week’s been busy at the stack and
the barn,
And you’ve hardly had a moment for a smoke or
a yarn;
And your eyes are weak with staring where the
sun is. always glaring,
And you’ve got a kind of feeling that you don’t
care a darn. |
Then it’s come along with me to the “room
behind the bar,”
Where the beer and the pipes and the jolly
fellows are,
For a game of “ fifteen-two ”
Just among a chosen few—
And “ School’s in ! ”
When prices take to dropping, and the crop’s
“ cleaning light,”
And the tanks are nearly empty, and the feed’s
turning white,
And the poison-weed’s a-growing, and the
“ norther ” is a-blowing,
And your credit is a-sinking till it sinks out of
sight—
Then it’s come and meet the boys in the little
private room,
Where the beer goes a-begging and the pipes are
all a-fume;
For we’ve got a special tap,
And a little game of “ Nap, ”
And “ School’s in ! ”
When dawn begins to glimmer thro’ the cracks in
the door,
And there’s something seems the matter with
your feet, or the floor,
And the lamps are looking foggy, and you’re just
a little groggy,
And the beer is nearly finished and there isn’t any
more,
Then it’s come and saddle-up, for the sparrow’s
on the wing,
And the magpie’s commencing to warble and
to sing,
And the “ double-header ”’s out,
And it’s no one’s turn to shout,
And “School’s out! ”
NIL.
“LET US LIQUOR!”
As you trudge the weary track
In the torrid land Out Back
There’s a 'greeting sets your pulse a-beating
quicker,
When —the shanty drawing near—
Friendly fist you feel, and hear
Friendly voice with—“ Blow me! You Jim?
Let us liquor ! ”
When the rafters brown have rung
With the festive songs you’ve sung,'
And the tuneful beer begins to brawl and bicker,
Like a messenger of Peace,
Bidding strife and tumult cease.
Comes the soothing call : “ Again, boys—let us
liquor! ”
When you feel you've had your day,
And your beard is growing gray,
When your vital flame begins to feebly flicker,
There’s a new grip in your hand,
And you feel your chest expand
When an anoient comrade hails you—“ Let us
liquor! ”
Water ? Drink it I! Well, I’m blest!
Why, the water in the West
(Though the proverb contradicts) than blood is
thicker;
And, despite teetotal theme,
There’s damnation in the stream,
And salvation in the scheme of “ Let us liquor ! ”
When your week o’ work is o’er,
And devotion’s to the fore,
There’s a pub. for church —and cheery Bung for
▼icar ;
And the text on each occasion
Is that touching exhortation,
“Dearly b’lovfcd mates and brethren—Let us
liquor! ”

SATURDAY, MARCH 19, 1898.
Deadlocks.
The Federal Convention, when it drafted its
scheme for the prevention of deadlocks
between the two Houses of the Common-
wealth Parliament, apparently did so in a
curious frame of mind. To the common
order of intelligence a deadlock is an un-
desirable thing. When there are two Houses
of the Legislature, and they are elected on
different suffrages, or represent different in-
terests, occasions must arise on which they
disagree absolutely. And when one House
demands one thing, and the other demands
quite a different thing, the natural result is
that, until a settlement is arrived at, neither
House gets anything, nor does the country.
The only results are exasperation and the
stoppage of public business. Under these
circumstances the most natural idea seems
to be to get rid of the situation with all
possible speed, and if a remedy can be
devised it should be made as rapid and easy
of application as possible. Its object should
be to bring the political deadlock to an
end in thirty minutes if possible, and if it
can be done in twenty-five minutes so much
the better. But the Convention apparently
regarded the trouble as a sacred feature of
the Constitution —something to be carefully
nursed, and preserved in spirits, and trodden
on very gently and apologetically. The
general attitude of members was that of men
who stepped lightly for fear of waking the
animal. And this attitude seems utterly
illogical. $
It is an obvious fact that the Common-
wealth Parliament will be more than usually
liable to deadlocks. The two Houses of the
provincial Legislatures at least represent, in
some sort of fashion, the same province. The
two Houses of the Federal Legislature
will, until population becomes more evenly
distributed, to a great extent represent
different provinces. The Lower House
will be elected according to population,
therefore New South Wales and Victoria
will command a large majority of the votes,
and, if it ever becomes a question of the
large provinces against the small ones, that
House will represent New South Wales and
Victoria. In the Upper House each State
will have equal representation, therefore
Tasmania, South Australia, and Westralia
can run the show there if it is ever
worth their while to combine for any pur-
pose. Neither of these combinations is
likely to be of frequent occurrence —still
they will arise, and when they do the
situation becomes so complex that some
remedy must be provided. If the trouble
arises through a real divergence of interests
between the large and the small States, there
is nothing to be gained by a dissolution ; the
Lower House will get its orders from the
two large provinces to stand firm, and the
three small States will give similar instruc-
tions to the Senate, and things will be as
they were. The case is one which the
Referendum doesn’t fit in any sense. If the
popular vote is taken according to the
majority of people, then New South Wales
and Victoria will always have power to
settle the matter. That will be the same as
leaving the decision to the Lower House
every time, and the matter may as well be
settled that way. If the majority of States
is to decide, then Tasmania, South Australia,
and Westralia will always have power to
decide the question. That will be the same
as leaving the decision to the Senate every
time, and the matter may as well be settled
that way. If, as proposed by Premier
Turner, a majority of the people and of the
States is required, that will simply leave the
deadlock where it was. The only way any
serious deadlock can arise is through the
majority of the people being on one side and
the majority of States on the other.
* * * •
The short, cheap, and reasonably efficient
way out of the difficulty which The Bulletin
has always recommended is to let the two
Houses sit together and decide the disputed
question by their joint vote. This would
give the States’ majority of one House and
the popular majority of the other an equal
show. It would make neither House sub-
servient to the other, and it would save the
months of delay and the enormous expense
involved in taking a popular vote which,
when it was taken, couldn’t give satisfaction
to anybody. It would supply a reasonably
definite settlement of the worst deadlock in
somewhere between 30 minutes and a week,
and there would be about it all the elements
of an interesting gamble. The Convention,
after wrestling with the problem for many
days, at last came round to this view. But,
at the same time, it parted with its sacred
deadlock reluctantly and with anguish in its
soul. It didn’t like to drop the fine old con-
stitutional difficulty overboard in a rude or
overbearing fashion, so it let the thing down
easily as follows :
If the House of Representatives passes any proposed
law, and the Senate rejects the same or fails to pass it, or
passes it with amendments to which the House of Repre-
sentatives will not agree, the Governor-General may dis-
solve the Senate and the House of Representatives simul-
taneously, but such dissolution shall not take place within
six months before the date of the expiry of the House of
Representatives by effluxion of time, and if after such
dissolution the proposed law fails to pass with or without
amendments the Governor-General ma3 r convene a joint
sitting of the members of the Senate and of the House of
Representatives, and the members present at such joint
sitting may deliberate and shall vote together upon the
proposed law, and if it is affirmed by a majority of three-
fifths of the members present and voting thereon, it
shall be taken to have been duly passed by the Senate
and the House of Representatives, and shall be pre-
sented to the Governor-General for the Queen’s assent.
* * * *
The yearnful desire of the Convention to
keep its deadlock on hand as long as possible
stands out conspicuously in these provisions.
In the first place, the measure is to pass the
House of Representatives and be rejected
by the Senate in two successive sessions,
which means a delay of a year, at all
events. Then there is to be another dreary
interval of three months or more before the
dissolution, and the election will occupy at
least another three months, and after that
the Legislature must meet and go through
the Bill again. And if all this trouble—
spreading over, probably, 2-| years of worry,
curse, and exasperation—produces no good
result, the remedy at last gets in motion.
But it only gets into motion in a feeble and
ineffective fashion after all, for even then, in
a joint House with, say, 90 members present,
53 members may vote to no purpose that the
Bill be passed, while 37 votes on the other
side can prevent it passing and leave things
as they were in the beginning.
* * * *
Another curious feature is that this proviso
only applies when the Lower House desires
to pass a measure and the Senate blocks the
way. If the Senate desires to pass a measure
and the other House obstructs, there is no
road out of that deadlock whatsoever. And
the Senate, if it can’t get its measure passed,
and it has no court of appeal to go to, is
quite as likely to become exasperated, and to
try to find satisfaction by blocking all legis-
lation, as the other House would be. One
great reason why there should be some court
of appeal in the shape of a joint House is
that it provides a species of safety-valve
The joint House is, after a fashion, a court
of conciliation ; it gives the defeated party
at least a fair hearing, and a chance to win
its case ; but, if there is a court to which
one party can appeal while the other can’t,
the situation is wholly one-sided.
* * * *
The scheme is evidently based on the idea
that the House of Representatives should be
the predominant partner, and as it will mus-
ter 60 votes at a joint sitting against the 30
of the Senate, its predominance is sufficiently-
secured. Then the demand for a three-
fourths majority is evidently inserted to keep
the predominant' House from being too pre-
dominant. The scheme is also based on the
idea that the House of Representatives is
likely to be the more liberal Chamber, which
it possibly will be—sometimes. As the more
liberal Chamber, it is the only one which can
demand the joint sitting. Then it is provided
that, at the joint sitting, it will require 54
voces to do anything, while 36 will be suffi-
cient to prevent anything being done, which
is giving stagnation a ridiculously long start
over progress. The presumably liberal House
is the only one that can ask for a joint sitting,
but, lest it should get swelled head, this pro-
viso will prevent it doing too much when it
gets what it has been asking for. As for the
Senate, it is only to have a hearing in the
joint House when it wants to prevent some-
thing being done ; if it wants to do some-
thing—possibly something good—and the
other House, being dominated at the
time by a Tory majority, blocks the way,
the deadlock simply remains. The pro-
ceedings then become a dog-fight with
only one dog allowed to be present.
If the proposal is defeated at the joint sit-
ting, it presumably doesn’t get another
chance for years more, or thereabouts.
Altogether, the situation is a remarkable
case of doing a good thing as badly as
possible. It is so adjusted that ' the
idea of not doing anything will always get
an enormous start over the idea of doing
something, and there is nothing especially
beautiful about the idea of not doing any-
thing which seems to call for this concession
Tattersall and His Critics.
i lie lucky drawer of Loch Leifrh in ‘ Tattersall’s ’
sweep had to lay the owner £l5OO to nothing out of the
£4500 net.”
Adelaide, 9th Feb., 98.
Editor Rulletin.— Sir,—The above is a clipping out of
the S.A. Reoister of the Bth Feb., which, I think,
deserves censure by your paper. We have time after
time heard of fortunate drawers of horses in the big
sweeps conducted by “Tattersall” Adams being required
by owners, under threat of scratching their horses, to lay
them several hundreds to nothing ; and in the case of
Loch Leigh it seems nothing less than £l5OO is the
amount demanded by the owner out of a possible t'4500
priz-. Where do the investors in these sweeps come in ?
Their chances of striking a winner are, say, 100,000 to 1.
Yet, if a speculitor be lucky enough to hit it, he has to
give the owner of the horse £l5OO for nothing. We have
been told by Adams’s people time after time that drawers
of horses are not divulged. But this is all “rot.” No
sooner does a man get a wire that he has drawn a horse
than, next thing, word comes from the owner —“ What
are you going to lay me?” The sooner the various
Governments introduce a State lottery, and do away with
all these absurd modes of gambling, the better.
A Subscriber to Tattersall’s Sweep.
• * * »
The above is a letter out of many
in similar strain which The Bulletin re-
ceives concerning a much-discussed griev-
ance against “ Tattersall.” Before going
into the subject it is necessary to state the
conditions under which “Tattersall” is allowed
by the Tasmanian Govt, to conduct his sweeps in
Hobart. The Tas. Govt, could not license a
sweep-promoter without clashing with English
law, but it. reached the desideratum by granting
him a “permit.” And the understanding between
“ Tattersall ” and the Tas. Govt, provides that the
Govt, shall audit his books once a month or
oftener, and that every sweep shall be drawn
under the supervision of a Govt, official. The
sweeps are drawn publicly, too ; there is no re-
striction to subscribers aud champagne, as in the
old days in Sydney ; nor are the police now ad-
mitted sub-rosa. The Tas. Govt, holds “Tatter-
sail’s” deposit of .£lO,OOO to be forfeited should he
be discovered knowingly violating the terms of
his “ permit.” It is hardly necessary to say that
the Tas. Govt., while granting “Tattersall” his
“ permit,” was bound to safeguard the public as
much as possible.
* • * *
Three well-known facts : (1) Horse-owners get
to know the names and addresses of horse-
drawers in the sweeps ; (2) “ Tattersall” employs
an agent—a bookmaker—to hedge with a horse-
drawer’s sweep-money, should the horse-drawer
desire it; (3) “ Tattersall’s ” agent lays horse-
owners so much to nothing to win ; (3a) horse-
owners always desire horse-drawers to authorise
“Tattersall-” to lay the horse-owner this so-mucli
to nothing. These facts constitute another:
“ Tattersall ” is a go-between betwixt horse-drawer
and horse-owner ! And the public is crying out
because horse-owners are constantly wiring drawers
“ How much will you lay me ? ” The wonder is
that a public educated in mining gambles and
so on has not “ kicked ” more. It would have
done so, were it logical ; but the gambling
spirit is always illogical. Ask anybody who
knows the alphabet of horsey ways and betting-
business. If these statements do not appear
very relevant to the above enumerated facts,
the following is obvious at all issues : That
the arrangements, methods and circumstances
of “ Tattersall’s ” business are such that they
afford him every facility for acting in collu-
sion with the horse-owner, did he desire it. Every
facility for safe, secret manipulation ! In the sense
that a grateful horse-owner would never betray a
man who gives him information which secures him
from <£lsoo to 0 up to as much as ,£lO,OOO to 0 on
a Melb. Cup. Horse-owners “ blab ” ! No, they
would be all ready to shut their mouths and to
halve the spoil. Why not? Horse-owners run
their horses for cash, do they not? And aren’t
the horse-owners and the horse-sweep promoter in
the same boat for business? They all subsist on
the public. The horse-race prize-money and the
horse sweep-money come from the public purse.
* * • •
Everybody knows that a horse-owner will pay
for name and address of the drawer of his horse
in a sweep. And the explanation which “ Tatter-
sall ” could give to a victimised horse-drawer
would be : “ Oh ! you have been telling your luck,
or else some telegraph official has ; it has reached
the ' ars of somebody on the make, and this some-
body has wired horse-owner that he knows who
has drawn the owner's horse, and sking owner
what the information is worth. The owner has
wired back to informer, and the pair have come to
terms.” Any clerk of ours who dared to give in-
formation outside would be at once dismissed.
* * • »
Why does not “ Tattersall ” give up the power
to act unfairly? He could do so by drawing
his sweeps the moment after the race was run;
the sweeps are drawn publicly now and under
Government supervision, and any member of the
public can participate in the act/jf drawing. Look
at “ Taitersall’s ” present practice. He draws a
sweep either when full, or if not full, then pro
rata two or three days before the race. Then at
once he wires to each horse-drawer his horse’s
name. Thus to No. 25 ticket-holder: “ Ticket
No. 25 has drawn Martini-Henry in 100,000
sweep,” and the telegram is addressed, for in-
stance, to “John Smith, Milparinka Station,
Queensland.” Therefore, when a horse-owner
gets to know who has his horse in any sweep, the
information must come directly or indirectly—
(l) From “ Tattersall’s ” own office; (2) From
Hobart telegraph-otfice; (3) From the Queensland
or other telegraph-otfice; (4) From the horse-
drawer himself.
* * * *
Clearly, ‘ Tattersall ” has the power, opportu-
nities and means to sacrifice horse-draw'er to horse-
owner should he so desire. And why, in fairness ,
to his own good reputation, does he continue
to possess these ? Does a man, sitting down to
gamble, carry with him a marked pack of cards
in order to ensure honesty all round ? If a man is
found in the cellar of a house, with barrels of gun-
powder and a lighted fuse, is he given the
benefit of the doubt concerning his intentions ? If
a man is found in a bank, with a burglar’s kit in
his possession, is he to be set free of suspicion be-
cause, so far as is known, he has done nothing
improper, and because he assures everybody:
“ Gentlemen, lam an honest man ” ? Isn’t “ Tat-
tersall’s ” position that of a man who, while pre-
sumably honest, constantly has the means of dis-
honesty—which, with a little trouble, he could
absolutely discard, much to his own ultimate
benefit—in his power? A sweep is a gamble, and
the first principle of gambling is to trust nobody.
“Oh! but,” says someone, “‘Tattersall’ would
never do anything to risk his reputation ; that is
his fortune ; besides, he would be naturally satis-
fied with his big commission of 10 per cent.” In-
deed ! is it not clear that “ Tattersall ” is not
only risking his reputation, but irretrievably in-
juring it by allowing his methods of business to
be the cause of this horse-owner and horse-drawer
scandal ? And, concerning his 10 per cent, being
a guarantee of good faith, wouldn’t 90 per cent,
be a still safer guarantee ? The 10 per cent, is no
guarantee of honesty ; it is merely a guarantee of
what “Tattersall” considers the most the public
will stand. And if a sweep-promoter were avari-
cious, couldn’t hetakeadvantage of “ Tattersall’s”
present business methods so as to get a considera-
tion from the horse-owner in addition to his 10 per
cent, commission ? And “ Tattersall ” is avari-
cious to the considerable degree that his 10 per
cent, is a thumping fat commission on a great cash
business.
* * * *
“ Bosh ! ” interjects somebody. “ ‘ Tattersall ’
runs his sweeps for the public ; not for the horse-
owners.” Well, now ; an ordinary, unsophisti-
cated man would have thought he ran them for
neither—that he ran them for “Tattersall,’’and him
only ; for his 10 per cent, commission. It would
be impossible to conceive a more invidious posi-
tion than that in which “Tattersall” is placed
by his method of conducting his sweeps. Every-
body knows that when an average man draws a
horse in a sweep, the fact immediately bursts
him. He proclaims it in his home, in his office,
in the street, in the restaurant, in the bar, on the
house-top. In this big fact, and in the fact that at
least two telegraph offices possessthe sameinforma-
tion, would lie “ Tattersall’s ” anchor of safety,
should he desire to treat, without discovery, with
the horse-owner. For how could the divulging of
information be driven home solely to “ Tatter-
sall?” Who is to discover who first communi-
cated with horse-owner? Could not “Tattersall”
look at the subject in this way: “If a horse-
drawer. by laying owner <£sooo to 0 on a Melb.
Cup, netts thereby .£20,000, is he likely to com-
plain on getting such a fortune for £1 ? And, at
the same time, in laying this <£sooo to 0 he loses
nothing if the horse loses ; and, consequently, hav-
ing nothing to complain of, is he likely to com-
plain ? He’s had a big run for .£1 ! Therefore,
couldn’t I safely deal with the horse-owner?”
This is all hypothesis, of course. It is
for the public to say whether it is reason-
able or not. But, whether or no, this is
certain, that “ Tattersall’s ” known acts, irre-
spective of any hypothesis, are responsible for the
complaints that are now constant. What objec-
tions can there be to drawing each sweep after the
running of its race ? This plan would totally re-
move the grievance.
* * * *
This objection, however, has been urged : Sup-
posing there were five sweeps on one particular
race ; and supposing they had all to be drawn on
one day ; then “ Tattersall ” tvould lose business
in this way : As the sweeps are drawn now,
cash winners in No. 1 will often invest all
—or part of—their win in No. 2, and so on,
to No. 5. \yhereas, if the five sweeps were
all drawn on one day these cash wins could
not be invested in the way set forth. Cer-
tainly not ! But they could be invested in a
sweep on some other event; and, therefore,
“ Tattersall ” would not lose. Take “Tattersall’s’
present advts. as basis. Eight sweeps are adver-
tised—The Silver City Cup, 25,000 at 55., on
March 23; the Hawkesbury Handicap, of 25,000
at 55., on April 2; the Doncaster Handicap, of
25,000 at 55., on April 9 ; the Sydney Cup, of
25,000 at 55., on April 11; the Adelaide Cup, of
100,000 at 55., on May 11; the Birthday Cup, of
50,000 at 55., on May 24 ; the Brighton Birthday
Cup, of 10,000 at 55., on May 24; the Ellesmere
Stakes, of 25,000 at 55., on June 4. Eight
sweeps in two month ! —an average of one a
week ! But the excuse is that the Doncaster Han-
dicap of 25,000 at 55., might fill up so quickly
that a second Doncaster Handicap of 25,000 at ss.
has to be started, and when No. 1 is drawn,
some of the cash winners invest part of their
wins in No. 2. Yes, but supposing No. 1 and
No. 2 Doneasters were both drawn on the day of
the race, April 9, couldn’t the cash winners in No.
1 Doncaster invest their w r ins in Sydney Cup of
April 11 Only two days to wait! Eight sweeps
in two months ! How is “ Tattersall ” to lose
business by cash winners wishing to re-invest their
wins ?
* * • *
To sum up. Let the public for once be con-
sidered. Let “Tattersall” abolish a system by
which he is made the cause of grave and utterly
unnecessary imputations on his own honesty. Let
him fulfil the promises conveyed in his circulars
that is, let horse-drawers get their prizes less 10
per cent., not less 10 per cent, and less sonie person
or persons’ “ big fat cut. ” Let the hedging agent
be abolished- Let the sweeps be drawn after the
race is run. In a word, as we must gamble, let
us gamble under the fairest possible conditions.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
Rotten Ships and Drowned Sailors.
Extract from an article on “ Rotten Ships and
Drowned Sailors ” in Sydney Telegraph :
In the port of Sydney, regulations for the safety of
ships and sea-going passengers of a similar nature to those
of the English Board of Trade exist. This means that
unless a vessel carries passengers it is subject to prac-
tically no supervision whatever. Under the Navigation
Act the Marine Board is invested with certain powers
which do not extend to the compulsory examination of
vessels not carrying passengers. The arrangements made
by the board for the supervision of passenger vessels are
satisfactory, and, if they are faithfully carried out, should
assure passengers an immunity from risk in the direc-
tion of unseaworthiness. But the power to examine
ships which carry no human beings but their
crews only exists in cases where the crews make
a complaint that a vessel is not safe. Then the
Marine Board steps in and makes an investigation.
If the complaint of the crew is borne out, the vessel
must be made seaworthy before it is allowed to proceed
to sea_. In the case of a crew shipped on a vessel in Syd-
ney, it is, of course, impossible for them to he aware
whether the craft is seaworthy or not, and in the absence
of any regulations dealing with this side of the shipping,
they have, perforce, to go without any guarantee. And
the provision giving the Marine Board power to act on a
complaint from the crew is, in an instance of that nature,
absolutely inoperative. Generally speaking, there is no
supervision of vessels which don’t carry passengers, and
it is an open secret that while a great proportion of them
are undoubtedly thoroughly sound ships, a number are
utterly unseaworthy.
No wonder the coal-ship which never returned
is one of the institutions of N.S. W. Newcastle
coal-ships have a reputation for ill-luck ; there
is a big graveyard of them somewhere between
here and the American coast. Also, there is an
awful suddenness about them ; in no trade on
earth that The Bulletin knows of do the
whole crew and every rag and splinter of the
vessel go down together so unanimously.
Either the average lost coal-ship carries no
boats that will float, or it doubles up so sud-
denly that there is no time to get the boats
out, or that ocean graveyard lies very far
away from any place, and the crew generally
pegs out and the boats themselves fall to pieces
before land is reached. The present law is a
very beautiful arrangement. No matter how
unspeakable the coffin-ship may be it won’t in-
terfere unless the crew complains. And the
crew which complains thereby puts the captain
in a particularly good humor, and as it has to
go to sea with him afterwards, and be almost
entirely at his mercy for months, it is often
much wiser to take the risk of the aged hulk
living through one more voyage.
The Need of a Crown Defender.
There is a certain amount of consolation in the
fact that the Victorian authorities have “ called
for a report ” re the conduct of the police in
suppressing certain evidence in order to obtain
the conviction of George Easton (it appears
to be frankly acknowledged by the Crown
Prosecutor that that was why the evi-
dence was suppressed). The circumstances
of the case might justify a call for some-
thing a great deal stronger than a report;
still, the fact that the Crown has moved in the
matter at all is something. There are a
number of curious features about Easton’s
case. Some time about June last a man
committed acts of indecency in the presence
of five girls, who supplied a description
of him to the police. Five months later,
Easton was arrested. In several important
respects he didn’t agree with the description-
still the latter might have been incorrect.
Three of the five girls held that Easton was
the offender; the other two said that he was
not. There is no such thing as a Crown
Defender in Victoria, though there are
stacks of Crown Prosecutor, and so things went
the usual way. The police produced the three
girls who were prepared to swear that Easton
was the offender, and they didn’t produce the
two who were prepared to swear that he wasn’t.
Easton, being locked up, couldn’t hunt for the
latter himself; he had no money with which
to hire private detectives and other expensive
functionaries to do it for him ; possibly he
didn’t know of the girls’ existence. They
didn’t come forward voluntarily—and the
accused got 12 months. He appealed, and
then the suppressed evidence somehow came to
light. Another thing which came to light was
the fact that the Crown law authorities, like
the police, apparently see no special harm in
getting convictions by suppressing evidence
favorable to the accused, as witness this col-
loquy
3Judge Gatjnt : “Why was this girl not called in the
lower court ? ”
Mr. Walsh, Q.O. (who appeared in support of the con-
viction) : “ Because she was unable to identify the
accused.”
That was all. There was no attempt at
apology. Walsh, Q.C., didn’t rise up and say,
with noble indignation, that he would have no
further hand in supporting a conviction which
was gained by the suppression of evidence in
the accused’s favor. He didn’t even seem
ashamed of the ghastly iniquity ; so far as the
daily paper report goes it would appear that
he explained, as a simple matter of course, that
the Crown had got Easton into prison by care-
fully dropping out of sight the evidence which
might have proved his innocence, or at least
raised a strong doubt as to his guilt, and that
he (Walsh, Q.C.) was there officially to get
Easton kept in prison if possible. And yet
there are people who assert that the present
system is quite satisfactory, and that a State
Defender would be superfluous.
Some Tardy Justice Wanted,
4t E.F.” thus addresses The Bulletin :
Now that the programme of the Federal Convention
is nearly finished and hung up to dry, it is singular
—or perhaps it isn’t—that there is no provision in
it for the protection and preservation of such abori-
gines as are still in their natural state in unoccupied
areas. Those few of the delegates whose ideas are
not limited merely to flippant interruptions and com-
mercial-traveller jokes, might have thought of this—
BARTON'or Dkakin, for example. The protection of the
bush natives under a mutual scheme is possible, should
Federation be achieved, but whilst the continent is divi-
ded into independent provinces it is impossible. N.S.W.
and Victoria have no longer any blacks living in their
primeval state ; and those who wander, loafing about the
country, have been too much degraded by contact with
our glorious civilisation to be redeemable. Each State
pursues its own course in its treatment of the > atives.
After N.S.W., Queensland and her native policy has
borne the odium of the world, until Weshulia' has,
in these last years of the century, completely eclipsed the
Northern province. Not so much by individual cruelty—
for brutalised men can be found everywhere—as by the
open and shameless way in which the Government of that
self-sufficient place winks at and condones crimes against
the blacks. Those “in the know ” smile at the idea of
the surviving Anderson serving the full term of his
sentence, and tell you that he will be out within three
years. In any district where the votes are essential to
the five-family Government, any man can wallop his own
nigger ad lib., as long as he does not actually kill him.
Westralia, with regard to the native question, is at pre-
sent a festering canker of shame on the face of the Aus-
tralian continent.
It is impossible to give even a rough estimate, but,
crudely speaking, there are some 100,000 aborigines
still uncontaminated by intercourse with the whites, and
living by hunting and fishing, as did their forefathers.
These are the blacks who might be saved. Not by send-
ing missionaries amongst them—although missionaries
show no desire to go ;it isn’t picturesque enough—but by
creating a system of reserves, under Federal control, from
which all white men —without any exception but the pro-
perly-appointed Federal officials -should be strictly
excluded. The object should be to let the natives live out
their lives as decent savages, working by their own lights
for their living. The civilisation (God save the mark !)
that touches only to destroy should be kept aloof from
them, as also the missionary and the slave-monger.
There are thousands of square miles throughout the con-
tinent, so far utterly useless for miner and pastoralist,
that could be turned into reserves ; and there are many
men—trained bushmen, who know the habits of the
blacks and understand them—whose services could be
made use of. On the country designated there is per-
manent water and sufficient game, and, without going
into detail, supplies could, if needed, be augmented by
small grants of cattle. The scheme is, of course, on the
face of it, a difficult one to carry into practice, but it is
feasible enough if the proper policy be pursued. It
has worked in other countries, and there is no reason
why it should not work here. We have country
enough and to spare, for as long a time in the
future as the blacks are likely to last, and it could not be
made better use of than in forming a hunting-ground for
the preservation of the last of the aborigines, as a people.
That they would die off in the end there is no doubt, but
they would die as free men, not as degraded loafers,
cursed by all the vices so liberally bestowed by civilized
man. A characteristic example of Australia’s treatment
of the native population, indeed, is found in the fact that
a convention, met to federate their country, should in-
troduce into the constitution no meed of justice to them.
For it is certain that no independent province will allow
any other province to interfere with its nigger-flogging,
while those provinces which have no more niggers to kill
off won’t trouble about the matter. The protection an
care of the natives is plainly a Federal duty and will not
be achieved by religion or so-called civilization ; but by
such isolation as will allow the blacks to follow to the end
their natural way of living.

That Tired Political Feeling.
The Seddon Ministry put forth its utmost
efforts to win the Wellington (M.L.) seat,
recently vacated by Robert Stout, and its
nominee was beaten by 1000 votes. John
Duthie, the man with the iron jaw, walked
over Kirk, though the latter had the Minis-
terial as well as the trades-union influence
behind him. Duthie was a kind of Liberal in
the very long ago ; now he is probably the
bitterest Tory in Maoriland. The result cer-
tainly leaves the numbers just as they were,
and in some respects the Govt, gains by having
Duthib for an opponent instead of Stout.
Stout was also a Tory ; he called himself,
and probably thought himself, an advanced
Democrat, but he voted against almost every-
thing which bore the semblance of democratic
legislation ; and the catchword of his little knot
of followers and cold-tea fanatics was that the
Seddon Government must be utterly rotten
and base when a Democrat like Stout was
always against it. The alleged Liberalism of
the overrated Stout did a vast amount
of damage to the cause of progress, and
Duthie, as a fierce, raging Tory, will be a
change for the better. But the fact remains
that the Government made a tremendous effort
to capture Wellington, and failed very badly.
It was a serious defeat, and helps to confirm
the idea that the Seddon Ministry is slowly
dying of “that tired feeling.” The country
has had eight years of it, and is growing weary
of the big surplus and the steady growth of
national industries, and the increase of popula-
tion and employment, and the all-round im-
provement that is manifest. Everythingbecomes
monotonous in course of time, and the desire
to kick is obviously growing. If a statesman
survives everything else he goes down at last
over the sentiment which is so ably voiced by
the hairy orator on the barrel on the wharf:
“If we let the cove stay in any longer he’ll
think we can’t get on without him.” A
general feeling that the cove has been
allowed to stay in till he thinks the country
can’t get on without him is fatal.
Magistrate Addison’s Justice.
Two weeks ago The Bulletin drew attention
to the following weird sample of N.S.W.
“justice ” :
On Feb. 24 a man and wife named Williams, against
whose character nothing- could be alleged, took lodgings
with one Mart M ‘Don ell, in Palmer-street. They in-
spected a room offered them, approved it, paid a deposit,
went away and returned with their luggage and their
little child. During their absence M'Donell says she
missed 17s. 6Jd. in money, which she alleges she had
placed in a purse under a bed in the room ; and on their
return she gave them in charge for stealing it. Beyond
that of prosecutrix there was no evidence that the money
was ever under the bed ; even prosecutrix couldn’t swear
defendants had stolen it—all she did allege was that the
money had been there before defendants came and was
not there after —“she kept other lodgers, but they were
all out, and she was positive nobody else could have
taken it.” Also, there was apparently no identification of
the money. There was, to say the least, a strong
doubt in the matter; the charge was certainly
not satisfactorily proven ; nothing corroborated prosecu-
trix’s statement; the appearance and evidence of the
defendants left the reporters in court with a strong im-
pression of their innocence ; yet Mr. Addison fined Wil-
liams —a workless, almost penniless man—£2 for himself
and £1 for his wife, which was an asinine distinction in
any case. As the ordinary punishment for such larceny
is gaol, not fine, it is evident that the magistrate was
doubtful, and split the difference against the accused.
But as the Williamses had no money to pay the fine,
Mr. Addison alternatively awarded (as reported in Sydney
Evening News) two weeks’ imprisonment to the man, and
three to the woman—which was reversing his own peculiar
distinction ! The case strikes The Bulletin as a most
shocking illustration of the Botany Bay tendency to make
an accused person prove his innocence, instead of forcing
the prosecution to establish his guilt.
After the above statement appeared The Bulle-
tin was informed in a semi-official fashion that
the woman bad a record, in so far as she had
been convicted for drunkenness. It wasn’t
much of an excuse, inasmuch as drunkenness
and theft are two entirely different things, and,
moreover, the man apparently had no police
record at all ; yet the mere fact that
one of the two accused had been pre-
viously convicted of something or anything
might possibly be held to make the injustice
rather less glaring than it otherwise would
appear. By way of further light, on the sub-
ject, however, The Bulletin is now informed
that the said record was only hunted up
some time after the sentence —apparently it
was dug out after the unpleasant comments
began, as a species of tardy justification. It
wasn’t before the court at the trial, and conse-
quently it didn’t influence the decision ; there-
fore the glaring injustice of Magistrate
Addison’s finding remains just as glar-
ing as it originally was. The situation
may be put in these five propositions :
(1) There was no evidence save the assertion
of one person that any money was stolen or
that the allegedly stolen money ever existed.
Such accusations may be made with no basis ;
if Brown swears that Smith stole a sovereign
from him, one of the possibilities of the case is
that he will get his alleged sov. back out of the
money found on Smith when the latter is
convicted and sent up. (2) The accused
were not seen taking the money, nor was
any identifiable coin found on them, and
there was nothing known against them to
lead Magistrate Addison to believe that
they were people who would steal money.
(3) Therefore two people (the two accused) said
they didn’t take the money, while one person
(the accuser) said she didn’t see them take it.
(4) As a further point in their favor, they came
back to the house where the alleged theft had
been committed. (5) As an after-justification
of a sentence, which was apparently based on
no evidence, the police seem to have received
instructions to find a record for the ac-
cused, if possible. The result is that nothing
seems to be known against one of them.
The other had been several times convicted
of drunkenness. And the fact that one person
was guilty of drunkenness, certainly doesn’t
prove that two persons were guilty of theft.
Therefore, the necessity for a logical explana-
tion of Magistrate Addison’s method of ad-
ministering justice remains just as urgent as
ever.

A Convention Summary.
George Reid, a man of enormous dispropor-
tions.
George Turner is called a well-meaning man
by his enemies. It is the regret of those who wish
him well that he deserves no worse name.
Premier Forrest, the protege of Chance posing
as the man of Destiny.
Alfred Deakin, an orator whom it is always a
pleasure to see.
Lyne, a hot, calculating man, never so rational
as when he’s mad.
Adye Douglas, the statesman who has great
hopes for the past.
Peacock, the promising young politician who
died laughing.
Brunker. —lf fi t for Heaven, then not for earth—
Mere mortals cannot see his worth.
Barton (the Imperialist), so far-seeing that he
generally overlooks Australia.
Tribune Trenwith, the passive voice.
Zeal, represents the “ha \e-beans.”
Nine subordinate Westralian delegates—the stin
tongues that denote a wise head.
E. Dyson.
In all the gas that escaped at the Argus
change-of-editors’ shivoo, everybody somehow
forgot to remember the fact —the most glorious
fact in Argus history—that George Higin-
botham was once editor of the big journal.
George Higinbotham of the lofty brow, the
noblest - looking as he was the noblest-
minded man who ever drove a pen in
that or any other Australian newspaper
office, or represented an Australian con-
stituency in Parliament, or raised his voice
in an Australian hall of justice. George
Higinbotham, the one politician in all Austra-
lian history who was equally distinguished by
his magnificent intellect and the absolute purity
of his public and private life. Higinbotham,
who, even as Chief Justice, remained the same
unswerving, whole-souled Democrat to the end.
But, then, Higinbotham was the man who advo-
cated one-man-one-vote, the abolition of capital
punishment, a land-tax, and many other things
abhorred by the modern Argus.

The Australian Brewers’ Journal, on N.S.
Wales free newspaper postage :
The extent to which many Sydney firms now produce
alleged newspapers, with the sole object of cheaply
advertising their own wares, is a national scandal, and a
gross injustice to bona-fide journals conducted to directly
pay as such. Free newspaper postage in N.S. Wales has
become the curse of the bona-fide newspaper proprietors,
for whose benefit it was partially intended. At least a
dozen firms have, for some time past, been making use of
this dodge. The advts. with which they formerly favored
the genuine journals are now confined’ to their own cir-
culars. Worse still, to pay their printers they send out
canvassers to tout for advts. from other firms', and these
they can afford to publish at far less than can the news-
papers which live on their own merits, instead of being
convenient offshoots of commercial concerns. Here is
a most glaring instance of this kind. A N.S. Wales
firm, acting as agents for people at the other end of the
globe, is presumably entrusted with much money for
advertising. These agents appear to have devoted
it to founding an obscure periodical, with which they
deny any connection, though people smile at the denial.
A dummy proprietor is seemingly engaged at a salary,
the ostensible object of this dodge being to secure, under
false pretences, the advts. of rival traders and agents,
who, of course, are not aware that, in supporting the
publication in question, they are helping to prop up a
firm in the same line as themselves. The A.B. J. cannot
help repeating its former assertion that commercial
morality has reached a low ebb in Sydney, and further
adds that the N.S.W. law with respect to newspaper post-
age sadly needs alteration.
Dear Bulletin.— l note on a recent “ Red
Page ” some remarks about Dr. Schenck’s
alleged discovery for the control of the sex of
unborn infants, and a reference is made en
passant to “ The Evolution of Sex,” by Geddes
and Thomson ; but, if my memory serves me,
the conclusions arrived at by these biologists
are exactly the reverse of those now proclaimed
by the Viennese professor. In experimenting
with bees and frogs it was the female, and not
the male, which demanded superior feeding for
its development. In the case of tadpoles a poor
dietary is said to be productive almost exclu-
sively of a spawn of males, while with a rich
dietary the sex is practically reversed. There
may be little in this, but it is well-known that
in the lower forms of life the male exists exclu-
sively for the purpose of fecundation, and that
when this function is accomplished he imme-
diately perishes. This is true also of the male
blossoms of much of the earth’s flora. Apart
from all this, Schenck’s discovery, if bona-fde,
ia bound to have a remarkable influence
on the future of the world. Herein, again,
we note the wonderful provision and prevision
of Nature : There is never the bane without the
antidote. We have the New Woman, now we
have the boy-creator. Surely, it should not take
more than a generation or two to swamp man’s
hereditary enemy out of existence. The trades
and professions are all being invaded by women.
We can now restore the balance. Man’s wages
need not be longer subjected to ruinous reduc-
tion by the competition of his neighbour’s
daughters—for his neighbour’s daughters will,
be all boys. Then the social evil will also dis-
appear. Released from fear of his living wage
dwindling down to the bare subsistence point
from female competition, man will once more
become a marrying animal, and there need be
no more girls brought into the world than stand
an actuarially-calculated chance of becoming
wives. Evidently Schenk spells Millennium.—
An Old Man.
4.
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley the other day
added the weight of his authority to the argu-
ment which declares that, if Australian mis-
sionaries (and especially Australian female
missionaries) are really desirous of doing good,
they will stay at home and help the heathen of>
their own country. His Honor hinted at the
awful need for missionary ministrations in the
N.S.W. backblocks—a need which few city
people realise. There are N.S.W. communities
where incest is common, where the marital
relations between blood kindred are a
commonplace, and where the ignorance of
the most ordinary moral and sexual obligations
is almost incredible. The Bulletin does not
care to specify ; but the facts are known to every
intelligent traveller away from the towns and
the main roads, and occasionally—in one case
out of a hundred, maybe—they come to the
cognisance of the law courts. It is in the
Australian bush—as well as in the Aus-
tralian city-slums that Australian mission-
aries are needed ; and the female missionary
is particularly needed, because it is the scarcity
of females in the backblocks which causes most
of the trouble —she can be a first-class mis-
sionary without being anything else than a
first-class woman. Of course, there is little
hope of change. It is not religion, not the idea
of 3elf-sacrifice, however she may delude herself,
which impels the female missionary to the dis-
tant fields of China. It is the familiar feminine
need of excitement, of sensation, the desire of
becoming the centre of an admiring crowd
on a farewell platform or a foreign-bound
steamer, which sends our girls to the Chinese
holocaust. And as most of them, however
honest and earnest, are just women—mere
vessels of emotion—they will continue to follow
their own impulses as long as foolish male
friends and relatives find the funds to expat-
riate them—the more’s the pity.
The conviction that Major-poet Neild will
retire from N.S.W. Parliament one of these
days to sit on an official velvet cushion is natural
in a community in which an unmanageable and
aggressive politician so often gets a well-paid
billet. Abbott might have been Premier, but
he was addicted to sneering at both sides, so
both sides agreed to let him be Speaker while
he likes. Though most of his mana is now
spent, if he likes the Agent-Generalship both
sides will let him have that too. Dibbs, an-
other man of restless energy and liable to break
out to the confusion of friends and enemies,
similarly receives a good billet with the assent
of both sides. Indeed, so general is the political
custom of billeting above-mediocrity intract-
ables that B. It. Wise is already spoken of as
having claims on a Supreme Court Judgeship.
The determination of Judge G. B. Simp-
son to keep down, as much as possible, law-
costs in N.S.W. Divorce Court, does not meet
with the approval of barrister Ralston. This
gentleman accordingly took the opportunity,
during a divorce appeal, to inform the Full
Court that, through the Judge’s action, a very
undesirable class of attorneys are appearing for
wives in the Divorce Court. A few days after-
wards Judge Simpson, from the bench of the
Divorce Court, flatly contradicted this state-
ment of Mr. Ralston. After all, it depends on
what is meant by “undesirable.” From one
point of view, attorneys who do not send a
barrister many briefs are no doubt an undesir-
able class. Again, many attorneys—especially
those engaged by women suitors—take their
own cases into Court—they brief no counsel at
all ; in the barristerial mind these, too, may
surely be classed as “undesirable practitioners. ”
It depends on the point of view ; so, after all.
barrister Ralston may have been quite right in
saying that an undesirable class of attorneys
practise in the Divorce Court. The public,
however, will be glad to learn that law-costs
are to be kept down, and that a Supreme Court
Judge has the courage to try and keep them
down ; at the same time, it will be gratified to
learn from the same Judge that the attorneys
who practise before him are reputable mem-
bers of their profession. There is nothing
in Mr; Ralston’s reply that large firms
will not take up wives’ cases, for that
does not leave the wives without legal assist-
ance. That is no more a thing to complain of
than that the services of the most eminent
surgeons in Sydney are not available for every-
body at fees of a few guineas—fees which other
men will gladly accept.
The choice of a site for a Federal city should
now lie between N.S.W. and South Australia.
Westralia is practically out of the running by
reason of its remoteness; and Tasmania is
wholly out of it because no sane Legislature
would plant its capital on an island where a
war and a blockade w'ould cut the Government
off from the country it is supposed to govern.
This leaves N.S.W., Victoria and S.A. in the
competition. The Convention has accepted
Premier Turner’s suggestion that the capital
shall be a piece of territory severed entirely
from the province to which it formerly belonged,
and under Federal control only. Victoria can
hardly spare any territory ; there is so little of
that province now that the excision of a Federal
territory of any size would be a ser'ons matter;
so probably it won’t v\ant the capital very
badly under these new conditions and won’t,
mind withdrawing its claim.
