The new shriek of wrath at Sydney
Lyceum, which goes by the name of the “ Terror
of Paris,” is an affair with a reasonable amount
of glitter and decoration about it, but it hardly
lives up to its name. The Terror’s business-card
bears the name of Johnstone Weir, anarchist,
explosions promptly attended to, and families
carefully dynamited at their own residence. But
this old-established family assassin doesn’t bear
out his reputation. He doesn’t explode once all
through the piece. A great attempt is made to
blow up the residence of the Prefect, but the hour
for the explosion conies and goes, and it is simply
mentioned in the next act that the bomb was made
in Germany and didn’t go off. A new bomb is
constructed—a beautiful, glossy, globular thing
which Old Dad, the most ancient anarchist of
tht-m all, raves over—and Johnstone Weir does
think of blowing up Diver and his soldiers with
it. He thinks better of it, however, and goes
away with the bomb in his hand. The Terror
changes his mind about shoving the traitorous
brother head-first into the furnace so that his
whiskers will blaze up, and decides to blind him
instead ; but before he has got through IS closely
printed foolscap pages of oration about the awful-
ness of the brother, and settled down to business,
the soldiers arrive. It takes the Terror and about
twelve other anarchists to kill Diver in the last
act —it is the only deed of violence they really
manage to commit, and Diver kills the Terror’s
best girl in the scrimmage. The curtain falls as
the girl dies in the moonlight, with her Terror
weeping beside her, and Paris, not blown up or
combusted, sleeping as usual beneath the stars.
The present scribe will make a louder Terror any
day with a white sheet and a dinner-bell and a
pint of beer.
» * • *
The Terror is a very uneven goblin, any-
way. In one act he strikes an attitude and briefly
orders Diver and eight soldiers to “ release that
rnan,”and they drop their prisoner on the spot.
In the next, Diver is chasing the Terror over the
housetops, and he hasn’t sufficient attitude about
him to order his own release even. He conspires
publicly in cafes, and darkly commands Ins fol-
lowers to meet at the glassworks at midnight, but
they do nothing special when they do meet, except
that Old Dad makes an oration about the new
bomb. The Terror states at the beginning that
death is too small a thing for his enemy ; he will
be made to live in torment with the worm, eight
feet long, which dieth not, gnawing at
his vitals. “Worm .... Mr. Brown”
would have looked well in the bill, any-
how. But there is no worm after all, and
the enemy dies an easy death by gunshot. The
“Terror” is interesting in its own wildly irrele-
vant way, and has some rather better comedy
effects than usual, but its only apparent purpose is
to get W alner Gregory married to Miss William-
son, and that gentleman is lamentably stiff and
uninteresting. Also, the impressive wave of
Johnstone Weir’s hand is a very weary gesture.
The other people, however, are mostly good—Har-
court, Diver, Sterling Whyte, Frank Crossley,
Charles Field, and Misses Lilian Wheeler and
Watts-Phillips are good enough to drag even a
very limp and ineffectual Terror through his diffi-
culties. Crossley, Charles Field and Sterling
Whyte have no real connection with the story, but
they area lightsome trio, and the latter, as a bald
comedy dipsomaniac, is especially valuable as an
essay on Der-rink.
** * *
Wilson Barrett’s season at Her Majesty’s
has been a very good financial boom so
far. The “ Manxman ” has undoubtedly taken a
big hold, the lovely Maud Jeffries and her father,
old publican Cregeen, being the principal visible
causes. Publican Cregeen, the way Ambrose
Manning plays him, is sufficient to account for the
boom off his own bat. His pious Manx gin-seller
is a great conception ; and he is also quite new,
and quite unlike any other stage gin-seller of this
paper’s acquaintance. The Bulletin s love for
the meanest old snuffle-buster in modern dramatic
history has increased rather than diminished with
further consideration. The character is full of
possibilities—all of them sour and mean, and low
and sordid—and Ambrose Manning makes the
very best of it, and scores every point. If Philip
Christian were only more impressive, it would
help the play a great deal. Anyone can
understand why Kate abandoned her Pete;
a man who, even in the days of his afflu-
ence, goes around in those awful clothes,
is calculated to make any nicely-dressed woman
quit her home. But when she flies to a sketchy
and dismal lover, whose long-tailed coat is almost
equally saddening, the situation loses its point.
Anyhow, the “ Manxman ” will disappear from
the Sydney stage at the end of this week, so the
matter is perhaps hardly worth discussing. Ore-
geen, with his enraged whiskers and his wicked
old soul looking out after the main chance through
his huge spectacles, is the one character in it that
this paper parts from with deep regret, and with
a hope that it may meet him sourly chopping
wood in the back yard of a better world, where
the choir pays up honestly for its liquor. “ Clau-
dian,” with its deserving earthquake, follows on
Saturday.
* * *
“Tracked Down,” which was staged at
Sydney Opera House on Saturday, is a wild Aus-
tralian wail of letters which is rendered painfully
indefinite by the want of any adequate explanation
as to the contents of the pocket-book. It is a
drama with pallid gum-trees and glaring red
grass in it, and in a general way it hinges on the
fact that the squatter is deeply mortgaged, and
the only way out is to sell his plump and pleasing
daughter to the villain. In melodrama the bad
man’s desire to give an enormous sum for a nice,
fat, white girl, is very marked. The present bad
man is comparatively young, therefore when the
tottering, white-haired tramp comes inand explains
{that he was the villain’s otfice-boy many years
ago, and has got the pocket-book which contains
the whole record of his crimes, things seem a trifle
mixed. Apparently the villain had opened that
office at the early age of 12, and engaged an aged
person of 50 or more as boy. And, like most
miscreants, h.e had evidently written a full
account of his crimes in the pocket-book, and left
it loose in the fowl-house and the boy had got hold
of it. How the boy got it doesn t appear. The
squatter, who is a dreary character at the best, is
afterwards murdered with it in his possession, and
the assassin gets it off his dead body, and then a
grisly hand conies out from behind the curtain
and seizes it. Thereafter, the villain and the
squatter’s daughter and the incriminating pocket-
book make a procession of three over the face of
the earth, and the lady spends most of her time
in a chloroformed state on straw in cellars, and
the bony claw of fate reaches out incessantly from
behind something to get the pocket-book aforemen-
tioned. Misses Alice Senior and Therese Lindahl,
who both deserve better luck, save the drama from
utter contempt, and C. R. Stanford, as a cop of
Irish extraction, assists in the good work
with considerable ability. The name of
the man who plays the part of the incrim-
inating document in the pocket - book has
escaped the writer’s memory. The reason
why the bad character is allowed to escape from
custody and come back with a burst collar to
murder the squatter’s daughter, and the cause of
the red grass, and the villain’s object in carrying
the daughter in a fainting condition through all
the finest scenery in Australasia, and the cause of
everything and the general purpose of anything,
are a problem which is too large for discussion.

“Jack the Giant-Killer,” in a new and
revised form, returns to Sydney on March 22
(Tuesday), at which date he commences a season
at the Tivoli. “Jack" was a very respectable
pantomime, and was received with much approval
on his Xmas outburst at the Royal, but whether
the time is ripe for a revival of him is a matter
concerning which the present writer sits on the
rail of uncertainty, with a devout hope that he
won’t tear his clotlms in getting down.

Sydney Royal Standard finds that combination
still going strong. The present shocker is
described as “ The Brand of Crime,” which is a
good, useful title, and one that would fit any melo-
drama about as well as another.

** ¥ *
Harmston’s great circus, which opens at
Belmore Park on April 2, calculates to stun Sydney
for seven or eight weeks. Sydney can stand a
lot of circus ; it loves horse and things pertaining
to horse, and it takes all manner of smellful
animals to its capacious bosom. Harmston’s show
is understood to be great in respect of horse, and
in every other respect as well, and it comes to
Sydney with a good name, having just practically
devastated Banaualand of its last copper.
#* * *
The impudent Sydney City Council pro-
posal to compensate for a reduced rental of the
Town Hall by levying a special after-charge upon
all specially successful entertainments therein,
means simply that the Council wants to cut in
with success without taking any of the risks of
failure. How enormous those risks mostly are
Manager J. C. Williamson indicates by the fact
that before, for instance, he will see a larthing
of profit on the Wilson-Barrett season, he will
have to rake in the trifling amount of <£22,000 ;
and he further points out that, considering the
high-production-standard which he has steadily
maintained in the teeth of heavy losses, only the
most narrow-minded parochialism could think of
penalising' the energy and ability to which amuse-
ment-seeking Australia owes so much. Unto all
which The Bulletin saith the very biggest kind
of Amen !
** * *
Saturday’s bill at Melbourne Bijou gave a
bright, new turn to the Cartwright co.’s season.
“ A Marriage of Convenience,” adapted by Syd-
ney Grundy from Dumas’ “Un Mariage sous
Louis XV.,” may take credit for being about the
most enjoyable comedy of manners that the Bijou
has known within the last live years. It may be
too wittily satirical and out-of : the-coinmon to strike
the unintelligent majority, but it will make the
judicious laugh. Some rather long waits between
the four acts were the only dull features of this
otherwise festive show on Saturday night. “ A
Marriage of Convenience ” is a Parisian incident
happening at a well-dressed period when the
Nicest husbands and wives were expected
to follow a fashion of easy infidelity,
and anything approaching mutual affection be-
tween those whom the Pope had joined to-
gether was bad form. The argument of the
Dumas - Grundy production is extravagantly
naughty, but the upshot of it all is quite clean
and respectable. Also, the characters arrive at
the moral denouement in such a graceful spirit
that they appear rather admirable “ products of a
sham civilisation ” —as Ouida would term them—
when the cloud of impending adultery rolls by.
The play is as a spicy bridal anecdote told in a
drawing-room, and depending for success upon the
wit of the narrator. Its good points are too
numerous and subtle for summarising in a Bulle-
tin par.
« • • •
Charles Thursby, who gave only a slight
indication of acting ability in his two previous
attempts, turns out to be a lady-killing comedian
on the present occasion. He sports fine clothes
with a fine air, and forms an unexpected com-
bination of Ryley, Kyrle Bellew and Cecil Ward.
Of the three old Australian idols suggested by '
Thursby’s appearance as a dandy bridegroom,
only one—Bellow—could have put a higher polish
on the part. Arthur Styne, as a serio-comic
chevalier, prepared to love his friend’s wife on the
latest Louis XV. principles, reveals a humor
which was also unexpected ; and Charles Cart-
wright plays a scandalised military uncle »ith all
his heartas usual. Cartwright’s morality and aspect
are so far behind the fashion that the chevalier can
only think of him as an irate family portrait. An
Australian debutante, Miss Edith Morley (she is
Mr. Cartwright’s daughter off the stage) intro-
duces her fair young self in the difficult position
of a bride, fresh from a convent, who starts mar-
ried life under a promise of eternal devotion to
her husband’s friend. Very few actresses could
act the part naturally. Miss Morley walks
through it in several gorgeous costumes, and,
being a tall, slim, unassuming damsel, who
might have come fresh from a high-toned
convent for all that the audience can suppose
to the contrary, she is a very satisfactory orna-
ment to the proceedings. If she were not
satisfactory the performance in general would
not be a success. “Dream Faces” followed
“ A Marriage of Convenience” on Saturday in-
stead of preceding it. “ Dream Faces ” is a
gloomsome trifle of the prettily-sentimental order,
and, under present conditions, it would seem a
sad mistake at either end of the programme.. The
day for such stagey “dramatic fancies” is not
now.

Miss Ada Reeve’s wrenched ankle was not long

At the Royal they were still playing
“ Shadows of a Great City ” when these remarks
left Melbourne, but the spectacular tale of woe in
a pawnshop was to retire on Tuesday. It will not
be badly missed. The one redeeming circum-
stance in “Shadows” is the acrobatic convict
who throws a small child in the deep blue sea.
“ Would you drown the little innocent 1 ” asks his
horrified companion. “ Well,” says the sprightly
criminal, “unless the little innocent can swim."
The duty of every melodrama villain is to take
his crimes cheerfully. Ou Wednesday Grattan
Riggs will appear as “Shin Fane,” which is to
run for a week. On Thursday and Friday of next
week the broguey veteran bids some more fare-
well to Melbourne in “The Irish Detective,” and
when he has whacked the table with his shtick for
the last time, the management will stage “ When
London Sleeps.”
** * *
The pantomime at the Opera House is
disappearing on Friday, after which sad occasion
the Rickards establishment will, presumably, take
up its former line of business, and the voice of the
gallery boy will chip into choruses as in the nights
of old. At the Alexandra a sudden increase of
patronage has resulted from “ The Kelly Gang,” a
drama abounding in gory perversions of criminal
truth, and full of low comedy reflections on police
valour.
** * *
The fourth Albani concert at Melh. Town
Hall achieved the great managerial purpose of
turning money away, and the Firm’s wisdom in
importing a great vocal Reputation and running
it for all it is worth seems now to be clearly proved.
Albani, with many royal orders glittering on her
breast, is a personage to see and hear—an object of
public interest—a financial success. The famous
artist will give another concert on the 26th, and
return to Melb. for a short oratorio season in the
Exhibition Building at Faster.
** * *
Nothing better has been seen on the
Melb. stage for a long time than Cartwright’s per-
formance in the third act of “Moths.” Savage
Zouroff’s momentary tenderness to his self-sacri-
ficing wife, when he understands how she was
tricked into the marriage by her heartless mother,
is a splendid piece of work. The artist loses him-
self in the character—voice, face, every move-
ment of the hands tells of his sudden awakening
to the truth of the situation. It is a great
dramatic scene. Cartwright’s power seems to
communicate force to the acting of Miss Beatrice
Lamb at this point, and from then until the fall
of the curtain the thinking portion of the audi-
ence is impressed.
At Paris Grand Opera, on a high recent occasion, when
seats had been sold at a premium by speculators, the chief
tenor could not sing, and the money had to be returned.
Those who paid high premiums only got the admission
money. The speculators were not touched.

ward,” writes a Bulletin correspondent, “ was certain
cottages at Wingfield worth some hundreds of pounds.
After all, the whole estate was under £20,000, and, ‘close’

thing like what he was accredited with. The platonic
devotion of the lady he so appreciated in life has never
been liberally recognised by this stage-hero—all strut and
niggardliness.”

T.D.: “ Here is Albani parading the vocal defects to
which even boastful Nellie Melba can scarcely help look-
ing forward as the inevitable result rk.a quarter century’s
continuous singing. Albani’s fault, nowadays, is in the
groundwork of all vocal art—she breathes like an amateur.
Her chest-muscles have hardened with constant exercise,
and lost flexibility, so that, except in cantabile work, her
singing is effortful. One hears the wheels go round.
Amy Sherwin’s auditors noted how the raspberry night-
ingale, a comparatively lacile singer, threw back her
head to obtain greater laryngeal freedom, and they now
note how Albani has taken to this i ocal artifice even more
markedly.” {But isn’t it Ihe sin of tight corsets that finds
all lady-vocalists out ?—Ed. B.j
Dear Bullkiin.— Your correspondent, “ Viator,” didn’t
drop upon a London notice of Gus Glover’s fl6but as
Colonel Briton in “The Wonder.” lie confuses Augustus
with Bob Glover, a man not unlike his namesake in
appearance. Augustus G. got his first show in London at
the Roy a t.v Theatre, afterwards he played several parts
at the Adelphi, where he figured imposingly as the
wicked Colonel to Emmett's Fritz. Later than that lie
put in three seasons at Drury Lane, but, meanwhile, he
iiad lagoed to Wilson Barren’s Othello in the provinces.
’Gus Glover was a strong lago, and an even stronger
Othello, as many Australians can testify from his per-
formances with Bandmanu out here. His proudest recol-
lection is of an English engagement to alternate the two
leading characters in Shakespeare’s tragedy with the
veteran Creswick. Glover started as lago, and was so
warmly received that Creswick declined to “swap ’’ parts
on the second night.—F.
Paradis: “The co. supporting Mr. Barrett and Miss
Jeffries got fairly gauged in Melbourne. The prize is
given to Mr. Manning. Whatever he did was real good,
from Cregeen, in ‘The Manxman,’ to Elijah Coombe
(although he over-acted in this part) and the Grave-
digger. Next comes Miss McCarthy. As the Queen, in
‘ Hamlet,’ she showed very considerable ability. Carter
Edwards is a useful stage-carpenter; Irwin, a pleasant,
fleshy, and lumbering young fellow. Hodges, excellent as
the caddish cousin, in ‘The Manxman,’ can be put into
Jaikes, Polonius, or low comedy, and vindicates a
place on the stage, without scorching it, or burning the
planks as the French saying is.”
The pupils and friends of Miss Eloise Juno gave a
sociable little show at St. George's Hall, Melbourne,
the other night. Two or three promising ama-
teurs cropped up during the perpetration of a programme
that was too light to be painful. Miss Florence Wilson,
daughter of the Bijou Theatre proprietor, took honors in
the opening item ; Miss Maggie M'Cann shone amidst the
mediocrity, and a Miss Trissie Born proved to be a
charming young vision whose intelligence should soon
get the better of her “colonial twang.” Several other
fair amateurs did justice to their Junoesque training in
Scotch accent.
Maud Jeffries acts best as Kate Cregeen, looks best as
Mona in “ Ben-my-chree,” and feels best as “ Virginia,”
her favorite character.
Marie Elster, at latest, was mixing operatic with con-
cert engagements, and is likely to be heard of in London
this season. Buxom Marie played prima-donna parts
with the J. W. Turner opera co., at Manchester, not long
ago.
Maud Jeffries, in Melbourne, followed the same domes-
tic policy as Ada Ferrar had adopted off the stage, de-
clining all sorts of invitations to star at garden parties,
&e. “If you want to see us book a seat.’’
In the widely-distributed photo, of Wilson Barrett as
the Manxman holding the baby, the baby is the twelve-
months’ old daughter of Geo. Pack and wife (Florence
Esdaile).
“Compton,” to The Bulletin Have heard Albani,
and gone into the bush to think about it. Conclusion :
That it is a treat to hear her, but that it is an intellectual
treat, and not a sensuous one. One is lost in admiration
at the prodigious skill with which the slender remaining
powers of her voice are manipulated. It is like Napoleon
conducting the retreat from Moscow after the loss of the
Grand ArmCe, or Montague Tigg financing the Anglo-
Bengalee on a grand piano and a door-plate, always on
the very edge and limit of their resources, and yet always
just equal to every crisis. Good generalship is always a
magnificent thing. It is magnificent in Albani Magnifi-
cent, but not song. There was no suggestion in her
exhibition of that intoxicating abandon in sensuousness,
of that easy ‘ swinging’ of the voice in the careless con-
sciousness of inexhaustible reserves of power, which is
the thrilling element in vocal work of real magnetic
quality. Give us force, and plenty of it, if you want to
lift us off our feet. Rather a hundred tons in excess than
a couple of ounces short. It is the Darling of theconcert-
platform that touches the emotional chord in us, and not
the Ranji—the hard hitter rather than the exhibitor of
cool dexterity. Flexibility, range, execution, ease, even
physical: perfection (or something sufficiently near it) in
tone, may all be allowed. So, too, may judgment—taste
—consummate discrimination in the attacking of difficul-
ties and the handling of resources. And with it all the
electrifying soul of vocal music is not there. We listen,
we admire—unstintedly. But we remain cold.”
The most successful sacred composition yet written in
the seven provinces is probably Sydney Iloben’s “ Ave
Maria,” of which the Ascherbergs lately issued a new
edition. De Vere Sapio, Belle Cole, and a host of other
professional and amateur warblers sing it ; and it is
to be heard not, only in all the principal R.C. churches
about Sydney, but all over Australia, and in the big
European cathedrals; and it frequently figures on
concert programmes. Hoben, who graduated at Leipsic,
succeeded the late Mrs. Carl Fischer as musical critic
of Sydney Mail, but has nothing to do with the Herald
criticisms, as has been implied by some people. Hoben
is a Sydney native, who has a decided gift of melody,
evidenced in such diverse songs as his “Ave Maria,"
“ Cromaboo,” “Melons,” etc.
Though spuriously-devotional ballads of the mono-
tonous “Holy City” stamp still arrive with every batch
of new music, there seems to be a dead slump in the call
for that class of composition outside the variety ’alls and
suburban small and-earlies. Sydney choir-masters, who
used to put “Star of Bethlehem ’’ and “ Holy City ” on
the bill for alternate Sabbaths, are now quick t-o suppress
anyone who threatens to sing Ihe glories of Jerusalem
the Golden. “Stephen Adams,” boss of the twopenny
“royalty-ring," probably wrote those dreadful items
with his tongue in his cheek—but how did he bamboozle
the Sankeyites! ”

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
The Federal Convention first decided to make
the taking-over of the provincial debts by the Common-
wealth compulsory; then it went back on that decision,
a , n . ( i made it purely optional. The reason assigned for
this change of front was that the Federal credit must
necessarily be better than that of any separate State ; if
the taking-over is compulsory, then the public creditor
gets the benefit of the Federal guarantee gratis, while if
it is made optional the Commonwealth can offer to convert
the State debts into a uniform Federal 3 or 2f or 24 per
cent, stock, and if the holders won’t accept the offer and
convert into lower-priced stock, then they must do with-
out the extra security. This is logical enough, yet it is
yist possible that it would be cheaper and better for the
Commonwealth to take over the debts at once, and make
the creditor a present of any profit he can get out of the
Federal guarantee, and be content to convert the debts
into a low-priced Federal stock as they fall due. Very often
the other process costs more than it is worth. Owing
td the want of foresight manifested by certain beastly
financial forefathers, almost all the Australian loans are
repayable on a fixed date, therefore, if a conversion is
do |le before that date, the bondholder must be paid to let
go. Often he has to be paid an awful lot. The general
course is that he takes less interest on condition that the
principal is increased and the loan made fixed and per-
manent for a longer period.
The trouble is that no one can tell what the in-
terest rate will be a number of years ahead. The only
big conversion yet done in these provinces—that of Maori-
land—was originally based on the idea that interest
would never get below 4 per cent, for 60 years or so. It
added over VI,600,(100 to the principal of its debt—in
other words, it gave the public creditor that amount of
bonus—on condition that he took 4 per cent, stock instead
of the old 5 and 6 per cent securities. It also fixed
£22,000,000 of debt till 1929. There was an im-
mediate saving of interest, certainly, but the trans-
action is a big loss ; if the old 5 and 6 per cent, had been
paid till the loans ran out, a lot of them would be falling
due now, and could be converted into 3 per cent, stock.
As it is, Maoriland loses £1,600,000 principal, and pays 1
per cent, more than it should do for 30 years. When the
darned foolishness of this arrangement at last dawned on
the AI.L. legislators, they decided to do the rest of their
loan conversions at 31 per cent. They lost another
£438,000 in converting five millions of old high-priced
loans into 31 per cent, stock which isn’t repayable till
1940. They were very proud of this deal also at the
moment; now it appears that they paid a lot of money
for the privilege of fixing five millions of debt for 40 years
at l per cent, above the present market rate. In all these
cases it would have been cheaper to pay the old, exor-
bitant rate to the bitter end; the immediate saving- in
interest was bought a great deal too expensively. And it
is quite possible that it would be cheaper for the Federal
Government simply to take over the provincial debts as
mey stand, and convert them into a cheap, uniform
Federal stock when they fall in. The man who holds
N.S. Wales 4 per cent, stock due 1912 would probably
want a decent bonus and an extension to 1930 or so to
induce him to accept 3 per cents, in exchange. And by
the year 1912 interest might be down to 2 per cent.,
which would make the transaction a heavy loss. Again,’
the rate might then be up to 44 per cent., in which case
there would be a large profit. The conversion business
is a gamble, and all that is known for certain is that
hitherto these provinces have found it a rather profitless
gamble.

The City Mutual Life Assurance Society
(Sydney) shows a very distinct improvement for 1897.
The income is larger than it has ever yet been ; the ex-
penses are much lower, and the amount added to the
funds is the largest in any single year so far. The last
five years’ record under these heads is as follows :
Income Expenses. added
to funds.
1893 .. £32,576 £8,939 £10,021
1894 .. 34,018 9,907 13,140
1895 .. 36,849 10,697 14,260
1896 .. 39,290 9,776 13,319
1897 .. 41,229 9,305 19,200
The expenses are still heavy, being 22A per cent, of total
income. Still, th's is a great reduction as compared with
the 25 per cent, paid in.’96, 29 per cent, in ’95, and 291
per cent, in ’94, and if the improvement continues this
society will shortly lose its bad reputation for expen-
sive management.
The City Mutual looks like a steadily-improving
concern in a number of other respects. In addition to
the constant advance of the premium income, the income
from investments (£S3S4) is £953 more than it was last
year. The amount of interest overdue is only £231, which
very small as compared with an annual income of over
£BOOO, anti says a great deal for the security of the invest-
ments. (Curiously enough, the balance-sheet makes no
mention of accrued interest.) The item “ Properties
acquired by foreclosure, £4335,” is also moderate. Within
the last year £lll3 has been written off for depreciation,
and within the last three years £5853 has been applied to
l’ u n :,osc '- This is a large provision for depreciation on
£165,000 worth of assets, and it looks like a vigorous and
consistent effort to keep the assets down to bedrock
values. Also it is announced that no further additions
will be made to that rather dubious asset, “Deferred
Instalments of Annual Premiums,” and that the £7518
standing against it will be gradually written off. Deferred
instalments certainly are an asset of a kind, and some
good offices, such as the National Mutual of Australia, the
Bun of Canada, the Patriotic of England (which isn’t par-
ticularly good) and a few others include them. Still,
there is a certain amount of doubt about them, and the
decision to write off this item will make the City Mutual
all the stronger.
The City Mutual now is certainly very unlike
the shabby little concern of a few years ago. The ac-
counts are clearer, mainly because thev specify the
amount of foreclosed property and the interest arrears—
both valuable items in estimating the solvency of an
insurance concern. The premium income and income
from investments have increased largely. Expenses have
been reduced, provision made for depreciation, the dubious
deferred instalment ” item is disappearing, and the last
vestige of the old management vanished in ’94, when
£lBOl was paid as the cost of the disastrous law-suit with
the late J.P. Garvan, once managing director. The City
Mutual, this year, begins to look like a very respectable
institution with a future before it

The Union Bank of Australia, as the result of
increased expenses (the increase appears to be mostly
income-tax), shows a diminished profit of £37,436 for the
half-year ended 3lst Aug., 1897 ; and, as the 5 per cent,
dividend absorbs £37,500, the balance at credit of profit
and loss is reduced by £64. The Union Bank’s profits
lor the last five years have been as follows :
February,'93 ..£69,609 | August,’9s ..£36,063
August, ’93 .. 60,295 February, ’96 .. 37,996
February,'94 .. 44,132 August,’96 .. 68,837
August,’94 .. 44,676 February,’97 .. 37,578
February, ’95 37,172 August,’97 .. 37,436
Ine big 1 bank has obviously faded a good deal in recent
years, and this is the third occasion since ’93 when it
hasnt earned quite 5 per cent. Considering that it has
over £4,000,000 in cash, it seems remarkable that it doesn't
turn at least a portion of this mass of gold into State
securities, and thereby supplement its income—the invest-
ment of a million at 3 per cent, would add £15,000 per
half-year to the profits, which would be a material increase.
Curiously enough, however, the Union holds £360,000 less
of Govt. securities than it did six months ago, the reduc-
tion being wholly in British consols. It would be inter-
esting to know what profit there was on the sale, and
how much or how little of the half-year’s profits were
derived from this source instead of from the ordinary
banking business.

Sydney Permanent Freehold Land and Building
Co. (Sydney) last year showed a profit of £lB7, equal to
about 3s. 7d. per Cent, per annum on the nominal capi-
tal of £102,755. The co. has set aside £58,000 for
depreciation—it hasn’t taken the amount off capital in
the usual way, but put it on the depressing side
of the profit and loss account, and the year’s
transactions reduce this vast debit to £57,813.
If this amount is taken off capital then the
profit equals about Bs. per cent, per annum, which seems
to suggest that another £40,000 requires to be written off
to bring values down to their real level. The Sydney
P.F.L. and B. Co. looks as if it had very little prospect
ahead of it. It owes £120,000 to depositors and mort-
gagees, and its principal visible assets are £166,000 (paper
values) of more or less unsaleable properties. And if it
hopes to come right at the rate of £lB7 a year it has a
long tramp in view.
* * * *
The decision of N. S. W, Supreme Court that,
under the existing Mines Act all coal must be weighed as
it comes from the mine, is calculated to make the coal
industry sit up violently. The miners are paid by weight,
but, as a rule, the management weighs one skip in ten or
twenty—the smallest one for choice—and averages the
rest. None of the mines have sufficient appliances
to weigh more than a small part of the coal, and
if the law is enforced there is talk of shutting down
till the necessary weighing machinerv is obtained.
Probably, however, the trouble will be settled by the law
turning its blind eye—the same eye that it turns on the
Sunday banana and the tote. That glazed and sightless
optic gets its owner out of a multitude of difficulties.

private usurer—some thrifty usu’ally of low
grade, who makes an “honest” penny lending out his
savings at rates of interest which would turn a Hebrew
money-lender green with horror. At one Melb. skyscraper,
the liftboy confided to the writer that his screw for the
week was all mortgaged to the caretaker. “ He’s very
■ reasonable though,” added the relenting youngster, “he
only charges a bob for the lend of a bob for a week.”

Melbourne money-lenders now take bills of sale
down to as low as 205., and do not hesitate to publish the
rate of interest—6o to 90 per cent., as the case may be.
They laugh at the proposed Usury Bill of the Turner
Government. They will evade that bv selling, say, a
chromo, worth 6d., for £2O, taking a bill of sale over it
for that amount. No money-lending there—only a leetle
commercial transaction. A partial remedy would be a
State or municipal Mont de Pi6t6—which is bound to
come.

* ♦ * *
A Yankee syndicate has erected and is now
running an aerial tramway over the 3500 ft. high Chilkoot
Pass. It is eight miles long and begins eight miles from
the wharf at Dyea. The engine-house is placed at Sheep
Camp at the top of the incline. The track is a five-
eighths steel-wire rope on iron trestles, the longest span
being 1600 ft. It reduces the journey from Tacoma to
Dawson City to 15 days.
*‘* * *
All that is the matter with the Girilambone
copper-mine, N.S W., is a total indebtedness of £19,000.
The working account shows a surplus, and it is explained
that this includes all mining development. But it is not
explained clearly how this huge debt arose. What some
people want to know is—what proportion, if any, of the
company is represented merely by the option to purchase
the property? It looks as though this co. has been
cleverly worked for the good of a certain section of the
shareholders ; if the cleverness had been used for the mine
only, things would be all right. Strange, too, that the
effort to put the co. in a better financial position was not
made when the shares were at a premium, and when the
money could have been raised on much better terms than
those now offered.

Dear Bulletin,—The writer of the par. re the
Illawarra Smelting Works (Dec. 25, 1597) asks why the
works were not located in Sydney ? In the first place,
Sydney people would have risen as one man to protest
against huge smelting works being erected on the shores
of “our beautiful harbor.” Take, as an instance, the
fierce opposition to the locating of the coal-mine works of
the Sydney Harbor Collieries Co. The owners of the
Illawarra Smelting Works knew their business when they
erected their plant in its present situation, and, so far
from it being “ a gigantic mistake,” as the writer of the
par. so fatuousty asserts, the selection was a wise one.
Smelting works established in San Francisco, at a huge
outlay, had to be removed 10 miles further away from the
city on account of the fumes. Does any reasonable man
think that Sydney people could have put up with
big smelting works on the shores of Port Jack-
son ? The co. has a 2s. 9d. rate from Dar-
ling Harbor to the works, where it has cheap
coal, and the latter is the essential factor in success
in general smelting works. It has established works
in N.S. W. to divert the trade from S. Australia, and are
now treating and will treat Broken Hill and W.A. gold
ores in competition with S.A. and Europe. The Illawarra
Harbour works would have now been completed by the
same people, but for the action of the Government in
bringing on the Port Kembla bill. Their £lO,OOO
deposit has been paid to the Government and the harbour
will be made, despite the fact that the co’sdifficulties have
been vastly increased by the avowed intention of Govt, to
construct an opposition harbour.—L. B. London, Feb 4
* * * *
The auditing of municipal accounts in N.S.W.
is no more lax than the auditing of hospital accounts.
Though the Govt, contributes much more than half the
funds, it is not represented on the boards of management,
which mostly elect themselves at annual meetings that
nobody else attends, when they also appoint two of them-
selves to be auditors. Now and then curious things with
regard to the supply of stores, &c., are said ; and only the
other day one of the largest hospitals outside the capital
discovered that an official was over £lOO short on the
year’s accounts—precisely how much it couldn’t say—and,
since then, charges have been made that somebody or
other has been paid by the hospital for things that other
people presented to it. Moreover, there is to be no prose-
cution.

A party of N.Q. miners recently sent away
two parcels of silver ore—one to commission agents in
England, and the other to Aldershot (Q.) The lot sent to
England was much the more valuable- -yet. net returns
per ton from the latter (Aldershot) far exceeded gross
returns from the former. On enquiry it was found that
the commission agents in England were also either agents
or owners of the smelting works benefited, and that they
sold the ore to themselves.
* • * •
In the year 97 there were 31 accidents on the
Towers, of which eight were fatal, 15 serious, and the rest
not serious. The year ’96 gave 55 accidents, of which 11
were fatal and 17 serious cases. Consequently the Towers
plumes itself on a diminishing annual holocaust. But an
average of about two men per month either killed or
seriously injured is a very fair sacrifice to the gods of
Greed and Negligence.

* * * *
Chillagoe, Queensland, is the rage in Melb.
Sydney is asking where Chillagoe is, and what about it ?
Away inland from Cairns, worked for the last few years
for copper, principally by veteran John Moffatt, who has
recently had a blast furnace at work, and has worked
altogether some 30,000 tons of ore. J.M. is one of the
pluckiest and straightest of Australian mining men. In
New England districts, N.S.W., he is known as the father
of local tin-mining. He also opened up the tin-mines of
Herberton, and with their failure directed his attention to
the copper deposits of Chillagoe. Over a year ago some
Broken Hill and Mt. Lyell men sent experts to examine
the property, and on their reports it was purchased.
Moffatt retains a third, and received £20,000 for the
balance; £30,000 was placed to working account. The
area has been enlarged to some 3000 acres ; the Govt, has
granted a 50 years lease free from any labor conditions,
and the co. undertakes to build a railway from Mareeba’
the present terminus from Cairns, to Chillagoe, some 90
miles away. Schlapp has not examined, but will do so
shortly. Dr. Peters, on whose report the same
crowd handled Mt. Lyell, has been communicated
with, and will probably accept the modest fee
of £5OOO for his examination—to be made as soon
as he finishes the work on which he is now engaged
in Mexico. YVhispered in Melbourne last week that
Stewart, manager of Broken Hill, and formerly of Rio
Tinto, the great copper-mine of Spain, has been offered
the biggest salary ever paid in Australia to take control of
Chillagoe, and will give his answer as soon as he has
inspected the mine. Hence, it is hardly to be wondered
that Chillagoe is already the talk of Melb. Shares issued
at £2OO each have sold up to £l5OO. Nothing has occurred
in the way of development to warrant this rapid increase'
in value, which is largely accounted for by the number of
persons whose anxiety to get into Chillagoe is prompted
by remembrances of “ missing Mt. Lyells when they were
only £l.” Time and work have, however, proved the
founders of the copper-era of Queensland to be a good
crowd to follow; but whether they are good enough to
follow at present share prices—well, time and work only
can show.

Ed. Bulletin. —Re your recent par. about
Sheepstation, Gippsland (Vic.): as a disinterested person,
let me say that some of the best shows in Gippsland are
to be found at Sheepstation, as stated by the minin«--
reporter of a local paper who has been much abused by cer-
tain syndicators for being over-truthful, and has been sacked
by the newspaper trust. Richmond’s and the Dorothy are
two properties that will hold their own with anything in
the market. There isn’t anything on the field 'to equal
either. Both Carry big reefs—£4 Is. 6d. gold, plenty of it,
and not at present the slightest indication that they are
going to bottom this side of Sheol.—Scribe.
* • * *
D.W.D. : The Lyell Boomlet is trembling
in the balance prior to drifting into Burst. The general
public has been misled into buying into properties
on account of their proximity to the big mine, and no
voice was vociferous enough to prove to them that, where
isolated and independent deposits, and not lodes, are
being worked, proximity doesn’t amount to anything.
Somehow, the bigger the deposit the greater the concen-
trative ability of the one big mine to rob the formations
surrounding it of their metallic contents. The remarks
of chairman Orotty, of North Lyell co., at the meeting
held in London, shortly after the alleged flotation, are to
hand. Crotty seems to have stated at that meeting that
there were 1,000,000 tons in sight at the moment.
Writer has seen the alleged deposit which was esti-
mated to show 1,000,000 tons, and could see
—well, let him put it mildly—several million tons
of country. The new And, which gave the stock a tem-
porary buck at a critical moment, was accidentally cut in
making a road, and now the holder of every cat on the
Lyell field is repeating the discovery of roast pork—burn-
ing his hut down and cutting tracks to nowhere in the
hope of striking what the Lyell pup crowd call a lode,
and which, in the majority of cases, is a floater, as broad
as it is long, leached from a big body. Now the N. Lyell
crowd say they kneiv of the existence of this accidentally-
struck deposit, and prove their knowledge by bending a
tunnel many degrees out of its original course to strike the
deposit at something like a depth. The fact that they
haven’t struck that deposit with their spiral tunnel is not
mentioned. Crotty’s real trouble seems to be his wounded
feelings. He wanted to be on the board of the big Lyell
mine, and the lordly Lyell directorate said they would re-
sign in a body if that person took a chair with them.
Meanwhile, Crotty talks of several million tons of North
Lyell ore at .£l5 per ton, and is happy.

If the published account of the new Nickum
light isn’t an unusually elaborate Yankee squib, there’s
trouble ahead for those interested in every other illumin- -
ant. A miniature sun (produced by the glowing of mixed
chemicals in a semi-vacuum) that can be carried about in .
the pocket, and can only be extinguished by breaking its
containing glass-globe, would be safe enough to catch on
at almost any price ; but when it is further asserted that
the light can be obtained at a first and only cost of about
lOd. for a 16-candle-power-“ sun ” that will burn for over
40 days and nights—well—um !

C.M.G. : “When the truth is told about a nest
of wild-cats out back, calling itself a mining district, it is
not the miner on the job who gets up and snorts. He re-
cognises the imperishable fact, and is gratified ; it is the
local storekeeper w’ho runs w'ild with the offending journal,
and howds in the hills. The storekeeper is almost invari-
ably a rabid boomster w’hose one idea is to drag people
out to his God-forsaken district to buy his tinned victuals,
and to keep those already there from slipping off in the
darkness with his unreceipted bills. The storekeeper it is
w’ho inspires the ‘ ow T n correspondent ’of the metropolitan
dailies with frantic enthusiasm about every little local
pot-hole, and his is the moving spirit that causes ‘ 4dwt. a
ton to read ‘ 4oz. a ton,’ and induces many similar
‘printer’s errors.’ Knowing these things, I am never
moved to tears when I find the back track from a tea-«-nd-
sugar ‘ rush ’ littered with Treacle’s unpaid bills.”

The most miserable “bike’’dealer of this con-
tinent is a mechanic who once ow’ned a nearly-new bike
and £3O sterling. He had a friend who could talk
any book-canvassser dumb. They entered into partner-
ship—the mechanic finding the bike and the £3O, his
chum putting in as capital his tongue and doing the can-
vassing. The orator persuaded his partner to increase the
stock by another bike costing * 25. The stock w'as sold
out the second day of the partnership, and the man of
tongue w'ent to collect the cash, but never returned, and
his present address may be Klondyke. Sympathy works
a lot, however, and the defrauded mechanic W’as accorded
credit to the extent of tw*o new bikes by a confiding hard-
ware man. He opened his shop next evening, Saturday,
displaying the machines in the shop window. No sale
that night, so he locked up and went to bed, to sleep away
his sorrows—and w’oke next morning to find that his
place had been burgled and his two machines stolen.

The latest pious embezzler seems to have got
away with about £3OOO, and the “ explanations ” of the
officials of his society are as lame as usual. Some time
ago the now’-missing local preacher was accidentally
bowled out, but his many pious promises of reform led to
forgiveness, and he then won! on the same game again.
Faked balance-sheets, it is now’discovered, have been pub-
lished for years. A Govt, auditor is certainly required for
all societies, but the society with an aggressive local
preacher as sec. seems to require two cold-blooded atheists
for the job.

Worth noting by insurance co.’s is the recent
experience of a Sydney man who happened to be awake
when a rat ignited with its teeth a match which set light
to a coverlet, and might, in other circumstances, have
caused the destruction of the house. People are possibly
too ready to cry “Rats!” to the rat-and-match explana-
tion of mysterious conflagrations—especially considering
the rodent s curious fondness for phosphrous in anv
shape. J
• * * •
Mine-nationalisers, and especially coal-mine
nationalises, had a chance to point a moral with the
almost-violent dissensions which occurred in the Federal
Convention on the question of permitting States to grant
bonuses or other aids to coal-mining. For the most
serious difficulty which the infant industry in Victoria
has to face is the unfairness of the competition which an
old-established firm or company elsewhere with plenty
of accumulated resources can afford to temporarily carry
on in order to knock out the potential rival. What the
nationalises would, of course, argue is that the State
would not be guilty of this unfair competition—this wage-
gnndmg and selling under cost price, &c. But that is not
certain, though ; for, notwithstanding that the railways
are in the hands of the State, cut-throat competition is
not unknown even there. At the same time, reasonable
regulation of trade ought to be and, in fact, is, more feasible
where the competition or possible competition is confined
to two or three States than where half a hundred
private hustlers, amongst whom half-a-dozen are bound
to be cantankerous, are in the race.

More than one big importer of grass-seeds down
South, who, two months ago, looked dolefully upon the
prospect of quitting stocks at a profit, bids fair to make a
pile ; and scores of settlers, who did not know which wav
to turn for money to clear their selections (within the
terms of the Act), are thanking God for the bush-fires.
In fact, the fires in Melbourne and country are bv no
means an unmixed evil “ A godsend for which I have
sat a-praying on my fence for the last four years,” is what
a Cockney selector told the writer last week.

N.S.W. Registrar-General Long states
definitely that all Registrars have specific instruc-
tions not to perform any marriage without signed
declaration under the Act that the parties have con-
scientious objection to the services of a minister of
religion. N.S.W. Registrars’ marriages, according
to Mr. Long, didn’t last year number more than
100 out of a total 8810—“ or, at the outside, 200.”
As who should say, “ 1 in 88 ; or perhaps 1 in 44
or something ! ” A little thing like that doesn’t
matter.
* * * *
The state of things at Norfolk Island, ac-
cording to a letter received in Sydney from a
native, is “simply awful.” A petty ex-official
accuses an important official of attempted rape;
Chief Magistrate has dismissed constable for false
imprisonment; Registrar accuses an ex-official of
forcing a Govt. House servant to commit forgery ;
various libel-actions threatened ; the Registrar at
loggerheads with the Council about payment of
fees; schoolmaster complaining about arrears of
pay ; many persons refusing to do public work ;
contractors appealing to the Executive against
Chief Magistrate’s actions ; whole community is
in a state of confusion worse confounded. The
Eden from which the Drink Fiend is carefully ex-
cluded seems to be able to get up a pretty fair
hell “ on its own.”
• • • *
Dr. Kinmonfc is the latest Adelaide Hos-
pital doctor to resign. His action was extremely
disappointing, as no official had resigned for nearly
a fortnight, and it was confidently anticipated
that a new local record would be put up. This
latest resignation arises, like previous ones, from
petty personal differences.

* * *
A woman was lately charged with bigamy
at a Melb. suburban court, the accuser being her
second husband. It was admitted that there had
been a first husband, and his death wasn’t proved.
But, at the same time, nobody could prove that
he was alive, or had been alive when the second
marriage took place ; no one knew where he was,
or where he had been for a long, long time, and
the case fell to pieces for the sufficient reason
that there was no case at all. Then the small,
dull Solomon on the bench said he would give the
woman the benefit of the doubt, and discharged her.
The benefit of what doubt ?
“ Lunchtime ” to The Bulletin :
The free Federal feeds in connection with the Conven-
tion session at Melb. have at least served one useful ob-
ject, and that is to show the depths of degradation to
which many individuals will descend to escape the pay-
ment of a shilling at a restaurant. By some extraordinary
means, the dinners at Melb. Parliament. House are thrown
open free of charge, not only to Convention delegates ane
pressmen actively engaged, but to the members of thd
ocal Legislatures and their friends. The result is a re
volting spectacle. M’sP. in dozens hang about the build-
ing, each with a group of from five to ten more or less
grimy satellites, the worst offenders of all being a handful of
Labor-members who turn up at every meal with the regu-
larity of the solar system. Each of the people’s tribunes
brings half-a-dozen deadbeats and vagrants with him,
and obviously the object of one or two is to see that the
whole of their constituency swills fizz at Australia’s
expense before the Convention rises. The question that
naturally arises is why such a wide invitation has been
extended, and by whom ; and, secondly, why champagne
and truffles and such delicacies should be provided at all,
even if the crowd that stuffs its victuals down its throat
with its fingers were excluded ? The Convention dinners
at Melbourne cost over £l5O a day, and fully £lOO of that
sum is spent on voters who make strange noises and
make the rest of the co. feel qualmish.

In Adelaide not long ago a riotous swell
embezzler shook society off its moral base by sub-
poenaing one of his employers to prove that they
| laf } visited bagnios together, and helped each
other to pursue the giddy Tottie round the house
with wine and wassail, and that he had shouted for
one of thebossesoutofthestolenloot, with the boss’s
full knowledge and hiccup. Also, he summoned
some eminent and respectable citizens with a view
to proving that they had pursued the lady of easy
virtue together, and that they and he and the
boss and the girl with her hair down her back had
habitually blown the froth off the flowing bowl in
company. He didn't manage to prove nearly
all that lie wanted to, yet be bad shaken things
up rather considerably before the law led him
away to do 18 months.
* * * *
The temperance party carries things with
a high hand in Adelaide. A publican in that
sanctimonious city was recently fined for merely
having his side-door open on a Sunday. This,
according to the Act of ’96, is prima facie evidence
of Sunday trading. The back-door must also be
kept shut. Hotelkeepers who have families or
lodgers must be placed in a fix. The only legal
way for the latter to obtain entrance to the build-
ing in which they live seems to be either by scaling
the backyard-fence, or by getting through one of
the windows. Why not simply insist that the bar
shall have only one door, and let the police lock
the place up on Saturday night, and deposit the
key with the Archbishop (who should be above
suspicion) to be kept in holy water till Monday
morning 1
« * » *
Paris Nesbit’s eccentricities keep S.A.
papers going. His latest playful idea was to walk
out of Parkside asylum (wherein he had been
lodged for the third time) and make a bee-
line across country. The tall and athletic
Q.C., an accomplished runner in his day, made the
pace hot from the start and jumped all opposing
fences; three perspiring warders vainly tried to
keep him in sight. Nesbit finally lodged in a
wood-shed and was immediately surrounded by
the whole available detective and police force (the
militia and fire-brigade were summoned but
arrived too late). When a torpedo and three
Maxims had arrived the force plucked up courage
and, in a loud, aggregate voice, demanded Nesbit’s
immediate surrender. The Q.C. then explained
that he was only out for exercise, and drove back
to the asylum again and the force breathed freely
once more. Nesbit’s escapades have come as a
relief to Adelaide journalists after the weary
hospital rows.

The dawning of a kindlier age. The
old George-street offices of the Sydney Govt,
medical officer were labelled with a notice in
which the word “paupers” was painfully frequent.
The notice at the new Board of Health offices
states that “ the sick poor who are entitled to
medical treatment at the public expense are to
apply here,” etc. And yet S.M. Herald fatly
gasps that the hold of Socialism on the minds of
men lessens as the century wanes.
Sydney Mayor Harris promises a strong
illumination of municipal hole-and-cornerisw, and
a consequent liberal pruning of expenses. Some
of the names upon the “ widows’ cart ” list, he
instanced, had been there for 40 years ! It is the
old story of the p 'rasitic growth of vested interests
upon unwholesome corporation carcases. The
“widows’ cart” fixture is an arrangement by
which the horse and vehicle of a defunct corpora-
tion carter are continued in municipal employ-
ment —on behalf of the widow, though as .she has
in turn to employ a cheap driver, the case is often
simply one of sweating the latter.

Q. convict Archer, charged with killing
prison-warder Dodd, pertinently asked for some
legal defence at the police-court trial, whereupon
the P.M. smugly said that it was then only a pre-
liminary investigation, and no doubt, later on, he
would be suitably provided with a legal
Had there been a Public Defender a lot of alleged
evidence now on record against the man would
have been effectually objected to. Archer is so
far gone in consumption that, if lie does not die
previously, he will probably be carried into court
in May.
RONDEL OF SMALL VERMIN.
[FOR TUB BULLETIN.]
How ’scape the bugs that creep and sting,
Or tiny flies that prick and go ?
While I’m asleep some skipping thing
Draws blood, and I may never know.
The village babblers catch and fling
My name in secret to and fro ;
How ’scape the bugs that creep and sting,
Or tiny flies that prick and go ?
Credulity has tales to bring ;
Low malice deals a coward blow :
“ They say ” —“ I’ve heard’’— the chorus sing;
“He says she says they told her so! ”
How ’scape the bugs that creep and sting,
Or tiny flies that prick and go ?
N.S.W.
J. LB GAY BRERERTON.
A HAPPY
FOR THE BULLETIN
THOUGHT.
Maud Mashford needed brains and sense,
I’d always understood ;
You could not call her smile “intense,”
Although her teeth were good,
“ A dolly thing !” her cousins said ;
When I remarked her pretty head,
They answered, “ Made of wood.”
No bumps upon her placid brow,
No strength of jaw, or chin,
No outward signs I must allow —
Of character within,
Had little Maud ; her hazel eyes
Proclaimed a spinster most unwise
In Latin, Greek, or sin.
Brown tresses, such as poets laud,
In sweet disorder twined
About the neck of dolly Maud,
Or flirted with the wind.
Methought, although the girl’s a dunce,
’Twere rather nice to spoon her once,
To prove her lack of mind.
A picnic gives an easy chance
To one who fain would learn
The meaning of a hasty glance,
Sigh, blush, or eyelid-turn.
So feeling, oh, an awful fraud,
I wandered off alone with Maud
To gather coral fern.
I quoted lines from famous books,
And talked my level best;
She gave me only artless looks,
Scarce feigning interest,
Whilst deftly mingling spray with sprit}
To build a coral-fern bouquet,
Its centre like a nest.
Yet, stealthily the foolish charm
Of Maud’s uncultured smile,
The dimples on her muslined arm,
And want oi picnic guile
Were prompting me, the more and more,
To e’en excuse her lack of lore
And academic style.
Could she but raise some bright remark
To witch the perfumed air,
The language of the morning lark
With Maud’s would not compare !
Ah ! then, metliought, upon the spot
I’d ask the lady, wise or not,
To be my future care.
A streamlet, ’neath the frondage green,
Ran gaily down the glade,
Quoth I, “ We have no earthly scene,
But Heaven here displayed.”
She gazed awhile on Nature’s grace,
And whispered, “This, then is the place
Where marriages are made.”
They call my love “ a dolly thing,”
Those cousins tough and tart,
Who’ll never win a golden ring
By saying something smart.
I’ll not attempt to cram her head,
Enough tor me, now we are wed,
To fill her tender heart.
Melb.
EDMUND FISHER.

* * * •
Melb. letter-carriers have naturally taken
up their abode in the suburbs which it is their
business to serve.' Under the new regulations,
the postman has to sign on and off duty at the
head-office. Therefore, a man delivering at, say,
Brighton, has to train it to town —three-quarters
of an hour’s journey—to let the department know
that it is lunch-time. If he desires to dine in the
bosom of his family, he has to do another three-
quarters of train-travelling, and return to town
again after his meal to sign on and get his bag.
This performance has to be repeated in the even-
ing. Another fantastic movement of the depart-
ment of high-kickers !

The kanaka is by no means the unassimi-
lative barbarian some folks fancy. The “boys”
on the Johnstone recently formed a Field La-
borers’ Trades Union, and fixed a minimum wage
for members (also non-members) of ,£36 per
annum. Kanaka unions are not bothered by
blacklegs. A good club or a broad cane-knife in
a brawny black arm makes short work of a ‘ scab ’
Polynesian if he wanders out late in the cane-
brake.

DRIVING MONEY OUT OF THE
COUNTRY.
(Interest on Australasian loans, £ 8,600,000 per
annum.)
Strange that the fat and respectable citizen who is
so anxious for further loans to increase our great
annual output of interest, is the man who is most
disgusted with any measure “ ca'culated to drive
money out of the country."
“The statistics of charitable institutions
are always misleading,” writes an embittered Bul-
letin correspondent. “ Thus so many meals are
given at Sydney soup-kitchen—but are they meals?
Sunday breakfast, basin of gruel; ditto, tea, no
bread ; dinner, basin of nondescript liquid more
like skilly than soup, and a junk of bread. Some
of the wealthiest men in Sydney restrict their
charity to soup-kitchen tickets. Verily they shall
have their reward.”
* * * »
When the Hobart steamer was leaving
Strahan (Tas. W\ Coast) wharf, the other morn-
ing, the passengers on deck were treated to a
unique undress rehearsal. The ship had got a
few yards from shore when a pyjama-clad figure,
waving its trousers in one hand and a portmanteau
in the other, was seen heading full-speed for the
wharf. It was a well-known interprovincial bag-
man who had been taking a farewell “bogey” in
the local creek when the final bell rang. He ran a
great race. Still waving his goods and trousers
he clambered helter-skelter across several barges
and over two or three boats and on to a steam-
launch, finally grabbing his passage as it went
floating by to sea. The congratulatory hand-
clapping which greeted him as he handed his
trousers over the ship's side would have appalled
and embarrassed anybody less modest than a drum-
mer ; he only passed a few forcible short-winded
remarks in a foreign tongue and went below.

The grass-grown grounds of a certain
Adelaide college were so haunted by wandering
cows that the astigmatic principal ukased
pound for next intruding bovine; whereupon
some of the pupils disguised the college “milker”

The engineer-in-chief to Melb. Met. Board
of Works states that the sewering of the smellful
city is being delayed by a dearth of plumbers.
Which sounds rather absurd. Why have a dearth
of plumbers, when all that the average licensed
fraud knows about plumbing could be learned in
six weeks by any intelligent mechanic outside of
the lunatic asylums ? Create plumbers if required,
and get some fresh young ideas into the industry

A Semaphore (S.A.) postman recently
applied to the corporation for suspension of bylaw
against riding bikes on footpaths—in bis case only.
Said he could lighten his work greatly, and would
take care not to bark the shins of the lieges.
Refused.

Melb. : “J2e mixed marriages, I do re-
member the white woman said to be the first who
married a Chinese. They were familiar in Haw-
thorn. He had a nice buggy, and drove her about
amid universal gapes. The women used almost
to faint, or pretend to, at the sight, exclaiming
‘ Good God ! ’ The saying long was that only
Irishwomen would marry Chinamen, but this is
a mistake, as things go. Possibly they were all
ignorant Irish immigrants at first. The Church
has naturally a great dislike to these marriages,
for there seem to be no Catholic Chinese in Aus-
tralia, although in China the native Catholics
number vastly more than all the Protestants put
together. Our immigration is from a very limited
part of China.”

Society item. Constable Shabler, of Bir-

Yet all are supposed to be “ past work.” Many have

Familiarity and contempt. At a presenta-
tion to coroners Candler and Morrison, Melbourne
morgue inquest-room was, the other day, trans-
formed into a banquet-room. The veteran
autopsian, Dr. Neild, presided with his endless
flow of anecdote, second only to that of the
deceased Dr. Youl himself. Unlimited fizz
graced the corpse-tables, and at the ends were
huge bowls of ice, about the history of which it
was not considered wise to speculate over-curiously.
Surely a veritable feast of ghouls !

Melbourne newspaper-readers have been
much confused of late to find accounts of a doctors’
strike side by side with announcements of a large
decrease of local mortality.
DIVORCE.
“Marriages are made in Heaven,”
Divorces, then, in Hell:
Which blest boon to mankind given
Is surely ordered well;
For since it is decreed that fools,
God-joined in nuptial revel,
Shall not by man be freed, ’tis wise
To fall back on the Devil.
P.M.
BOOM—WRECK.
The fate of fools who overmuch aspire
To gorge themselves with foul excess !
’Tis thwarted greediness with punctured tire
Stuck in a sandy wilderness!
P.M.
Wilson Barrett.
Wilson Barkett, actor, author, dramatist,
strikes an interviewer as a charming man of busi-
ness. Given the cue, he will talk on any subject,
in any mood. “ What do you mean?” he asked
at the outset—“art or cliatf? sensuousness or
funny stories ? ” His physical attractions are his
big, brainy-looking head, his strong body and legs,
his mastiff neck, his long, waving hair a la Mark
Twain. Speaking, he has the air of a man who,
having strong convictions and strong methods,
has won a following which yields a most satisfac-
tory income. Everyone knows that his success is
due at least quite as much to constitutional
strength and hard work as to talent. And,
to crown all, he possesses the happy endowment
of optimism and good nature to such a degree that
you reason he has the digestion of an alligator.
“ My father was a speculator, and as speculators
go nine times out of ten—he failed. I was stage-
struck as a child; knew the part of Hamlet at 12 ;
played first as a 14-year-old amateur in a barn ;
started work in a bank ; at 16 yielded to stage
fascination ; starteckon the boards at a guinea a
week ; have loved the boards ever since. My
enthusiasm and strength enabled me to do the
severest work—I broke down, however, in the
twenties ; but soon recovered. At 12, I vowed to
play Hamlet in a theatre controlled by myself ;
realized this ambition nearly 30 years after. Have
made two fortunes ; lost the first speculating: was
.£40,000 in debt, and sinking under the weight of
the interest calls when I wrote ‘The Sign of the
Cross.’ This lifted me out of the sea of trouble
and set me on the fair way of fortune again.” As
this religious play was so money-yielding, was it
God or the Devil that inspired the masterful Bar-
rett to write it ?
“ Hall Caine living and moving in London
could not write. I said to him ‘ lion’t move in
this London Society ; it only wants you as the
last curious sort of person to play with ; fail in
vmir next book and it kicks you out. Go back to
your Isle of Man and work.’ He went. I said
that London Society, higgling, haggling and
pecking at the man about his books would affect
his clearness of intellect. A man must be free from
vexatious influences to do his best. A newspaper-
editor who tried to be a society god would be
harassed into idiocy. Pinero, the best of our
dramatists, is destroying himself by listening to
people. He tries to write so as to catch both
Archer and Clement Scott — a great handicap,
when you remember that A.rcher receives 10 per
cent, on every Ibsen play that can be floated upon
the London market!
I dramatised Caine’s ‘ Mamxman * according
to my lights. I make Philip seduce Kate; not
Kate seduce Phillip, as is the case in the novel.
From my dramatic standpoint, it would never do
for the woman to seduce a man. I was introduced
by Hall Caine to Pete’s original—the ‘ Diamond
King ’ he is called on the island. The original of
Kate Cregeen? I am not at liberty to say. Much
of Pete’s baby was written from Caine’s own baby.
Gloria, in ‘ The Christian,’ was modelled on
Caine’s sister, who is on the stage. One very
successful Mercia is a niece of mine, who was
taken straight from a convent to play the part.
“Caine in ‘The Christian’ is hostile to the
stage ! Before he wrote the book I told him of a
manager trying to seduce a stage-struck girl ; and
of a man taking over a theatre in order to exploit
an actress. He uses these facts in ‘The Christian ’
‘Concerning Clement Scott and stage im-
morality I say that the surroundings of the stage
are more likely to conduce to morality. If an
actress is more inclined to immorality because she,
earning her own living, is independent of men
then women-doctors, artists, authors, painters’
&c., who make enough to live upon are just as
liable. The woman who is dependant upon man
is ,more liable. than the one that is independent.
It’s the mere spiteful scandal of inferiority that
says that the manager's mistress gets preference.
A manager is a business man, and has to secure the
talent which will draw houses and pay his bills.
“ Concerning the statement that an actress has
no social obligations, I would like to say that
so far as my own daughter is concerned I’d
rather see her going through a love-scene on the
stage than being hugged round a room in a volup-
tuous waltz by some society god who is the worse
for champagne. Social obligations ! Caulfield
race-course, for instance drinking whisky and
stealing liair-pins and soap ! Society must not
say anything against the stage, if only for the
reason that an actress has not to submit to all
the miserable shifts and orthodox artifice-, of
husband-hunting. To the prurient I would say
that we mummers are perhaps not better than
the best, but we are generally better than our
critics.”-
S. A. MILLS.
