All Drapers Everywhere.
Home, Sweet Home.’
What a beautiful place home is when the father
is bilious, the mother is suffering from female
weakness, and the baby has the croup ; father
frowns and swears mother scolds and grumbles,
and baby yells ! And yet this husband and wife
may be two of the best-natured and reasonable
people on the globe when they are in good health.
It may seem ridiculous to say that nearly all
domestic disagreements are caused by the liver,
but a careful study of the problem reveals the fact
that such an assertion is quite within the bounds
of reason. When a person is in ill-health they
are impatient, unreasonable, and quarrelsome ;
what more natural then than a family “ tiff.”
Few people are cynical enough to believe that
married couples don’t wish for peaco and har-
mony, and some reason must, therefore, be
found for their all too-common differences.
Bile Beans come, therefore, in the guise of a
peacemaker: they put the liver in good order, and
invigorate the whole system, giving new life, as it
were; then everything goes as smoothly as a
freshly-risen honeymoon. A word to the wise :
Marriage need not be a failure while Bile Beans
cure indigestion, liver troubles, and various other
ailments, thereby making husbands and wives
happy companions. Bile Beans are made in one
of the largest and best laboratories in the world at
Detroit, Michigan, U.S.A. They are an un-
doubted specific for constipation, biliousness,
headache, indigestion, debility, female weakness,
and all liver troubles. For sale at all chemists;
S>rice 9|d. per small box, large box Is. lid., or six
or 6s. Get the genuine; take no worthless sub-
stitutes.*
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 15, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
The choir-boys’ mothers and sisters gathered
round the christening-urn at St. Andrew’s Cathe-
dral last week, and strained maternal eyes at
Victor John, the Governor’s first-born grandson,
among the Bishop’s toby frills. No one else knew
when the event was to come off, so the choir-boys
and their relatives had it all to themselves. The
invited guests couldn’t get near the ceremony, and
sat thankfully in a back seat, the Admiral and
Miss Pearson among them. Mrs. Ferguson
smiled nervously whenever the baby cried, and
Miss Alice Brand, who is said to have the weird
record of never haying held a baby in her
arms before, looked piteously afraid of dropping it.
On the whole, the ceremony wasn’t nearly as ex-
citing as a christening in a Primitive Methodist
chapel'. There the babies go up to the font in
batches, and a dozen nervous parents stand round
the rail with bundles of pelissed infant, and the
parson mixes up the names, and the fathers whisper
inaudibly in his ear, and the miserable mothers
pull the fathers’coat-tails all in vain, and Mary
Ann is fatally mixed with Mary Jane, and Rose
Violet is condemned to the awful impossibility of
smelling as sweet as Ivy Daphne.
The first reception by the new Mayoress at the
Town Hal], last Thursday, was a brilliantly-
decorated affair of palms, and roses, and a lot of
smart dressing, with a suggestion of autumnal
decay in the gowning of the outer crowd. Mrs.
Harris received in a gown with Paris stamped in
sable on its white brocade, and bowed graciously
to several startled-looking sailor hats and morning
overalls that hadn’t expected to find themselves so
conspicuous, and didn’t seem to like it. An air
of lavishness spread itself over the official doings,
and the mayoral coffers are evidently going to be
freely unlocked in the determination to paint the
lily of the. field a brighter pink than other alder-
manic artists. The lady who couldn’t get anything
fit to wear in the London shops appeared to have
satisfied herself in Sydney or the suburbs with a
silk of a pinkish mauve, and a black bonnet with
a blue bow. The newest thing in brides looked
like a nice lolly-stick in striped grass-lawn. Mrs.
Burdekin wore the pink of the Albani reception,
and was evidently uncrushed by the verdict passed
on her by Frederick Dolman that her day of great
things is over.
Everyone is asking who Frederick Dolman is.
Last winter a mild young drummer connected
with Cassell’s publishing firm arrived in Sydney
with a big head, and an underbite, and let the re-
port be gently wafted round that he was working
up an exhaustive series of articles on Australian
art and literature for a tip-top English magazine.
For many months the Nineteenth Century and
other intellectual monthlies have been eagerly
searched by various feminine eyes, and it has been
a bit of a cropper to the interviewed ones to find
the whole exhaustion resolve itself into a meek
article in the good little Woman at Home, a ladies’
magazine edited by Annie Swan. The photograph
which purports to show our Victoria Salon is in-
deed a revelation. Frederick must have mixed
his photographs after he watered his lemonade,
and a vast continental hall lighted with innumer-
able candled chandeliers, tesselated floors, statues,
mirrors, and furniture of the first, second, or third
empire, have swallowed up at one bite the quiet
little rooms of the Salon we know.
Side by side with this picture of pcrmp and
dollars is the statement that the club has seventy
members paying an annual subscription of £3 35.,
which brings the income up to ,£220 a year !
Casting round for a reflection on Sydney ladies,
Mr. Dolman fishes up the marrowy one that for
some time past there has been a sort of feud
between the women whose power was essentially
the power of the purse, and those whose, newer
influence arose from—eye, hand and mind. I
don’t know much about the influences that rule
the ends of women’s feuding, but always thought
the hand was a primeval one. It is old enough,
anyway, to punish little Frederick for his malice
in raking up an old story gone to sleep some time
now, but which burnt both sides of the conflict
sorely while it: was awake. There are several
women scribblers in Sydney who were approached
by him last winter with a facetious smile, and
asked inquisitively, “ Now, wasn’t it you who
wrote the ‘Sin of the Angels?’ Be so much
obliged if you’d give me the details of the fun ! ”
The theatrical amateur has already awakened
from its summer sleep, and last Thursday evening
turned up in force to shed a dingy light on Tom
Taylor’s “Fouehe.” Ivy Gorrick played leading
lady, and made her debut before a Sydney public,
with a view to further appearances. The theatre -
was crammed to the top windows, but only the
three front rows and the boxes heard anything.
The men whispered through their parts in the
shiny dancing pumps of 1898, and the
plash and breeches of a century ago, and Ivy
Gorrick herself was too heavily weighted by
her company to do much starring, A
pretty brunette, with a pouting mouth and
flashing eyes, she showed a capacity for throwing
herself about naturally quite remarkable in an
amateur.
Another Sydney lady, Miss Inez Bensusan, who
has only been seen out here on the amateur stage,
will shortly appear at the Lyceum, where she
takes Lilian Wheeler’s place as juvenile lead.
Miss Bensusan has a record of hard work and suc-
cess in South Africa with Herbert Flemming’s co.
where she played second lead to Hope Dudley!
She also went on tour with Mrs. Kendall through
Scotland.
The “ Terror of Paris ” has scared the Parisian
element right out of the dressing of the latest
Lyceum shocker, and a dust-cloak, a velveteen
wrapper, and a macintosh wander about between
Maud Williamson, her maid, and her plump pale
artist who stands out with an amount of adipose
tissue Sydney artists can’t emulate. By the way,
the story our longest-haired artist has been telling
about one of his brethren has ended in smoke.
The longest-haired one had an auction sale of his
pictures, and declared that a short-haired artist
wept to the auction rooms when nobody was look-
ing, and slipped two of his own pictures in among
the lot that Artist No. 1 had canvassed for so
energetically. The two stray ducklings went off
at fancy prices, and a bountiful hair-shedding
threatened, until the auctioneer stepped in and
explained that it was his hair that ought to be
shed. He bought the pictures from an outsider,
and put them into the sale in the primitive belief
that all Sydney artists were alike to the general
public.
The ambition of the average youthful male to
be a tram-guard when the days of sailor-suits are
over, will die a natural death this century. When
young men are temporarily taken on by the
N.S.W. tramway department, and have a
suburban tram given into their care, the artful
department turns the wiles of still more artful
women on them, first day of their charge. Two
nice-looking women enter, and when the guard,
in the bravery of his new regimentals, conies
round for tickets, they look into his eyes
and smile bewitchingly. If the smile doesn’t
smite him, and he still demands their
fares, they begin to wheedle him. In a weak
moment, he succumbs, gives back smile for
smile, and doesn't take their tickets. That after-
noon he is reported, next day suspended, and
sacked the day after.
A lady lecturer from Sydney discoursed in a
country town, a Sunday or two back, on “ The
Dark Side of Sydney Life.” As one of the Sydney
rescue workers, the lecturess had her subject at
her finger-tips, and the bucolic audience of Sleepy
Hollow were thrilled in their seats as she fright-
ened them with all the old familiar bogies, the
pushes, the opium-dens, Wexford-street, and all
the horrors which Chief Justice Darley described
at the Foreign Missionary meeting, when he was
lukewarm on the subject of Christians travelling
Cathaywards in order to upset the Confueian
apple-cart and place a copy of “Sacred Songs and
Solos” by Ira D. Sankey within the reach of
every Chow, however cashless. But these old,
familiar rocks have been thrown so often that the
public has grown accustomed to the shock, and
doesn’t feel the bump when they are hurled for
the hundredth time. Why doesn’t an orator who
wants to be sensationally original select another
set of shocking examples—all out of the same old,
sinful Sydney—lecture with lime-light views, say,
on the Gentle Art of Spitting as practised by the
Sydney loafer, who is an abomination at
every street corner, and makes a ’bus-ride such a
joy to the martyred inside-fare on a holiday, when
all the windows.are open and the cascades fall all
around from the spitters on the roof. And yet
some folk can’t understand why women want to
ride on top of the ’buses.
The long-looked-for remittance had arrived, and
the lady decided a visit to Sydney for a fit-out,
and, to keep well within her resources, she stayed
with a friend. After being some time in the city,
and buying no end of furniture and fixings, the
friend suggested making a call on some acquaint-
ances, but, when the time arrived, the guest was
discovered in bed. “I had to wash my stockings,”
she explained, “ and, as I only have the one pair,
I was waiting till they dried.” She had spent
<£lso in the city—but not on stockings !
Bananas and buns, which are cheap and filling
at the price, are said to form the staple of Wes-
tralian vice-regal ball-suppers. Imagine the in-
ternal tortures of an intrepid waltzer who should
essay to whirl ecstatically after putting himself
outside such provender. Also the latest official
entertainment list contained about 2000 names
scraped from all sections of the naicest and others.
The six families haven’t increased to that number,
or anything like it, and the invitees included a
legion that never should have been listed of absen-
tees, deaders, and other ineligibles taken at ran-
dom from the directory.
Fiction is sometimes stranger than truth. In a
tale by a leading author in a leading Sydney
morning paper, “ She had stepped out and had
taken one strapping warrior by the arm, studying
with concerned face a long, ugly gash. Tearing a
strip from her handkerchief she bound it round.”
A handkerchief as marvellous as Treasurer Reid’s
surplus.
It was rather rough on man when a woman’s-
rights lecturer lately proposed, as a way out of
the difficulty of women usurping men’s billets,
that each young man pressed too hard by the com-
petition should marry the girl. Like the usual
out-and-out women’s-righter’s demand, this claim
insists upon woman eating her cake and having it,
while meek, down-trodden man foots the baker’s
bill and is alwavs on hand to provide fresh duff.
It gives a girl a choice of two careers—for matri-
mony is, after all, a very old-established and re-
spectable female occupation ; while the only alter-
native to being ousted it offers the young man is
to have to support a probable business rival till
death or the divorce-court do them part. But,
taking them all round, there are so few satisfac-
tory jobs which the average woman can take on
that I think about the best billet now going is to
be a School of Industry orphan kept by the State
and turned out at 18 with a neat £3O Govt, cheque
’•—only each heiress generally marries, whereupon
the husband blews the dowry in a month.
Sydney Unitarian Church is presided over by
an up-to-date pastor who likes to move with the
times. Hence black-lettered calico-signs on a
board facing Liverpool-st. announce, during the
week, the topics to be wrestled with on the coming
Sawbath. Rev. “ Joseph-in-Canaan ” Walters
chooses subjects that will draw, such as “ The
Modern Marriage Market,” and other topics
bruited by the “ New” scare-women. It wouldn’t
be surprising to see the board loudly querying the
passing tram and ’bus traffic “Does Jones Throw?”
or “ That Fly.” The consequence of this parsoni-
cal enterprise is full houses and much clinking of
collection coin. Rev. Walters had a career in
Melbourne —he virtually built up the present
tabernacle, a cheerful and handsome brick struc-
ture vice a dirty little square hall now pulled down
—and vvas the most successful pastor the sect had,
the next being Miss Martha Turner, afterwards
Mrs. Webster/sister of H. Gyles Turner. But the
pillars of the church quarrelled with their shepherd
over that negligeable quantity, Unitarian theology.
During the illness in Queensland of one of Aus-
tralia’s wealthiest women, her son by a previous
marriage invited her to his country mansion, and,
while there, persuaded her to alter her will, leaving
him everything, and cutting off the second family
without the proverbial, red cent. Unfortunately
for this little scheme, news of it reached theseeond
husband’s ears, and the result was that the pliable
invalid altered her alteration, this time leaving
out the already well-gilt firstborn. Some weeks
later the end came, and the obsequies were no
sooner over than the latter magnificent person de-
manded that the- will- should- be- road. —lt wae
read, and a few minutes later a particularly
swaggah funeral-party was shocked by the eldest
son dashing out of the house, slamming the door,
and remarking that the whole thing was a rascally
swindle, and he had a good mind to call the
police in.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent :
“ Toorak has been whispering and hinting for
the last week or two about the doings of a willowy
young wife, whose languishing eyes were supposed
to have worked havoc in the wrong male bosom.
At afternoon-teas one heard that the lady had
actually eloped, and that hubby was in hot pur-
suit. Mrs. Grundy couldn’t believe her eyes
when the alleged fugitive turned up at the Go-
vernment House hop t’other week resplendent in
a new frock, and attended by her spouse appar-
ently as devoted as ever. The first shock of
astonishment was succeeded by a feeling of intense
annoyance on Mrs. Grundy's part.
“ Poor Gov. Brassey, unless his looks belied
him, loathed and hated that Thursday concert.
No old curmudgeon ever seemed more bored, cross
and sleepy than did the Ancient Mariner after his
third Albani pill. As the night waxed old, and
people persisted in encoring everything, the vice-
regal martyr crossed his arms on his heaving
breast and literally hugged his misery, while his
legs jiggered irritably. Men always show temper
in their legs. Go up to the Presbyterian Assembly
when there’s a row on, and see if I’m not right.
Prof. Rentoul bounds from one foot to the other
like a jumping-jack, and shakes his fist alternately.
Prof. Harper sits waiting for a chance to pitch in,
nearly tugging his moustache out by the roots and
convulsing one shin until you expect it to break
off at the knee.
“ Lady Brassey, in her black frock, rather
relished Judge Madden’s jokes. Bishop Goe’s
good lady, who came with the Brasseys, set an
example of Christian contentment to everybody,
and beamed and smiled upon that dear, meri-
torious Albani, the friend of our beloved Queen,
until her (Mrs. G.’s) plump cheeks must have
ached.
“Albani herself was imposing in cream satin,
with a sort of deep apron of impressive black lace.
Two frills of lace bordered a thrilling train,
cherished by its wearer as the apple of her eye
through the storm and stress of all her antics. Of
course, she wore the tiara, and the medal, and a
diamond necklet, not to speak of a chain which
kept finding its way out of some nook near the
singer’s heart. No doubt there was a portrait of
‘ Her Most Gracious 5 slung on to the end of it,
or a lock of hair, or something.
“Talking of legs, do you notice that Madame
occasionally indulges in a triumphant little kick
when she successfully cracks a loud vocal nut. It
reminds me of Alice Leamar in ‘Sister Mary
Jane’s top note.’

only daughter of St. Albans’ Wilson.

“Gov. Brassey’s Sunday-school picnic to his
brother Masons is reckoned about his sickest per-
formance during a notable career. The brethren
were invited to the ’Ouse from four to six with
their female adjuncts, and regaled on claret-cup
and pastry. Not a drop nor a snuff of any stronger
liquor was available. Just think of it—no whisky
tor seasoned Masons ! One P.G.M.X.Y.Z., being
asked why he went, said, “O, to please the
missus !” Poor Masons ! They were so proud, too,
when they lassoed Uncle Tom and made him
goat-actor-in-chief of the Brotherhood.
“ English cricket-supporters seemed down in
the dumps on Saturday. Women worked off their
dejection by audibly slating their neighbors’
raiment. Under the awning, a society damsel of
strong anti-Australian sympathies openly aired
her desire for a British ball to cross the fence
thereabouts and land on the largest hat m her
vicinity. Another untamed young thing let her
angry passions carry her to the point of patting
down with her parasol (accidentally, she said) an
aggressive aigrette in the bonnet in front. The
wearer promptly accepted the challenge, and in
two minutes talked the aggressor limp and apolo-
getic.
“ The Governor’s wife was there all the fore-
noon, and came back after lunch without changing
her ungaudy navy-blue skirt, white-scrolled blouse,
and green-and-white millinery. Capt. Brand and
Aide Neville, gaunter than, ever in tweeds and
sailor hats, sat on either side of Lady 8., wistfully
watching the mediocrity of their compatriots. At
dinner-hour, the patriotic trio trudged out to in-
spect the pitch, and shake their heads sadly over
the torn, worn wicket.

makes up as an ancient embonpointy man-catcher,

“ What is sauce for the gander is evidently not
sauce for the goose. Recently two young men and
a young woman were brought before Williamstown
Police-court charged with having unlawfully dis-
posed of tickets in a * sweep ’ on the A.N. A.
wheel-race. The miserable male prisoners were
fined .£1 each with £1 Is. costs. The young
woman was let off with a fine of ss. and no costs—
‘in consideration of her sex.’ Of course, she was
pretty. Judges don’t seem to have changed much
in their attitude towards Lovely Woman since
the days of Phryne.
“ Dr. Cockburn (S.A. Minister for Education),
speaking to a large Melb. audience of ladies in*
terested in Woman’s Suffrage, told them that, in
S.A., 'a man would formerly raise his hat only
to a woman of rank or title, but now he would do
so to a washerwoman.’ The ladies had listened
respectfully to his previous remarks, but when ha
made this statement they laughed the hard,
metallic, feminine laugh of derision. As well
they might. A male speaker may confuse a
female audience over questions of politics, but
when it comes to trying the same game with re-
gard to social observances, he merely makes of
himself in their eyes an unhappy Johnass. Of
course if the washerwoman happens to be young
and pretty, or even buxom, anything might
happen.
“ There is a lot of gush over the departure of
Pastor Macartney, youngest son of the demised
Dean, to take up the nice billet of secretary to the
Bible Society in London. He is a sincere and
amiable man, but the most depressing cold-water
douche who ever stepped into a pulpit. At Caul-
field Church he has been a dreadful infliction, and
the place will spring into life on his departure.
Mr. Macartney’s wife is wealthy, and was widow
of the best-known Tasmanian, Kermode the
merino man. After one of his British trips, Mr.
M. wrote a characteristic little book, ‘England,
Home, and Beauty,’from which it will be inferred
that we are parting with yet another man who
carries his eyes in the back of his head.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI
R.C. Bishop Cani, of Queensland, lately
deceased, was a strict disciplinarian, and dreaded
by his easy-living underlings. For years be slept
invariably on the floor of his bedroom, no matter
where domiciled, which trait at times gave rise to
strange stories.
Paris Nesbit, Q.C., an excellent and fear-
less horseman, will, from all accounts, have to be
restrained from emulating Lindsay Gordon’s
famous feat of jumping one of the fences surround-
ing the Blue Lake at Mt. Gambier. Nesbit, by
the way, is somewhat of a replica of Gordon, both
in appearance and temperament.
Chief Justice Way has just asxed S.A.
Register to start a subscription-fund for a Marcus
Clarke memorial. “ His remains lie in Melb.
cemetery, unmarked save by a plain iron rail
without inscription.” A preposterous monument
of the bourgeois type would be out of place, but
possibly there is an Australian artist who could
design something simple and suitable. The
Bulletin will be glad to acknowledge subscrip-
tions.
Trenwith, the Vie. laborist, has made
himself rather ridiculous to the experienced parlia-
mentarians of the Convention by apeing the
House-of-Commons-mannah and wearing his hat
religiously on all occasions. The tile, when it
isn’t a black bell-topper, is a grey cross between a
boxer and a slouch, which would cause even Andy
Kelly to weep tears of sorrow. Beyond endeavor-
ing to introduce the hat habit, Trenwith has been
of little apparent service to the Federation cause
this innings.
Ricardo : “As I write, Henry Gyles
Turner lies in a serious condition, as the result of
a coach accident in Maoriland. Not a better-
liked man in Melb. city than this literary manager
of the Commercial Bank of Australia. The lay-to-
your-book people quarrelled with his dabbling in
books. However, he came out of the boom crash
as well as those bankers who only bank. Turner
founded the Melbourne Review, but he will be
better remembered as the man who invented re-
construction.

Sydney Catholic Press is for ever girding
at the secular journals and the “ Godless schools/’
because of their alleged disregard of the lofty
moral standard which inspires its own columns.
Here is an instance of the way in which it trains
the youthful mind by example—the most forcible
kind of training. In its issue of Feb. 26 it serves
up the well-known scheme of ethical exercise pro-
pounded by Franklin, introducing it as “the
moral code of Benjamin Franklin, the greatest
all-round American.” Now “the greatest all-
round American ” was a pronounced deist of the
same pattern as his friend, Tom Paine, and
against the virtue “Humility” in his list of the
moralities he wrote the words, “Imitate Jesus
and Socrates,” as any one may learn by consulting
the greatest all-round American’s autobiography.
This sort of teaching would scarcely suit the
Catholic Press, so, in order to encourage the
youthful Australian in ways of veracity, it calmly
falsifies the record, and represents Franklin as
having set before him this lesson : “ Humility—
imitate Jesus Christ,” omitting all mention of
Socrates

“ No ; why did you think so? ”
Because I once heard you say it was a mystery
to you how people could keep awake after
dining at mid-day.”
“Ah! that was before I got LOASEyS
‘WAHOO.’ You see, I was a dyspeptio
then. Now, I dine when I like, and eat
what 1 like; but always with my eye on
the ‘WAHOO’ bottle.”

Rev. W. B. Kelly, appointed last week to
be first Bishop of Perth, is the second native-born
to be made an R.C. Bishop. Dr. Dwyer, of Mait-
land, was the first.
Line on the street contents-board of John
Norton’s organ, Truth: “Our Rotten Corpora-
tion!” John should be more careful about little
things like this. Being an alderman now, he is
getting fat himself.
The manful and benevolent Francis Sceusa,
the solitary avowed Socialist in N.S.W. Civil
Service, has been left money by Italian relatives,
and goes to the land of the Carbonari to reduce
his high ideals to practice.
Editor Curnow, of S.M. Herald, has left on
a six-months’ furlough, and Henry Gullett will act
at his locum tenens. The queerest thing in the
world is that Gullet happens to be the biggest
shareholder in the rival Sydney Daily Tele-
graph.
Henry Lavrson is now the proud possessor
of a son and heir ; born about six weeks ago at Wel-
lington, M.L. He is to be called Jim, in memory
of the many unknown heroes of that name who
have been buried under the mulga trees out in the
Never Never.
A “SWEEP CONSULTATION”: A sketch
in Belmore Park, Sydney.
Singular coincidence that a majority of
the Melb. Board of Works employes names (or
their wives’maiden names) are identical with the
patronymics of the commissioners, distinguished
civic luminaries, and suburban town clerks. Of
course it is merely a coincidence.
The unctuous slobbering of certain Low
Churchites over the arrival of the Anglican
Archbishop of Sydney has disgusted the High
Churchites, whose thanksgivings would have been
deep, if not loud, had Saumarez Shilling Smith
been detained in England for the term of his
natural life.
Justice Matthew Henry Stephen will ob-
tain a long-expected knighthood at the next date
of general distribution. It was due a year ago,
but was inadvertently overlooked. Justice
Stephen will be the first N.S.W. son of a knight
to be knighted. In Victoria Judge Williams was
similarly knighted.
Ex-editor Haddon, at his banquet, referred
generously to the demised worthies of the Argus,
the foremost being that peerless leader-writer,
Jardine Smith. And yet, strange to say. Syme
had thought Jardine not good enough even for the
then paltry Herald, when evening satellite to the
Age.
One Australian Governor pours laments
into the ears of his friends about the awful expense
of representing Queen Victoria. “ Demands are
being made on ray purse every day ; my hand is
never out of my pocket !” is his dirge. It’s quite
true about his hand never being out of his pocket.
He keeps it there all right.

W.A. Lands Department, and spends his time in
humbugging the backblocks visitors, survivors of
frehistoric man, who come from Tooroomoora or
.yndliwadalup, bringing long complaints and
large-sized mangold-wurzels.

bone of Berryism, such little as re-
mains. In Ballarat the women

the idea locally prevails
that he has been a gay
old bird. When Mae-

ful eyes ?’ ”
Gist of what English
Church papers have been say-
ing about Archbishop Bobcab
Smith’s departure for Australia :
“For the third time he leaves our
shores for far-off Australia leaving behind
him the comforts and pleasures of civilisation
to labor among the almost heathen residents
of that great continent. Again will he assume the
weather-beaten hat, the tattered shirt,, the well-
patched breeches, the blue blanket; but ere long
to return again, we hope, to our shores for ‘ rest
and recuperation. ’ ”
Ex-editor Haddon, of Melb. Argus, will
receive, in his new capacity of representative of
the Wilson interest, =£looo a year, plus ,£250 out
of the estate. As it stands at present the Wilson
estate has more money than it knows what to do
with ; when Edward Wilson died a large number
of annuities were left to his personal friends, and
as these have, one by one, followed him, the
money has fallen into the residuary estate until
this—after paying the large claims of the legatees
and the charitable institutions—reaches at least
£lOO,OOO. When the last annuitant dies the whole
of the estate will be divided among charities.
“ Wod,” to The Bulletin :
That big reporter and D. Syme varn is surely a bit
stretched. Big as the biggest Melb* scribe may" be, he
must he a giant indeed to beat the massive D. S. There
have been few men in Melbourne equal in height, breadth
of shoulders and strength of limb to the taciturn Age
proprietor. If that reporter really did threaten S. as
stated, then allow me lo whisper in his ear. Before his
newspaper days D. S. was a contractor, and it is on
record that he would put his strongest navvies to the
blush in matters of strength. Also, Syme is frequently
credited (or blamed ?) with a broad Scotch accent. He
is a “border man,” and there is nothing in his pro-
nunciation to suggest the sobriquet of “ Dawvid.”
“Leo” to The Bulletin :
In “mixed” marriages will probably be found the
reason for the appearance of ladies with Jewish names at
the meeting re the N.S.W. Irish ’9B Centenary Com-
memoration. The husbands of these ladies bear" any of
the regulation Hebrew names, ai d are not from Erin’s
Isle—hut find the ladies' maiden names, and the mystery
will probably be explained. Many ladies married to
scions of the Chosen keep their own national sympathies
intact. One Irish-Jewess, when her husband was dying,
did all she could to induce hint to receive the ministra-
tions of a priest, and even introduced one into the house
—but to no purpose. Isaac Moses (which name does for
the occasion) simply turned to the wall and went to sleep
in Abraham’s bosom.

Re riding to church. In Vic. boom-time
a little tin god, whose umbrella was famous, drove
to prayer-shop in carriage and pair. Then John
drove back and appeared with a complete new
turn-out to drive the bqomster home. Since
which the little man has apparently seen the evil
of his ways. Anyway, he walks now.

Miss Joan Nevill, daughter of Lord Wil-
liam N.’s elder brother, became engaged to marry
the Marquis of Camden, a few months ago. A
passing cloud of family disgrace is apt to be useful
in affaires de cceur. The gallant Marquis, perhaps,
discovered the young lady weeping over uncle’s
downfall, and promptly offered her the consola-
tion of a coronet.
The historical M'Sharry arbitration case
will barely reach the re-heated stage on its reopen-
ing after the close of the Convention, when it is
estimated it will be again adjourned for the diver-
sion of N.S.W. general elections. One Q.C.’s
address will, it is said, extend over 30 or 40 days—
which is quite likely, as plaintiff’s evidence covered
143 days and defendant’s 92.
Thus the wild and hairy M.P. Hoolan’s
Mundic Miner on itself :
The Mundic is at present an awfully measly concern in
appeal anee, and its belly has quite a greyhound look
through long fasting. But, notwithstanding the dry-
weather scabs that adorn its hide, its teeth are white and
sharp, and its mouth requires neither roofing, stumping,
gumming, nor bridging. It is ready to sink its molars
into the flesh of all who throw stones at it, whether they
come as old-time friends or new-form d foes, and it yells
out to “ Come on ! ”
Sam Winter, of Melb. Herald, Australia’s
champion objnrgator, is going to tour the Hcly
Land—probably as a penance for his long, long
years of bad language. After he has purified
himself in the Jordan, dabbled in the Dead Sea,
and meditated on the Mount of Olives, he intends
to get along to England in time for the Derby.
Then he is going to see the Grand Prix run at
Paris. Which recalls that when Joe Thompson
made a trip to England (in the same boat that
took back Lord and Lady Rosebery), he also went
to see the great French race. And that when he
returned to Melbourne, Sam Winter directed a
reporter to interview him. “And ask parti-
cularly,” said Sam, “what Mr. Thompson thinks
of the Grand Prix!” Now, Sam does not speak
French, and as he made the second word rhyme
with “sticks,” the soul of that uninstructed
reporter-boy (he came from Barnawartha and his
salary was 235.) was mightily abashed as he put
the question to the bookmaker. Joe stared ; then
be grinned. “ Well—wot a hignorant crowd
noosepaper men is to be sure. Tell your boss,
cully, that he don’t know French enough to plant
beans. It's ‘ Grong Pree ! —Pree ! see !’ ”
Mansfield (Vic.) Caledonian Society lately
appointed one M'Kenzie as president, and ap-
prised him by letter of the honor that had fallen
upon him. Whereupon the M‘Kenzie wrote as
follows :
In acknowledging your memo, of the 12t,h inst., I have
much pleasure in accepting the honor accorded to me as
President of the Caledonian Society. I hope yourself and
committee will (as before) work with good-will and
energy, and not, strive with opposition to each other ; at
the same time 1 shall do my best to act fairly on all sides,
and show no party feeling. I have no doubt the ensuing
sports gathering will be as successful as in former
years, and be a credit to the Caledonians of the district.
Thanking you for the position in which you have placed
nte.—P.S.—l authorise vou to obtain from Mrs. Comer
drinks, at mv expense, to the amount of 55., with best
wishes to the society, for yourself and committee, as I
cannot attend through it not being moonlight.
There were nine Caledonians present on the occa-
sion, and when an entry had been made in the
minute-book that the letter was “ received,’’the
society wiped its pen on its kilt and looked at
itself sorrowfully. Nine Caledonians and ten
sax pences worth of whusky—only one bottle among
them all 1
A Chicago paper prints an elaborate notice
on J. M. Smart, well-known as a Melb. boom-time
book-canvasser. Smart seems to have taken a bill
of kava-root home to the States and has worked
its value up so much that the Smithsonian Insti-
tute has put two special detectives on to watch
it whilst Smart, Esq., exhibits it privately.
That bit of kava has boomed so greatly
that it’s long odds he will sell it at a guinea an
ounce. “Sacred root only allowed to Samoan
royalty,” says he. The root has been viewed —as
a special favor —by all the big bugs in Chicago,
and is now to be awarded a special case in the
Smithsonian Institute. Smith, before he left
Australia, said that America •'-'as the place to
“ work a fake.”
Melbourne illustrated wetuiies are giving
pretty pictures of Corporal Adair, of Ballarat.
Corporal Adair (sweet name !) is a beautiful young
soldier with a spiked moustache, who. has had
fame thrust upon him by a real live duchess-—a nice
little widow, by the way. The Duchess of Albany
wrote a letter to Corporal Adair, informing him
that Daisy Bell, the kangaroo presented to Queen
Victoria by the Victorian Mounted Rifles, was
dead, and consequently Corporal Adair is covered
with glory, and is basking in the smiles of the
awfully “ naice ” and the very “naicest.” It is
also alleged that Corporal Adair was photo’d by
the Ductless while he was in England, her Grace
—artful little dear “ wishing to have a picture
to show what an Australian soldier is like.”
Naturally the corporal is tall, and slim, and unut-
terably lovely, and tailored with exquisite care,
and not a bit like the raw Australian warrior.
However, he is quite in accordance with a woman’s
ideal son of ina’s.
Scribe : Argus Editor Willoughby oegan
as a reporter on the Age, like the Brittons, and so
many others, when the coveted step was from
Elizabeth to Coll ins-street. On the Argus he be-
came correspondent in Maoriland, during the war.
Then he left to join the original Hansard staff,
with Hadley and Robinson, the present Vic.
Assembly clerk, all three being shed by the Argus,
which thereupon stripped the Age treeof its plums.
Willoughby left Hansard, handing his billet to
Gilbert, who yielded it to Willoughby’s brother-
in-law, Wheeler. He married one Miss Wil-
loughby, and Aliek Britton the other. Willoughby,
Somerton and Persse launched Melb. Daily
Telegraph, where Willoughby appeared, perhaps,
to the greatest advantage as a writer, and was not
replaced in any measure till Mr. Fitchett’s ap-
pearance, after many years. Willoughby left the
Telegraph to return to the Argus, as head of the
staff, where he remained for some years, till he
became a leader-writer, pure and simple. Even
Willoughby is by no means a satisfied man, for he
has an unjournalistic craving to personally
dabble in public affairs. He and Walter Cooper,
the dramatist, wrote the most beautiful shorthand
I have ever seen. Both Pitmanites. They were
slow and yet rapid, a paradox which stenographers
will understand. This was through their expert-
ness in phraseograms.

H. : “The highly -.cultured Mr. Check
Hong Cheong, who has lectured all over England,
is the presiding genius of the C. of E. Chinese
Mission, Little Bourke-street, Melb. But for his
prim black jumper, he would be just like Thorn-
ton’s Private Secretary, wearing the same little
black billycock. When a stranger comes to the
door, Mr. Cheong’s gold spectacles appear, with
his double row of perfect ivories on the smile
childlike and bland, and the mild question, ‘Are
you requiring anything?’ ‘Oh dear, no!’ and
Paul Pry departs awkwardly, while the loud
susurrus is heard within of young ladies heckling
their individual Chinamen—a crowd of nice,
genteel ones, not the handful of dismal, vulgar
Chows who whine Celestial versions of Wesley’s

Pioneer squatter Ben Richards, recently
demised at Richmond (N.S.W.), was an enormous
exporter of meat, his figure sometimes reaching
500,000 mutton carcases per annum. His immense
cattle sales were principally to the local trade.
The huge Riverstone meat-works are the old man’s
most appropriate monument.
Anarquia : If the blowing-up of the U.S.
Maine was done by a torpedo there is something
to be learned from the incident. The torpedo
represents the bowling of warfare, and it is de-
cidedly beating the batting. What is needed, for
the benefit of the world, is a good, healthy terror
on the part of navy and army men as to the new
chemical agencies which abolish bravery. Yes,
torpedo Brennan and his rivals are the grand
bowlers in the cause of humanity. Let the Arch-
bishop of Canterbury, who blesses the launch of
ironclads, have a prayer at the torpedo-factory:
“ Lord, wc thank Thee for this lovely little engine,
the true evangel and messenger of peace.”
Dear Bulletin.— Re Mr. A. B. Kellie’s
observation, in his Xmas Bulletin yarn (“ What
the White Pig said to Leila ”), “ True, she had
been brutally assaulted as a child, on the labor-
schooner,” let me say that no children are carried
upon Q. labor-vessels. Only three classes of
islanders are brought—recruits, boat’s crews, and
interpreters. The recruits, men and women, being
for fieldwork, require to be well-developed physi-
cally, and not apparently under 16 years old. A
mother, with young family, would be an anomaly ;
a child solus would be a greater one. No skipper
would be verdant enough to ship children, with
the cocksurity of having to return them next trip,
at owner’s expense. The trail of the missionary
is over that “brutal assault.” Assault upon
children is not the vioe of the sailorman; it is
mostly an amenity of large cities. E. J. Brady
was right in—
You get the virgins, brothers;
We get, God wot, the others;
Your castoffs come to us!
—Harry Monok.

to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-

a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Aboriginalities.
A Grippsland bush-fire incident. As Swing-
ler of Red Jacket, near Woods’ Point, was riding
alone he got thrown from his horse, fracturing
several ribs in the fall. He lay insensible all night.
The horse stayed beside him all the time, and, so
he stat is, brought him to his senses in the morning
by “stroking him with its hoof.” Then he
managed to mount and ride home. Macaulay’s
verse goes—
But, like a graven image,
Black Aust-er kept his place,
And ever wistfully he looked
Into his master’s face.
The probably grass-fed moke of Mr. Swingler
surpassed in fidelity the horse of Herminius.
Plain prose is often more poetical than poetry.
* * » *
F.C.K. : When I was kicking about New
England in ’93, somewhere between Bundana and
Bendemere I dropped across a grizzled old chap
riding a blood mare, with foal at foot, and engaged
to put in a couple of weeks with him before shear-
ing. After two days’ travelling we reached his
home—a mere gunyah on a solitary mountain-
side, and 3 acres of land, with nothing on it but
three unbroken colts. Here he had dwelt alone
for 14 years, living entirely upon salt-junk and
damper, with never so much as a potato, and
smoking an average of 24 pipes per diem. His
only cooking utensil was an iron pot, and he had
no clothes but one pair of trousers and two shirts.
He_ could neither read nor write, and had no
visitors—yet he was serenely happy. What is
happiness, after all ?
* • • •
Licensed Surveyor: “ One morning, a
couple of weeks ago, I saw a brown snake sunning
himself outside the kitchen, and chopped him in
two with a spade. Stooped down to pick up the
tail end, and—picked up both ends ! The head,
with only 9in. of body attached, moved a distance
of fully 12in., and seized me by the forefinger.
You see, he was cut clean through—with only a
broken back he would have been more helpless.
The finger immediately began to discolor, so, after
tying a string tightly round it, I scarified it with
a razor, and sucked it all the way into Murwillum-
bah, where the doctor fixed me up with strychnine,
and, after a couple of hours or so, said there was
no further danger. Felt little or no ill effects ;
but the next time I kill a snake with a spade, I’ll
kill him ‘ wid a stick. ’ Pretty rough, after 17
years of ‘ hairbreadth ’scapes ’ in the swamps and
jungles of the Tweed River, to be bitten almost
on my own hearthstone. P.S.—What about
M'Cooey and his assertion that jackasses never
kill snakes ? Will Mr. M'Cooey please note this
instance.”
* * * *
At the wedding of an uncivilised monster
of a bushman at Mickabidgee, the best man, fol-
lowing a good old custom, kissed the newly-made
bride. He had no sooner done so, however, than
he got a terrific “ stoush ” on the jaw from the
enraged bridegroom, who wanted to know what
the , etc., he meant by “ muggin’ me old
woman ? ” The best man retaliated, a ring was
at once formed by the bridal party, and a gory
battle ensued, with the whole local population as
referee and bottle-holder, while the blushing
bride stood by and advised her hubby to “ chaw
’im up.” After an exceedingly “ willing ” go, the
bridegroom knocked his man and, receiving
no response to his offer “ to eat any six of the
crowd, he spat out half a dozen teeth, straight-
ened his battered nose, and departed for the wed-
ding-breakfast with his pansy-blossom.
* • * *
A.E.P. : In ’94, at Binaway on the Castle-
reagh, I saw an old whaler—must have been near-
ing 60—not an ounce of flesh on him, skin like
tanned hide, with a horse-collar swag 851 b. weight.
Reaching Binaway from Coonabarabran, 21 miles,
he had the usual (“ gingerbeer,” I suppose), and
straightway became a “corpse.” After some
hours of oblivion he concluded to proceed. Didn’t
he “wrastle” with that “bluey”! It downed
him many times, but at last he succeeded and
shambled down the track. A gaunt cattle-pup
accompanied him, and performed on the boys who
dared to interfere with the veteran. Eighty-five
pounds—and 40 miles to Coolah. Think of it,
straw-hat brigade!
* * * *
Dear B.—Out west, where there is no town-
ship and no railway—nothing but a wire-fence
straddling across the plain on the road to eternity
—most of the events which stir the scum on the
lamentable green pool of existence are something
about a horse, or else something about a dog. If
something didn’t occasionally happen about a dog
Heaven only knows how it would be possible to
bear up against the limitless monotony of the
west. On one western station where I put in six
months I was just saved from madness by a dog
having convulsions. It was Old Swag, the owner’s
third son’s fifth yellow mongrel—the said youth
had eight of them in all —and he convulsed on the
front verandah on a hot, dank day, when the
heavens and the earth were a yawning emptiness,
and man was a dejected speck perishing in a dust-
storm. The alarmed youth called for assistance,
and the family swarmed out, also the red-
headed tutor, and the book-keeper, and the lady-
help. The latter, after waving her kid-gloved hands
despairingly in the air, proceeded to administer
tepid salt and water out of a whisky bottle. This
seemed to rouse the whole herd from a deadly
apathy and they were seized with a laudable
desire to assist. The red-headed tutor grasped
Swag by the tail and swung him round and round
with startling enthusiasm. When released in
mid-air Swag struck the opposite fence and fell
in a second fit. Bert availed himself of this
favorable opportunity to rush in and saw off one
of the sufferer’s ears with a rusty butcher’s knife.
Meanwhile Charley had prepared a huge plug of
tobacco by a weary process of mastication. It
having been sufficiently chewed, he deposited it
in his hand and proceeded to thrust it down old
Swag’s throat. Ted now emerged from the
kitchen, scarlet and tearful, bearing a large frying-
pan filled with hot fat, and poured it into old
Swag. Then Jack thought of castor-oil (“ knoohe’d
heard of it somewhere, anyways it wouldn’t do
him no harm ”). Old Swag accordingly received
half a bottle. Then the family, with the excep-
tion of Ted, retired inside. Ted, left to his own
resources, fervently administered two more pans
of melted fat, and remained with Swag till sun-
down. Next day, after lengthily discussing the
matter with a friend, he came home and “ wished
to goodness he’d known about camphor before.”
Two days later Old Swag, in the rudest health,
fought another dog to a finish under the house. It
is wonderful how the most ordinary-looking bush
dog can swallow twice his own weight of hot fat,
plug tobacco and castor-oil and thrive upon it.—
Mulga.

Every three months or so a certain com-
fortably-circumstanced individual in N.Q. takes
it into his head to hump a bluey from one end of
a certain railway-line to the other. He generally
manages to get about half-way before heat, flies,
and bad tucker convince him that it is advisable
to train back to his starting-point. But, after
taking his three months to recover, he has another
“ go ” at the same old impossible tramp.
* * * *
K. : The crying swaggie is the latest. Is
always to be found swag-carrying a few miles out
of town. I met him the other day. In a voice of
sorrow he pitched his little tale of woe, while tears
ran down his dirty face. I was touched, as was
my pocket. A few days after I met him again in
a pub.—same tale, same voice and tears. When
he spotted me he cleared. ' “ That man,” said the
landlord, “is the greatest artist on the road,
makes plenty of money, and can cry when he likes.
Makes a little pile out of the women % No woman
ever refuses help to a crying man.”

*•* * *
Dingoes of the East and West of N.S.W.
(writes “ Werrimee ”) differ much in taste. The
former is an indiscriminating glutton. Anything
in the form of flesh, bar bandicoot, goes down
with him, the condition of it being no considera-
tion. I saw one draw a piece of putrid bullock-
hide away from a heap of maggots with his paw,
take one end in his mouth, shake it, and then
“scoff ”it with gusto. The Western dingo is more
epicurean, and wouldn’t insult his stomach with
such things. Prefers to kill his own meat, but, if
Eushed, will pick up anything dainty without
eing over-inquisitive. He strongly objects to
anything that has been mauled about, and, if he
is to take a bait, it must have no human aroma
about it. In sheep - country, he rounds up a
flock about sundown, singles out a fat one, and
kills it for supper. In the morning, he will trot
disdainfully past that carcase, round up the flock
again, and kill a fresh one for breakfast. A sheep
a meal would be costly enough ; but the brute
mostly rips a dozen or so before making a kill,
because he likes some lively sport first to give
him an appetite. In capturing a calf, the dingo
shows good strategy. He has half-a-dozen mates
with him. These hide in different places in the
grass while the chief actor trots round and round
the cow, nipping the calf at every opportunity ;
and, when the calf darts away a bit after a bite on
the heel, the nearest dog instantly cuts it off from
the cow, and the whole pack fall upon and kill it.
One dog will kill a sheep ; but he’ll never tackle a
calf single-handed, unless he finds it out of reach
of its mother.

The toniest nugget ever found on Shell-
back was down in Bill Jones’ claim. Bill and his
mate, Rocky George, were stoping out one after-
noon during the wild sixties, when Rocky saw the
shaft suddenly darken. “ Come here, Bill !
Darn’d if Rainbow Sal hasn’t fallen down the
shaft! ” “It’s all right, boys, I thought youd be
dry, so I fetched the bottle,” whispered Sal, lying
in a heap at the bottom. She had fallen 00 feet
unhurt —and the bottle was unbroken ! It was in
the day of crinolines, and hers had opened out d
la parachute.

Writes “ 5.0.8.” re a recent discussion :
The term “tpe-ragger” was first used by the English
railway navvies, known as “Pinchers” or “thick legs,”
who slaved under Heard, Peto, and Brassey, &c. The art
of stocking-weaving wasn’t perfected in those days ; and,
as hand-knitted stockings were not abundant with the
navvies, they wrapped their feet in pieces of old woollen
cloth before putting on their high-lows or watertights,
also swathed their ankles and calves, hence “ thick
legs.” Peto and Brassey introduced the English navvy to
Australia, and he brought his “toe-rags” or “Prince
Alberts” with him, and carried the phrase into the coun-
try as the railway extended. Among the navvies them-
selves it came to be applied to any waster who only got
a job when better men could not be obtained.

That cultivated admirer of curves and out-
lines, the King of the Belgians, has arrived at an
age when he can properly appreciate the bearing
of art upon female beauty. The poetry of er -
stays has touched His Majesty’s royal soul, seem-
ingly. M. Paul Dutoict, who lately achieved a
Grand Prix award at the Brussels Exhibition, has
now been appointed a chevalier of the Legion in
honor of his P.D. corsets. The creator of the
P.D. is the first man in the world to get a decora-
tion for staymaking, and the Belgian monarch is
the first occupant of a throne to admit that the
world is governed by appearances. The majesty
which doth hedge a king is, when you come to
think about it, very much in the nature of P.D.
corsets. #

WATSON’S WHISKY.
“ SURPLUS.”
The term “ surplus ” is applied, in life insuranoe
affairs, to the balance, ascertained periodically,
between the assets and the liabilities. Out of this
balance or surplus, distributions of profits are
made to the policy-holders entitled to participate.
The natural tendency of distribution is to dimmish
the surplus for the moment; but in the case of a
prosperous company, the decrease is speedily more
than covered by accessions of profit from various
sources. It is clear, however, that the more
liberal the periodical distributions of profits, the
more gradual will be the growth of the surplus.
Conversely, if a life insurance company withho ds
from its policy-holders the full benefit of the pro-
portion of profits immediately available, and
prefers to accumulate in the interest of future
participants, it follows that the surplus will in-
crease more rapidly in proportion. Of course it
is easy to keep in hand more than the future will
require under any possible circumstances ; and
when that course is followed, injustice to present
participants is inevitable.
Now The Mutual Life of New Yoke, while
careful’ to make abundant reserves for future
bonuses, has always been equally careful to dis-
tribute to its policy-holders the full immediate
benefits which they are entitled to claim. Ihe
Company has never sacrificed the interests of
policy-holders entitled to immediate participation,
to the formation of an imposing but empirical
surplus, never likely to be required even in the
remotest future. The surplus shown by The
Mutual Life at the end of 1896 was ,£0,105,444.
It is a steadily increasing surplus, which, need-
less to say, is more than ample for all the
demands for which it has been created.
But, supposing the Company had, throughout
its history, acted on the principle which
has been adopted in, at least, one prominen
instance—of reserving a large proportion of the
surplus at every distribution, and, in addition,
reducing the surrender-values allowed for discon-
tinued policies, what does the reader suppose
would have been the amount of The Mutual s
surplus in 1896 ? Not six millions, but upwards of
U millions sterling ! This is not guesswork. Ihe
figures are based upon the official report issued by
the Insurance Department of the State of JNeiv
York, which gives annually the exact record of
every company doing business in that State.
Since its organisation, The Mutual Life has paid
in bonuses and surrender values to its policy-holders
£44 445 244, which is more than twice the amount
paid for’similar items by the next largest company.
Observe, then, what a magnificent surplus The
Mutual could have exhibited to-day, had an
illiberal policy been pursued in relation to the
assured—a surplus exceeding that of any other
office by more than 150 per cent. But, fortunately,
the Company preferred to sacrifice a mere useless
and vainglorious display to the solid interests of
the policy-holders. Who shall say that, in taking
thiscourse, the Mutual Life of New York has
not chosen the better part? and that, in so doing
it has not well earned the high reputation which
it has attained in every part of the world as the
Policy-holders’ C 0.,” “the Co. which does the most
good ? ” The intelligent reader will not allow him-
self to be misled by the parading of sums of no
use to the present generation, and in excess ot
any possible needs of the generations to come.

The newest “ function ” which the N.S.W
• Goyernor is called upon to perform is to give the
ha PPy dispatch to sharks caught in Sydney
Harbor. The shark is chained up by the .tail, and
the Gov. is sent for and “ does the rest ” —while
the shark has the sweet satisfaction of dying at
the hands of the Nicest people and in the nicest
way. The Bulletin is not aware that his Ex.
has yet been called upon to “ open ’’ any shark so
captured and killed.

' • >,92S per, ‘ ® ob1 ’ and Sllver Mining Company) in portions No. 802 W. and 803 W. (each containing 80 acres,
S n D ID in ICO acres), County of Bentinck, Parish of Stanthorpe, held by registered lease under the provisions of
the Real Property Acts of 1861 and 1877," from Frederick Robert White, the registered proprietor thereof and
which lease is for the term of 21 years from the 30th day of October, 1896, and gives the lessee a right of renewal for
a further period of 10 years, together with all shafts, buildings, tools, &c., thereon. The principal conditions of the
lease are . That Mr. White is to receive, by way of rent or royalty, one-quarter of the net profits of working the
properties comprised in the said lease, but which rent or royalty is to be reduced to one-sixth on the expenditure of
a sum,' within three years, of £40,000 on the properties comprised in the said lease, which reduction is to have
relation back to the commencement of the lease. The agreement for sale provides (inter alia) that the Vendors shall
obtain, if this Company should require it, an independent lease of the said portions from the registered proprietor,
containing similar covenants with such modifications as are applicable in respect to the difference in the property
comprised or to be comprised in the existing and new lease respectively.

The Mine has been actively at work for the last 12 months, a number of shafts sunk on the line of lode exposing
very large bodies of ore of very high values. A reverberatory furnace has also been erected, and about £3OOO worth
capital, so acquire the latest kind of furnaces and machinery.
n A PP e "defl are th ® reports of Mr. Q. H. Irvine, Metallurgist, of Brisbane, the Mining Commissioner, and Mr. W.
U. Koilda, the Managing Director. Also list of all assays of ore and matte made by Mr. Robert Mar (late Govern-
ment Analyst). As, however, the mine is situated only 20 miles from the Stanthorpe Railway Station, and only one
® J ourne y it can easily be inspected by anyone desirous of doing: so.
u u?,. n . ew Company to take over the two blocks as before mentioned, with all buildings, tools, machinery, &0., and
all liabilities, which vvill probably amount to £5OO, but will certainly not exceed £lOOO.
At any future time the property can be subdivided into four blocks of 40 acres each, and formed into four
separate companies, as 40 acres with the large quantities of ore contained therein is quite large enough for any one
company to work. 6 * 1
As the present proprietors intend to take up a good nnmber of the Contributing Shares, early application is
necessary. The allotment will be made according to priority of application.
The Provisional Directors reserve the right to declare the Company floated when in their opinion a sufficient
number of shares have been applied for.
Should the Company not be floated the application money will he returned in full.
Report of 4th February, 1898, by G. H. Irvine, Esq. (late Manager Mt. Morgan G.M. Co.)
The Directors, The Pikedale Copper, G., and S. Co., Ltd., Citt.
Dear Sirs,—
Acting under your instructions, I visited and inspected your copper and silver property (freehold) situated on
Pikedale Station, 21 miles W.S.W. from Stanthorpe. '
•i he '° de > wh j Cb runs N-NfW., with a slight dip to the W., has been exposed for a distance of fully a quarter of a
mile bv shafts and eosteens. These have proved the width of the lode to vary from 2ft. to 7ft., oarrving good copper
and silver contents. ’ J 6 6
Shaft C has been sunk at the extreme south, Shaft B 60ft. N. on the oourse of the lode, and Shaft A 92ft.
N. from B.
These have been sunk to a depth of 20,15, and 38ft. respectively, with an average width of 7ft., and from whioh
good ore was obtained, averaging 8 per cent, copper and 30oz. silver per ton.
„ No. l Shaft'is 85ft. N. of A., and has been sunk to a depth of 52ft. The ore body between walls has varied from
5 to 10ft. wide.
down a level S. was put in 38ft. and stoped to surface. At the same level N. was connected with No. 2
Shaft (56ft.), and stoped to surface.
From these stopes a large quantity of good ore has been obtained, varying from 2 per cent, copper and 20oz. of
silver to 13 per cent, copper and 90oz. silver, the average being 7 per cent, copper and 350 z. silver per ton.
On t^he face of the 52ft. level, going S., there is a fine chute of ore 4ft. 6in. wide, averaging 7 per cent, copper and
30oz. silver per ton.
On the N. end of this level the formation is fully 10ft. wide, carrying a chute of fair ore Sft. wide, varying from
2 7 per cent, copper and 14oz. silver to s’B per cent, copper and 420 z silver.
u N °'^u Sha£t , 1S 66 c ft ' N ' of N°; 1 > and , is sunk to a depth of 80ft., and averages 3ft. to 9ft. in width between the
walls. The quality of the ore during the sink varied from 2 per cent, copper and 17oz. silver to 7'3 per cent, copper
and 60oz. silver per ton. "
•i °P® n 'nj< drives N. and S. there is a good chute of ore, averaging 6 per cent, copper and 450 z.
silver, although the present faces, which are in 20ft. and 18ft. respectively, do not look so well, but will probably
improve on driving. In the S., the ore body measures 3ft. 6in., averaging 3.4 per oent. copper and 12oz. silver.
No. 6 Shaft is sunk in disturbed country, about 900 ft. N. from No. 2. The reason of this disturbance is a
fault takes place in the proximity of No. 4 Shaft, and has thrown the lode about a chain to the west.
Abe shaft (No. 5) has been sunk 52ft., carrying good ore, varying from U per cent, copper and lOoz. silver
to 16 per cent, copper and 118 oz. silver.
At 27 ft. down, adrive has been put in S. 40ft., and crosscuts E. and W. 14ft. and 16ft. respectively, without
meeting the walls. These have exposed a large body of gossany ore, averaging about 4 per cent, copper and 16oz.
j-m SM w LTIN -n'l NI) Fl ( ux ® 3 ;— The character of the ore at present exposed does not lead me to expect that any
difficulty will be met with in producing a good matte. In fact, your ores are nearly self-fluxing, with the excep-
tion of a small addition of limestone, which you have already at hand in quantity.
.... , j ... quantity.
At the same time, before considering and deciding the most economic mode of treatment, I would strongly
advocate sinking to a further depth of 100 ft. from the bottom of No. 2 Shaft and putting in the necessary drives,
to prove the quantity and nature of your ore at that depth.
In reviewing the state of affairs regarding the Pikedale Copper and Silver property, the good grade ore,
and .their occurrence in considerable quantities, so far as can be judged from the present workings, I am of
opinion that it warrants the further expenditure of a considerable sum for development as suggested. When this has
been accomplished, and the nature and body of ore exposed at this depth, it will be then time enough for you to
consider the best and most economic means for the treatment of same.
Mining Commissioner’s Report.
PIKEDALE SILVER MINE.
In my last report I referred to this mine as just opened and in its infancy, but since then it has been considerably
developed, and the mineral is similar to that found at the Silver Spur. It is known as the Pikedale Copper, Gold
and Sliver Mining Company, and is a freehold on the Pikedale Station, 20 miles from Stanthorpe. Active work was
P^°P er *'y i twelve months ago, and various shafts, five in number, have been sunk, varying in
■ P 40 £e „ e , fc / 0n J the lode the two extreme shafts are a quarter of a mile apart. The ore exposed varies
m width from sft. to 10ft., and averages 320 z. of silver per ton, and copper 5.72 per cent. A reverberatory furnace
was erected to experiment on the ore, but was not found to be successful, and the Company now contemplate
increasing the capital m order to more fully develop" the mine, further experiment on the ore treatment, and obtain
the best metallurgical advice thereon before deciding on any particular method of treatment. Meanwhile, the work
or? i i pending the completion of the newly-proposed financial arrangements. I may mention that something like
850 tons of ore has been put through the furnace, and 100 tons of matte obtained of an estimated value of £4 2s. 6d. on
the ton of ore. This is considered very satisfactory, especially when the mechanical troubles are taken into considera-
tion, with respeot to the constant stopping of the smelting for repairs owing to defective bricks, and then again the
estimated loss in calcining before smelting is calculated at 20 per cent. This loss will be obviated should the ore be
found amenable to the Pyritic Smelting process, such as is used at the Mount Lyell Mine in Tasmania, and so far as
the'experiments have gone they tend to prove that the above process will be adopted.
Manager s Report of July 19th, 1897.
Summary of work done from the commencement (January last) to date.
Oosteening work was carried on for the first two or three weeks, which exposed the lode in various places along
the surface one, varying from two feet to seven feet wide for a quarter of a mile in length. Although we have a length
u* U »i otle m u reef, operations have been necessarily confined to one fourth of this length, because of the
short time we have been working, besides it being advisable to concentrate the labor in order to get some stoping
• op u er l ’ doubt, judging from the strong character of the lode, but what the ore will live through-
\ 0 j • , „ The most southern shaft (known as B shaft) is in about the centre of the property (comprising
160 acres), and is fully a quarter of a mile distant from the most northern shaft (No. 5), which is now down over 40ft.
There are intervening shafts down on the lode from 50 to 70ft. deep, and although the lode on the surface was only
three feet thick in places, yet the deeper we sink the wider the ore becomes.
® shaft is 16ft. deep ; lode 7ft. wide, of good value. No. 1 Bhaft which is two chains, Nof B shaft is down over
50ft., and carries 10ft. of clean ore in the bottom, and still widening; the first stope we took out over the 50ft.
averaged fully 10ft. wide.
No. 2 shaft 90ft., north of No. 1, is down 70ft., and has averaged from 3ft. in width to 9ft., the present width in
the bottom being Sft. between walls. These two shafts are still being sunk by two shifts of men and horse whip.
No. 6 shaft, at the north end of workings, we are sinking in the lode without any walls ; the ore body must be of
immense width here, as at the 27ft. depth (sulphide level) a drive w'as put in South about 40ft., and crosscuts E. and
W. put in for 14ft. ana loft, each, without meeting any walls. I am convinced there are vast deposits of ore at this
end of the mine, therefore stored prospecting at the shallow level and resnnied sinking the s.haft, which is now
down 40 feet; am still sinking. You will notice that at the 27ft. level we have gone through ore 30ft. wi
without meeting either wall, the material assaying in bulk 16ozs. silver per ton, a little gold, and 4.0 per
rw>» f.n tVi o oho ft t tin nro to onlnhiilaa n •« 1.... ...1 • 1 , n,, ~ . r .
ide (all oxide)
cent, of cop-
per. In the shaft the ore is sulphides, and is improving in value as sunk upon. The assays varying from the 27ft.
level to the 40ft., from 27 to 31oz. silver and 5.0 per cent, to 7.0 per cent, copper ; nothing iike a wall has been seen in
this shaft since the 20ft. depth was passed, one wall going E. and the other VV.
All the ore raised and exposed at the Southern portion of our workings (includes B, A, and Nos. 1 and 2 shafts)
are sulphides (even to the surface), and will average in width nine feet (with every appearance of still further widen-
ing as depth is attained), the value in bulk being over 30oz. silver per ton, a little gold, and 6 per cent, to 7 per cent,
copper.
i umace, uui, 1 would like to araw your attention to tne tact
“that the plant is not near large enough to deal with the immense quantities of ore contained in the mine,” and I
would earnestly advise you to take the necessary steps to increase the capital of the Company, so that the mine
development may be pushed ahead and sufficient and suitable furnaces erected as such a mine as this assuredly
warrants.
At present we are smelting about 70 tons per week, including fluxes, but arrangements should be made to deal

A SPLENDID PREPARATION FOR STRENGTHENING
THE NERVES AND HEART.
ACTION ON THE HEART.—Professor Heekel states
that Kola, by the Caffeine and Theobromine which it con-
tains, is a tonic of the heart, whose pulsation it accele-
rates, while it increases its power and regulates its con-
tractions. Professor Heekel further points out that the
principle called Kolaine contained in Kola prevents rapid
waste of the tissues during fatiguing exertions, while the
Caffeine stimulates the muscles.
PNEUMONIA.—Worry kills businessmen. Heavy losses
and hard times make them depressed in spirits. The brain
and body become weak ; unknown to them they are de-
bilitated. It is then that the least chill strikes them, and
pneumonia carries them off. To fortify the system and
make the spirits buoyant, nothing can equal Fisher and
Co’s famous Kola Nut Tonic. It is the most remarkable
invigorator and sustainer known.
DIARRHCEA.—Dr. Durian and Dr. Cunio have both re-
ported cures in rebellious diarrhoea under the use of Kola.
A recent issue of the Planters’ Monthly contains a
description of this remarkable Nut and its uses:—“Kola
has the property of staying hunger, whilst supporting the
struggle for existence. If a supply of nuts had been in-
cluded in Mr. Stanley’s outfit, his men would not have
suffered so terribly for want of animal food. As might be
expected, the value of Kola increases as it makes its way
into the interior of Africa, and some of the tribes furthest
removed from the sea pay for the dry powder an equal
weight of gold-dust. In a despatch from Mr. George
Alexander Stevens, Her Majesty’s consul at Bahia, he calls
attention to the great powers of endurance in lifting
heavy loads and transporting them to long distance in the
tropical climate, attributed to the free use of Kola Bean.
An old African, after biting a piece of the magic bean,
lifted 179 English pounds weight on to his head and
carried the same for a distance of four miles.”
“ Still another merit is named, however, which will in-
terest the temperance party. The negroes who chew
Kola Nut have no desire to drink rum, and hence save
money and retain strength.”
Dr. LEON ERNEST MONNET, Paris, reportsl
gave a strong infusion of Kola Nut to a railroad track-
layer, who was suffering from ‘blue devils’ (hypochon-
dria) extremely, and within two hours he felt like going
to his hard duties, and laughed and talked.”
This TONIC acts with the most remarkable
rapidity in restoring a weakened Stomach, Im-
paired Digestion, Torpid Liver, Debility, and in
Reviving the Brain from the effects of Overwork
and Fatigue. It Stimulates and strengthens the
entire Muscular System, acting like magic, and
rouses into Vigor and Health the whole Physical
Energy of the Human Economy. To enable one
to enjoy a meal, a dose taken an hour or so before
immensely sharpens the appetite. With Soda
Water, before breakfast, it is the most beneficial
drink in the world. It has a most marvellous
effect in Checking the Craving for Alcohol, in
many cases haying restored Inebriates to their
Normal Conditions of Life.

“ Nevermind ! he is a long way off and
bedriddei), anyhow.”
The missionary is a person who ringbarks
savage races in the interests of the trader.
The Victorian House of 48 Rich Land-
lords always starts proceedings with the Lord’s
prayer. “Some of the members are beginning to
know it now,” says Alfred Deakin.
Vic. : “ Vic. Government has ukased that
no more grocers’ grog-licenses are to be issued.
At the recent Melb. licensed-victuallers’ picnic
there was serious talk about an onslaught on this
iniquity.
In re the S.A. Conventionist views on
deadlocks and two Chambers, an S.A. Governor—
Macdonnell—of nearly 40 years ago contended that
the only way S.A. could be safe from deadlocks
was to have only one Chamber.
There is a movement towards the re-con-
solidation of the miners’ associations of N.S.W.,
Vic., Tas., and W. A. Atone time they formed
a complete organisation, but disruptions, due
chiefly to political differences, occurred some years
ago.
Barton’s replies to interjections in Con-
vention are often brutally rough. Thus, Trenwith
pointed out, in the course of a speech by Barton,
that two clauses under discussion “ both dealt
with procedure.” Barton: “And a man and a
goat are both beasts, but it does not follow that
they are the same thing.” Barton flies to zoology
in supreme moments. On a former occasion,
speaking of his own temper, he quoted Shakes-
peare’s passa-e, commencing, “O, Cassius, you
are yoked with a lainb.”
QUID PRO QUO.
SQUATTER : “It's time you put that fence, up, Macpherson; I’m about tired of impound-
ing your cattle.”
SELECTOR : “ W eel — it’s no tired I am o’ yairdin’ yours."
Noticeable that Gold-cure Caulfield has
got nearly every get-atable N.S.W. politician to
give nominal countenance to his temperance
lecture, with the exception of three or four on
whom the cold blast of public opinion has been
blowing lately. Caulfield knows his book by
heart.
Victor : “The Siberian railway will bring
Australia seven days nearer London. Does any-
one suppose the clock of the world can be put
lack, so as to prevent Australia from taking
advantage of that? And scan the illimitable
vistas of trade ! ”
To-day's bold advocates for the absolute
equitableness of an income-tax—Ministers Brun-
ker Gould, Young, Carruthers, Garrard, and
Sydney Smith—all voted against G. R. Dibbs’
Income Tax Bill of 1893. Political opportunism
ratally blighted their sense of equity then.
The Victorian Government may shortly
propose to finance the new policy of “ reproduc-
tive expenditure ” with a loan of 2 or 3 millions—
3, probably. Premier George Turner has, in a
manner of speaking, put his hat on the back of his
head, and made up his legal mind to “go it.
dammy !
Is there no bright, live, young Australian
human man in N.S. Wales who can go up and
cook that cold-blooded political spider, Just-
ice Minister Gould at the forthcoming general
election ? that icy, pious, pulseless, fleshless
prosy administrator of Botany Bay justice accord-
ing to the la ws of Red Tape.
Attorney - General Isaacs announces that
Vic. Govt, will make another effort to pass the
Female Franchise Bill next session. The last
effort was wrecked in the House of 48 Rich Land-
lords, where most of the good intentions of the Vic.
Government are broken up .small, to make mac-
adam for the roadways of perdition.
Premier Nelson intimated on his way to
Melbourne to the Council of Premiers that if the
federal Convention pays any serious attention to
the separate claims of Central and Northern
Queensland to be heard at the Convention,
Queensland, that is his Queensland, will turn her
back on the whole scheme of Federal Union.
The alien vote for Divisional Boards in
N.Q. is becoming a matter for serious considera-
tion. There are districts wherein the Chow vote
turns the scale at every board election now.
European landlords manipulate their yellow and
piebald tenants to oust members thev don’t like.
They’ll find very shortly the same vote used to
oust themselves.
