Dear Bulletin. —ThePolice Commissioner
at Wellington (M.L.) recently stated that there
were so many cases of immorality, seduction and
heartless abandonment charged against the police
that he would not go into them for the sake of the
unfortunate women who would be thereby
exposed. He would confine himself to the
charges dealing with incapacity, assaults, em-
bezzlement, drunkenness and thieving. In many
cases men who were convicted of these offences
were reinstated by political influence. What a
lovely police force we have !— Maori.
Seeing that Sydney Truth practically
accused Lands-Minister Carruthers of (inter alia )
political dishonesty ; and that, although backed
to a remarkable degree by the whole force of the
Crown Law authorities, Carruthers could not suc-
ceed in convincing a jury that Editor Norton
should be punished for criminal libel ; and seeing,
also, that it is now definitely announced that Car-
ruthers (or the Crown Law Office) isn't going to
try further to convince a jury, etc., but is going
to sit down under the stigma ; —question is, What
is Carruthers going to do about it politically ?
MELTING THE TAR.
SCENE : Quartet-deck of H.M.S. Furoso. Afternoon
dance well under weigh. Society girl vainly try-
ing to make First Lieutenant talk.
SHE : “I suppose you adore your delightful profes-
sion?"
1ST. LIEUT. (all duty and bashfulness): “Well, yes.
I am very fond of it.”
SHE (still hopeful): “And do you like a storm?”
1ST. LIEUT. (as before): “No."
SHE (despairing but determined): “Ah, then you
must simply worship this divine day? Doesn't
it make you feel that we should make use of
every hour of it? ”
1ST. LIEUT. (brightening up on a congenial topic):
“ That’s just what I told the skipper this [?]
ing. I said, ‘ Here we are, sir, with a ripping
day for scrubbing hammocks and washing
clothes, and what are we doing with it? Why,
just dirtying the decks and’ (left talking).
Among Premier Forrest’s crowd of tame
disciples from W. A. the most amusing joke is A.
11. Henning, one-time an Adelaide lawyer. Hen-
ning—brother to the Black Flag magnate—is one
of Leg. Councillors for Coolgardie, and he
got pushed into Forrest’s Federal team at the last
moment, to fill a gap. From his first appearance
as a make-weight at the Melb. Convention up to
time of writing, Henning hasn’t uttered a word —
not even a mild “ heah, heah.”
Queensland Govt, has time and again
reiterated that its Kanaka-recruiting agents are
most capable and reliable men bent on doing their
duty properly. The other day a ship reached
Queensland with 14 “boys” on board, not one of
whom was over 13 years of age ! Apparently, the
men are all used up on the islands, and the black-
birders have to fall back on children. Medical
officers at Maryborough and Bundaberg inspected
these babes, and promptly condemned the sample
as being unfit even for cane-field manure. Now,
where was that recruiting agent, when boys were
shipped, and who was lie and is he to go recruiting
again ?
The clause which now most wants to be
inserted in the Federal Constitution is one pro-
viding that any powers which the Convention has
forgotten to define shall be assumed to belong to
the Commonwealth, and not to the States. The
biggest mistake of all—the ghastly blunder which
has pervaded the entire proceedings—is this : The
Constitution provides that certain defined powers
shall belong to the Commonwealth, while all other
powers, defined or undefined, rest with the States.
It should have been the other way about; the
Constitution Act should simply have provided
that certain powers shall be left to the States, and
that all other authority shall belong to the
Federal Government. In other words, the States
now get the benefit of the doubt, whereas the
Commonwealth should have it.
Germany’s most important ac-
quisition in China is the undying
hatred of Russia for obstructing the
southward march of that rapacious
power towards the fertile and popu-
lous Middle Kingdom.
Neat slab of criticism from
Maryborough (Q.) paper :
Tozer deserves to be bracketed with
Napoleon—“ extreme in all things.” From
private kindness to public harshness ; from
private honesty to public truth and utterly
regardless of consequences at that; from
assumed modesty to .unbridled effrontery,
he has been “ extreme in the extreme.”
Tasmanian Premier Braddon
says that there is no choice of suit-
able men in Australia from whom to
appoint State Governors! What 1—
when there are scores and scores of
hungry and homeless haw-haw remit-
tance men walking the streets on
their nppahs! Any one of these
would fill the billet of State Governor
quite as well —with the assistance of
an extra aide-de-camp to keep him
sober—as the average lord imported
at large expense.
When the Westralian Govern-
ment is at last driven to adjust repre-
sentation to population, there will be
a fearsome smash in Perth politics;
and the Five Families will go down
like a brick in the ocean. In Cool-,
gardie and Kalgoorlie, where the live
men are 2G50 p ..lb have two mem-
bers, while the dead men of Ashbur-
ton arid F. Kimberley have two mem-
bers for 155. The old fossils all re-
present small constituencies of two
m n and a dog that isn’t feeling well,
and when the inevitable readjustment
does come, the whole Ministry stands
a very fair chance of never being heard
of again.
A few days ago, in the tempo-
rary absence of Premier Reid, the
Convention decided to federalise the
public debts. There were 25 dele-
gates in favor, and only eight against,
and. as there are just 49 votes in the
Convention, the man in the chair
only coming in when parties are
evenly divided tins meant that an
absolute majority had been secured.
But Reid came-back, and heaved the
old threat that N.S.W. might feel
called upon to stand out unless the
vote was rescinded, and, owing to
that unlucky clause which provides
that there shall be no Federation un-
less N.S.W. is included, this meant
breaking up the whole movement. So
nine members—four Westralians, two
Tasmanians, and three from S.A. —
weakly changed sides, and one of the
few sensible tilings the Convention
has done went overboard.
Of nine Nelsonians returned to Q. Parlia-
ment by N.Q. electorates, four draw Parliamentary
salaries—Philp, Dalrymple and Chataway, <£looo
a y* ar apiece as Ministers ; Cowley, £lOOO a year
as Speaker—and Hamilton scoops in the emolu-
ments of Government whip. Of the five
Central Government members, none get any-
thing at all. The Wide Bay district has lost its
Minister in Tozer and has only Annear as
Chairman of Committees. The South is well
provided with Ministers Nelson, Dickson,
Byrnes. Foxton, Wilson and Barlow, the last-
named having no portfolio and drawing no salary.
Central Q. is thus governed by the extreme North
and the extreme South, in artificial alliance.
B. R. Wise :
A further and closer examination has led me to the
opinion, which I believe to be unassailable, that the
Referendum, in any form, in whatever circumstances, and
for whatever purposes, would strike a blow at the root of
Parli unentary Government, because it would make it
possible for a Ministry to remain in office without dis-
credit when it was unable to carry its measures through
Parliament.
In other words, Parliament rejects a Government
measure, and it is sent to a national vote. The
people accept it. The Government retains office
because the people are with it, whereas B. R.
Wise holds that it should go out of office because
the Parliament—which doesn’t represent the
people in this instance—is avainst it. So, in the
opinion of the academic B.R. vV., it is less impor-
tant that the people should govern than that the
old Parliamentary forms should be maintained.

intelligence can describe how the hon. member got here.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
No one has so far risen to point out the close
parallel between the stand which saved the last test
match for the Australians, and the stand which did the
same thing for the English in the closing match of
Stoddart’s last series. And yet the resemblance is most
detailed. In each case two good batsmen had fallen very
cheaply, Brockweil and Stoddart in the Englishmen’s
innings, McLeod and Hill in the Australians’. In each case
one of the players thus dismissed had also failed in
the first innings (Brockweil and Hill). A man who went
in first—Ward in the one case, and Darling in the other —
was a partner in the stand. The stand was effected by a
slow, defensive bat (Ward and Worrall), and by a power-
fully punishing cne (Brown and Darling) each time. Both
partners to it were eventually dismissed on both occa-
sions. Both matches were won by six wickets, the
bowling failing to do any more damage. The only
divergence is that, in the case of the English performance,
the rapid scorer (Brown) ran up his score and got out
before his partner, while, in the one lately put up, it was
the less venturesome player who went under first.
When cricket feels the sharp edge of the axe of
reform something will have to be done about l.b. w. There
is too much of personal equation in the rule as it now
stands, and quarrels, bad temper, and veiled allegations
of bias on the part of umpires are bound to occur. In
the last test-match Darling was given “ not out” once in
each innings, against fairly unanimous appeals, and
Ranji “out” over what looked a sufficiently doubtful
case to entitle the batsman to its benefit. All three
decisions from the Australian umpire. Until some auto-
matic registering apparatus; on a photographic basis, is
devised, which will follow the movements of a batsman’s
legs and show where they are when the hall hits ’em,
some simplification of the present complex rule is
urgently called for.
Re Richardson’s bowling and Sydney D.T.
thereon. The cricketing-seribe (writes a Bulletin corres-
pondent) had evidently been bursting with that little bit
of callow criticism a long time, and, with much ineptness,
got it off on the one day when it was most flagrantly in-
appropriate. I watched the bowling (with glasses) all
through the match. In the first Australian innings
Richardson’s efforts were beyond all cavil. His “length”
was splendid. In the second innings he began loosely,
but the ball that bowled Hill was perhaps the finest one
of the match. Then the Surrey man went all to bits.
He seemed to concentrate himself on mere pace (I saw
him doing it)and his “length” was childlike and bland.
The refusal of Bannerman to give a favorable ruling in
the appeal for l.b.w. against Darling was not the cause of
his wild play—it had been painfully erratic for many
overs before. But that seemed to complete his dis-
organisation, and, thereafter, the “one bowler of
the English team ” was not wanted on the voyage.
Can experts tell us why, with a player of Richardson’s
standing, length should be such a variable quantity?
Whv should the man who on Tuesday hits the same 3pot
every time with machine-like exactitude, send ’em in soft
and spotty on the Thursday when the weather conditions
are precisely similar? Oughtn’t it to be a purely mechani-
cal accomplishment ? Anyhow, these are the facts, that
Richardson's bowling on Monday and Tuesday was the
most brilliant feature of the match, and on Thursday,
with one or two balls excepted, beneath serious notice.

Re Ranji’s message for the boys of England ere
starting for his tour. Capt. Trott is now out with a
message to the boys of Australia. “Practice assiduously ;
never grumble; and try hard until the winning hit is
made. Let there be one captain, and, if possible, have
him always win the toss.”

Holdar M. : “Am satisfied that this ruthless
rout of English cricket will do —has done—more to
advance the cause of Australian nationality than could
ever be achieved by miles of erudite essays and impas-
sioned appeal. Nothing succeeds like success, and the
Australian is no exception to the admiration evoked by
the topmost dog. On that Wednesday afternoon when
Darling withered the remaining remnants of Hinglish
’ope, I marked a fresh era of patriotic pride stirring the
soul of Blankyland. Assuredly those silken bonds are in
a precarious condition.”

from S.A.C.A. to engage one or two ground bowlers for
Adelaide Oval. Jones is already employed on the Oval,
but refuses to be considered a professional bowler.
Adelaide has had no pro. since Jesse Hide left years ago,
and looal enthusiasts are anxious for a further experi-
ment.

Dear Bulletin. — Re par. in “Sporting Notions’*
(5/3 ’9B) about- Noble explaining his method of curling the
ball in mid air. I have seen the same thing done many
times by baseball players in America. The last time was
on 27th June, ’97 (Sunday afternoon), at a match between
a Chicago team and a team from Baltimore. Alongside
me sat an Oxford University professor who, before the
match, confided to me that he had only come there that
day to be able to prove .when he returned home that it
was impossible. But he had to believe his own eyes.—
Travelling Maorilandbr.
• • * *
During the visit of the English cricket-team to
Hamilton, Vic., “ the coming of the great Rajah” Ranji
caused all the local Hindoo hawkers to come round and
squat on their hams outside the Hamilton Club waiting
for their countryman’s appearance. Ranji, however,
didn’t appear, being too busy inventing excuses to the
Review of Reviews for the team’s recent knockout. Cer-
tain young squatters, tiring of the match, went back to
the club, grabbed a hawker “the dead spit” of Ranji,
took him inside and dressed him up in tall hat and frock-
coat like an A.D.C. at a garden-party. Taking a cab,
they started off to the match to give the people their
money’s worth. But Ranji No. 2 was too cunning—he
dropped out en route and took to the bush, fixings and all.
* * * *
As The Bulletin goes to press Victoria’s
match with the Englishmen looks like an easy win for the
latter. The local men went in first and scored 328 ; the
strangers following with 278. Towards the close of the
English innings rain fell, and the Victorians’ second
attempt was partly made in betwixt showers, with the
result of all wickets down for 132. This left Stoddart's men
with 183 to hit and win, and they laid the wood on
vigorously to the tune of three for' 138—which was the
score at time of writing. The match had no particular
points of interest, and the public earnestly stayed away.
And the commonplace person who suggests a fresh, dry
wicket for every innings of eaoh team talks to the winds
the same as ever.
* • * •
The Englishmen introduced a new effect into
their half-hearted struggle against Victoria, and the
novelty—like most fresh features in International cricket
—was a success. Little Board was put behind the
wickets, whilst perky Storer appeared in the character of
bowler and fieldsman. Result, four wickets for 79 by
Storer, two of them being due to Board’s smartness
behind the sticks. Also, for the first time, Mason was
treated as a principal bowler, and his record was four for
53. On this showing, Storer and Mason would seem to
have been entitled to lead the attack in previous
matches, but cricketing things are not what they seem.
* * • *
Stoddart’s team are dying tame, rather than
game. Witness the last performance in Melbourne. By a
strange coincidence the number of runs scored on the
first and second days, when the wicket played fast
enough, was only 283 each time.

Trotting stallion Honesty sold, the other day,
for 50 guineas. It is 15 years to-day (Thursday) since
Honesty trotted his fastest mile, 2min. 3Criksec., so his
new owner had best lose no time with him.

Rosehill Racing Club, N.S.W., intends in future
awarding a fairly substantial prize as a solatium to the
owner of the ever-uniuoky second. This move will be
appreciated by those who run their horses straight-out,
and thereby often secure the very barren honor of second
place.

Owners have responded well for trots at Sydney
Agricultural Show. Last year there was some little mis-
understanding over the inclusion of pacers in the trotting
exhibitions, but this year Mr. Webster has done the cor-
rect thing in making the competitions for pacers and
trotters. The entrants include such well-known per-
formers as Lucille, Charley, Cavalier, Syren, Harold B,
Fairbov, Tuxedo, and Picaninni. If Tuxedo is in any-
thing like form, the go between the little pacer and
Knight’s Syren should prove one of the events of the
Show.
* • * *
The curse of the trotting-race is the bottled-up
horse, which has every event in hand until the right time
comes and he is let loose. The fact of such a horse being
in a race cripples speculation and creates a field of non-
triers. And, unfortunately for the sport, everyone but
the adjuster of weights seems to know all about the good
things. Without mentioning names, the writer would like
to wager a bit that the next big Sydney trot will be won
bv a black horse, and a fine-looking animal at that, who
will probably start from scratch. Not so long since, a
well-performed mare won a race from a ridiculous mark,
the driver sending her with a watch in his hand, so that he
should keep her within a certain time-limit.
• * * •
Among the many reversals of form for which
Moorefield, Sydney, has become famous, it scored perhaps
a best-on-record last Saturday. Fassifern, carrying lOst.
61b., started a 6 to 4 favorite for Moorefield Welter Handi-
cap, and won as he liked. Naturally the public rushed
him for the Stewards’ Mile, in which he was weighted at
9st. 131 b. Starting at even money he failed to run into a
place. Farriday started in the Kogarah Stakes at 2to 1,
the next best being at Bto 1. He ran nowhere. In the
Stewards’ Mile he started at 6 to 1, and won handily from
Tripos.
* * • •
The V.A.T.C. is keeping up its reputation for
unsatisfactory decisions on protests. Ailsa Craig, a
despised outsider, won St. Kilda Cup, last Saturday',’ in
brilliant style, beating Goodman by a length and a half.
The victory left no room for supposing that the second
horse had been seriously interfered with. It was a
decisive win at the finish, anyhow, and the “books”
threw up their hats. Their joy was premature. The
owner of Goodman protested, on the ground of a cross,
and the despised outsider lost the race. An appeal to the
higher court of the V.R.C. will be watched with interest
• • * * s<’K

makes Mr. W. R. Wilson’s winnings for the present season
look formidable. They now total £9153, no mean sum for
eight months’ racing.
• * * *
The Collingwood tote-people are probably the
first in a civilised country to emerge victorious from a
protracted struggle with law norder. They have done
so, and they will apparently continue to do so as lon°- as
their funds hang out, for, as it now stands, their place is
impossible to carry under 15 minutes. By that time, the
whole of the conductors, the assistants and the gamesters
are scampering through the adjoining private houses
and therefore immune from arrest. The only entrance
is through a long right-of-way, bounded by brick walls
which has its end barricaded by a wooden wall 12ft’
high and faced with barbed wire. At the top is an in-
verted gin-case, pierced with holes, where a sentrv con-
stantly stands, and unless he reports favorably the tyler
below will not open the gate. Once past this barricade
the visitor is met by another a few feet further alon<>-’
and when this is negotiated he is in the yard and free to
gamble. On each side are low fences leading into private
houses, and on the outer boundary of an adjacent house
a high hoarding has been erected to prevent the detec-
tives identifying frequenters by means of telescopes.
This is almost an unnecessary precaution, too, for
the whole yard is almost covered by a low galva-
nised iron shed, the sides of which are enclosed by
an awning. The vigilance displayed at the gates has
utterly prevented the police getting in any number of
spies ; but, even did they flood the place, their chance of
convicting anybody connected with the working of the
tote would still be infinitesimal. The men who make the
wagers stand in a long cage of strong iron bars, at one
side of the allotment. They are dressed in long black
robes, wear masks, and are absolutely unidentifiable.
The cash-box has an opening, but in front of the opening
is a board, so that no winner sees who pays his money.
Behind the cash-box and cage are trap doors leadingTnto
the next house, and, as the detectives cannot enter that
house without fear of action for trespass, the tote people
escape every time. It was originally intended to prose-
cute the owner of the property, but it was found that the
nominal owner and the virtual owner were two different
parties. The only person who could legally be prosecuted
was an entirely innocent individual who had previously
sold the property, while the one that drew the rent was a
cunning landlord who declined to sign the transfer.
Under the circumstances, the police can do nothing but
break into the shop at every racing carnival and prevent
the promoter adding to the many thousands of pounds
which Collingwood already credits him with possessing.
Writes X.: “The Melb. detectives again raided
Wren’s celebrated fortified Collingwood tote-shop, the other
day, and occupied 15min. in knocking down back gates
12ft. high with axes, picks and crowbars. Meanwhile all the
inmates escaped through trap-doors, emerged into the
street through adjoining houses, and poked borak at the
officers. Now, providing Mr. Wren and the d.’s did not
understand one another, why were 12ft. high gates as-
sailed with battering-rams, an operation extending over a
quarter of an hour, when they could have been skipped
over noiseless!}' per ladder in 20sec. ? ”

“ With the result of the rubber match,” writes
Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ interest in
cricket fell with a dead, dry clump. Two single innings
and an eight wickets defeat in succession argumentatively
clattered across John Bull s brain and stunned him into
silence. The papers, after vainly grasping at all manner
of palliating excuses—(such as the Pall Mall’s explana-
tion that the heavy dew was responsible for the last three
English wickets flopping for nine 1 Dew in our midsum-
mer, at midday, Great Yellow Astor 1 No, the batsmen
were frozen stiff and howled with snowballs)—owned up in
one sobbing, manful heap : England was whopped squarely
and oblongly, Australia has the finest combination the
cricketing world ever saw, the game is not as interesting
as it once used to be, and Hill should be transported to
this country for life—without the option. So we are still
brothers."

paragraphist! Melb. Argus announces that “ Mr. George

* * * *
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League holds its Autumn
meeting on Sydney Agricultural Ground March 26 and
April 2 ; and, as the League has listened this time to its
very few members possessing sense, the result is a satis-
factory programme, which only needs fine weather to
ensure success. This time the'heats of the 2nd and 3rd
class handicaps will be run-off “surreptitiously” pre-
vious to race-day, and the Sydney crowd will bless and
praise the name of Sec. Hawley for the innovation. The
fact that the 2nd and 3rd class handicaps have been very
considerably squelched causes this paper to wish the
League good luck for its forchcoming meet. There are
many features worthy of note in the League’s programme.
Proceedings will begin with the Sydney Plate (£80),
limited to 20 first-class riders. The next race will be the
Amateur Handicap-affoiding the lily-white amateur an
opportunity to graft his gall out for the delectation of
barrackers. For this a record number of entries have
been received ; the March Stakes (£2O) has 120 entries ;
the Hampden Wheel Race (£75), 115. Another satisfac-
tory feature is the International Team Race, England v.
Australia, which should be a good draw. Also the Multi-
cycle Race (£18) will have 18 multicycles competing.
The League will hold its meet on the Agricul-
tural Ground because of the good Seating accommodation
that sec. Webster has now placed at its disposal with the
big new grand-stand and terraces ; and because the Agri-
cultural Ground requires only 10 per cent, of the gross
takings, as against the Cricket Ground’s 20 per cent. ;
further, because the Agricultural Ground cannot be
swamped by members and their wives, and sisters,
cousins, and aunts. The League claims to have looked at
the proposals of both grounds from pure, cold-blooded
business considerations; and doubtless whenever the
League got in doubt sec. Webster helped it out, with his
most comprehensive hand-shake and smile.
Of course, the League’s boom-day—the Zimmer-
man meet—drew 30,000 people to the Cricket Ground, but
the League’s second best day—the day that plugger Bill
Martin won six out of seven races—drew 20,000 people to
the Agricultural Ground. And on that day the Agricul-
tural Ground had comparatively no seating accommoda-
tion to what it has now. Referring to the day of
the 20,000 and plugger Bilmartin, this was the day
on which Walne made his debut in Sydney, winning a
£5O prize. He then weighed lOst; now, not two ye'ars
later, he weighs Hist. Good for the wheel as an advt.!
* • » ¥
The flight of plugger Bill Martin was rough on
Editor Bonython, of S.A. Advertiser. He published the
account of the Martin-Evans fight on excellent authority,
and when cast in damages for libel, because the witnesses
of the battle hung back and refused to give evidence, he
secured the prosecution of Swan for perjury, so that the
real facts of the case might be made known. Swan, to
save his own skin, induced several spectators to agree to
swear to the fight taking place, but meanwhile William
sailed for America. Swan was discharged “ without a
stain on his character," but the £5O awarded the cute
cyclist has gone to Yankeeland with him, and the lawyers
are richer by £lOO costs. Judge Way was cocksure the
fight did not take place, but now he regrets that he
allowed his “instincts” to outweigh the evidence.
Martin's bond (given when Swan was committed for trial)
was estreated, but, as it only amounted to a signature,
and there was no deposit of cash, that did not do him
much harm.

* * * *
The popular Cleveland wlseel, besides holding
the 10 and 20 miles road-records and one-hour amateur
path-record of N.S.W., also has the 100-miles world’s road-
record. The Cleveland is built strongly throughout, and
the ’9B models are among the mo. t finished cycles on the
market. The Sydney Cycle Co. Ltd., who are sole agents
in N.S.W. (or this wheei, are presenting their customers
with a souvenir in the shape of a watch or fob chain con-
sisting of four links of the splendid Cleveland chain with
a miniature sprocket wheel attached.*
* * * •
W. ,T. C. Elliott, who has just lowered by
several secs. Piatt-Betts’ record for the unpaced flying-
start quarter-mile, is one of the best all-round cyclists in
Australia. As a handicap rider he has won the ’97 Syd-
ney Wheel Race, £260; came second twice by a rim in
the A.N. A, ’97 and’9B, £llO each time; ran third in’94
and ’96 in the Austral Wheel Race. He is one of the best
pace-followers going, and, now that he has the best pace-
making in Australia at his disposal, he should cut down
the records considerably.
All this press apprehension about the
results of N.S.W. Attorney-General Want’s
refusal to seek extradition of the atmospheric
gas defaulters is pure hysteria. Maoriland has
for years refused to extradite, even from Aus-
tralia, embezzlers, absconding debtors and hus-
bands, and the like, unless the prosecutors
pay the expenses. And the volcanic province is
not yet eaten up with hordes of swindlers.
An ominous light is thrown upon the
prevalence of the cocaine habit by the recent acci-
dental poisoning of a doctor and his patient in
Melbourne. The medico, after injecting what ha
thought was cocaine into the patient, as an anaes-
thetic, gave himself an injection as a soporific,
and died of acute poisoning. Atropine had been
inadvertently substituted at the chemist’s for
cocaine. The Bulletin, by the way, knows a
man who, not long ago, took 20grs. atropine in
mistake for 20grs. antipyrine. Didn’t kill him,
either—but then he was a commercial traveller !
B. It. Wise, of N.S.W., demanded that
there should be no remedy for deadlocks in the
Federal Constitution, because “ deadlocks were
the price we paid for constitutional liberty.”
There was a time in England when smallpox was
so common that it might almost be said to be the
price that people paid for existence. (“ Servant
wanted, must be honest and industrious, and had
the smallpox,” was the kind of advertisement that,
was commonly seen in those days.) Presumably
B. R. Wise would like to preserve smallpox.
Acting-Judge Rogers, of N.S.W., says
that he is “old-fashioned enough to think that a
religious sanction is necessary to marriage.” One
thing which at least may be said on the other side
is that the secular ceremony is a lot more polite
than the religious one, inasmuch as it doesn’t call
upon the contracting parties to say things like
“with my body I thee worship.” The church’s
language is primitive, not to say aboriginal, at
times, and when a man leads his best girl, aged,
perhaps, 19 or 80, before a complete stranger, and
the latter begins making remaiks to her about her-
body, the proceedings may he very orthodox and
holy, but they are none too respectable.
Curious intermingling of farce with tragedy
in the recent Rookwood (Sydney) shooting case.
The grave-digger’s good offices in helping the old
man to a young wife; his reward with the “sack;”
his prompt return to manufacture in revenge a
couple of the cadavers through the falling-off in
the supply of which he had presumably lost his
employment;—it is hard to say whether in these
unique features of the gory episode the grim or
the grotesque is uppermost. Another remark-
able thing is that the female victim —with
a bullet in her spine, another in her right
lung, a bullet track from her left ear, through top
of right lung, to right armpit, and a hole through
the right temple to the cheek—should be still, not
only alive, but sensible and collected. Such
savagery of repeated shooting, also, combines
with what has since become known of the
slaughterer’s disposition to show that he is one of
those in whom rage is more than a merely nominal
furor brevis. When dismissed from a former em-
ployment for his insane ferocity towards the
horses under his charge, he made a display of such
murderous rage that some of the eye-witnesses, it
is said, predicted for him, some day, the rope
which he has now done his best to earn, though
the probability is that the lunatic-asylum would
have been the proper place for him long ago.


John Norton has been committed for trial
again. And what The Bulletin desires to say is
that if John is committed once or twice more he
will get so much advt. that he will wipe the floor

been born.
The Federal Convention grows more liberal
as it grows older. James Howe, of S.A., tried,
four weeks ago, to give the Federal Parliament
power to legislate on old-age pensions, and was
badly beaten. A week ago he carried his motion
by 26 votes to 4. The four members who voted on
the Tory side of the case were Barton, O’Connor,
and M‘Millan of N.S. W., and Lewis of Tasmania.
Which is a pretty bad advt. for N.S.W.

RETURN THANKS.
DR. ON LEE, after a residence in Australia
of about forty years, but mostly in New
South Wales, being about to pay an extended
visit to China, desires to return his SINCERE
THANKS for all the kindness and courtesy ex-
tended to him during that long period by all
classes of the community.
He would especially mention the Medical Pro-
fession for the liberal spirit always shown him ;
the Press, which has been uniformly fair ; the
N.S.W. Railway Officials and the Captains and
officers of steam-boats, who have ever been most
kind and attentive.
DR. ON LEE has found it impossible to call
on all his many friends, but will be glad to say

“ Tin Italiano in Australia : Note e Im-
pressioni ” (Milan, 1897). Mr. P. Munari, of
Sydney, has just published at the other side of
the world a book setting forth his impressions of
Australia. To see ourselves as others see us is
proverbially interesting, but generally entails a
shock to our self-conceit. The perusal of Mr.
Munari’s book brings, on the other hand, a shock
to our unassuming modesty. There is no doubt
we are, as a people, better off intellectually and
socially than the masses of the old world. To a
certain extent, higher ideals have become crys-
talised into our institutions and conditions of
everyday life. This the author has recognised,
but, in the pleasure of realising the contrast be-
tween his adopted country and that of his nativity,
he draws a picture of the state of affairs here that
most of us will be inclined to regard as over-rosy.
For instance, he says, speaking of the Australian
press—
Whoever has any ideas to promulgate, projects to moot,
blame to bestow, or answers to give, provided they are
not mere trivialities or personalities, finds a free field in
the columns of the papers. The liberty of the press is
unlimited for whoever expresses an opinion, a desire, or
resolution, whatever such may be; there are no laws that
“ regulate ” or rather blindfold it.
This may be relatively true as a contrast to the
state of affairs in Italy, where every paper is
brought out under the supervision of an official
censor, who reads the matter before allowing it to
be printed (very often before not allowing it to be
printed); but, alas! there are some among us
, who retain poignant memories in this connection.
1 And, again, speaking of the police: “ The major
part are married, and spend their lives tranquilly,
respected as useful citizens of society, and not
hated as odious tools in the pay of a tyrannical
Government for persecuting and repressing the
manifestations of thought.” [This The Bulletin
reads during the progress of a certain smellful
Sydney prosecution.] “ The laws, not the police,
rule in Australia,” and so forth. These mild
exaggerations apart, the writer takes a pretty
correct view, we think, of the Australian com-
munity and its prospects—certainly a cheerful
view. He finds the intellectual tendencies of the
people, resulting in, and in turn strengthened by,
the system of public instruction, superior in the
mass to what can be found in Europe ; a progres-
sive and liberal spirit is steadily materialising in
our institutions and at the root of our social con-
ditions, and he is confident that it is our destiny
to achieve the pacific solution of the social pro-
blem by this constant gradual evolution. Woman
here is, in point of emancipation and culture, far
ahead of her old-world sisters, and will therefore
contribute powerfully to the realisation of this
evolution. On the subject of Australian independ-
ence he says—
The Australians of to-day cannot be persuaded that
they ought to depend on England—being distant and
separated in spirit and interests—simply because their
fathers came from there.
And he concludes that the complete national in-
dependence of Australia is inevitable. The vitality
of the Federal movement is simply a manifesta-
tion of the growth towards this independence.
Altogether, the book is, at least, evidence that its
author ardently admires his adopted country and
has full confidence in her future—a species of opti-
mism which, when conjoined with advanced ideals,
is often a living power to work out its own justifi-
cation, and which, therefore, we should not be
sorry to see native Australians cultivate a little
more freely.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on nasre

Those who have tongues that never lie,
Truth on the lip, truth in the eye;
To Friend or to Foe,
To all above, and to all below.
These are the happy, these are the free,
So may it be with thee and me.

over Human pain ?

“S.L.” writes from Wyalong, N.S.W. :
Ed. Bulletin. —How's this for law ? A and B were
homestead selectors of adjoining blocks, and B refused to
erect his half of the dividing fence. A had consequently
to erect the whole of it, and intended bringing claim for
half cost before local Land Board. But before this could
be done B.’s selection was forfeited and all improvements
on forfeited selections revert, according to the present
law', to the Crown. B had made no improvements, but
the person who has now selected the forfeited block has
been officially notified that he will have to pay the Crown
half the value of the fence which A erected. Another
selector, whose land adjoins B’s forfeited block, has had
his fence, erected at his sole expense, up for over five
years, but is nevertheless informed that the Crown has
come into possession of half of it.—S.L., Temora.
One of those brilliant discoveries that lie
just under the public nose has been made by the
Sydney D.T. correspondent who suggests that, in
the matter of jams, pickles, and sauces, &c.,
Agricultural Show prizes should be allotted, not
upon the quality of articles specially prepared to
annex them, but upon the quality of the pro-
fessedly equal grade, as bought at haphazard from
the retailer. In a Word, hot upon what the com-
peting manufacturers can give the public, but upon
what they do give. Atrleib
S.M. Herald points odt, ftith quite uri-
usual vigor, thatN.S.W.Governtaentisnot Holding
its big land auction this month —the biggest thing
of its kind since 1882 —to raise money for the
present year’s revenue, which is, probably, in some
upside-down sort of way, the reason why all the
money is to be paid by 30th June, in time for
inclusion in the year’s revenue. Obviously, the
Government only demands the money so per-
emptorily because it doesn’t want it. The Herald
also points out that the land is only scraps and
remnants of no real use or value, which doubtless
explains why it includes selections as large as
624 acres, and why, even reckoning the half-acre
town allotments, the blocks average 110 acres
each. Also, the HERALD is of opinion that the
idea of the Reid Govt, being ili want of money is
too absurd to be worth mentioniiig, and as the
expenditure for the current year is already
i 8517,000 more than the revenue, why, of course,
it doesn’t want money. Any fool can see that.

“ Scribe,” to The Bulletin
Re the late Nathaniel Kinsman, tHe much-marrying
parson, of whom you printed a portrait recently. He
celebrated, in all, about 9000 weddings. This fact alone
explains the allegation that his. splicings figured more
often in the Divorce Court than those of any other cele-
brant. A wonder if they didn’t. Kinsman, indeed, did
as much as a whole big” denomination in himself. The
most searching inquiries by Govt., with a keen desire to
oust him, failed to discover anything essentially dis-
creditable. Kinsman was indeed a vast public benefit,
and he is certain to be replaced by another prac-
titioner of the same order, one who, , for 55., ec-
clesiastically varnishes oyer the dry, stodgy bru-
tality, as it were, of the registry. • The slanders
against him emanated from the clergy, because they got
two guineas, and upwards, in their limited way for doing
the same job. Kinsman was a legally and perfectly-
ordained clergyman, of nearly 40 years’ standing, of
unblemished character, thoroughly respected in the
suburbs of Fitzroy and Collingwood. To be sure, he
carried on the business of an auctioneer; but would it
not be highly creditable to the clergy at large if they
followed Paul’s example and did a little tent-making?
This ndarriage line happened to be thrust on Kinsman,
and proved lucky. He long officiated as minister of the
Free Church of England, in his little chapel, for
the love of the thing, before the marriage business
began to open up. Before this he was an ac-
tive member of St. Mark's, Fitzroy. With all his
estimable qualities, it cannot be denied that Kinsman
had the perfect ludicrous appearance of a Chadband—fat,
Uhctuous, clean-shaven, clad in rusty black with white-
chokers. The funniest thing was to see him in his auction
pulpit with the dames of Collingwood round him. He
used to say he could beat universal-provider Whiteley,
because he was able to provide the wife, too. In his
snuggery there was a little horsehair sofa, and he would
Say, “On that little sofa how many thousands of happy
couples have sat "—[Pinch for stale news.—Ed. B.j The
marriage-fees of his career during 30 years after all only
amounted to about £2OOO, or, say, £1 ss. a week, surely
not a thing to make a fuss about. That he was a kindly,
charitable, open-handed, large-hearted man is the mere
byeword of his locality.

An Australian Actor!!!
HE SUFFERS FROM NERVOUS PRO-
STRATION.
A PEFECT AND COMPLETE CURE.
WHAT MR. DAN BARRY SAYS.
The name of Mr. Dan Barry, the clever actor
and popular lessee of the Alexandra Theatre, Mel-
bourne, is one with which all Australian play-
goers are familiar ; it would be difficult, indeed,
to decide in which of the colonies his name and
admirable portrayal of various Irish characters
are least familiar. Mr. Barry is proud to class
himself as a self-made and educated man. From
the agg of elevep up to that of thirty-eight Mr.
Barry has retained his liking for a professional
life, graduating in a circus, following it up as
theatrical agent, joining a Melbourne paper, and
so on through a Bohemian life until he attained
the rank of manager and proprietor of his own
company. When a representative visited him,
the lessee of one of the most popular of Victorian
theatres courteously granted a request for a few
moments’ conversation. Mr. Barry said it would
be sufficient to fully explain the total benefit, he
had received from the use of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills.
“As I have told you,” said Mr. Barry, my life
has been a varied one, and there are but few
cities and towns in Australasia I have not visited
at some time or the other, and it will , not be
long,” he added, “before I visit America. A
theatrical manager has much to do mentally, and
as a result I commenced to feel the strain some
two years ago. A man of my profession, com-
pelled to be letter-perfect in some scores of charac-
ters, and having to attend to all departments of
the theatre, has his mental faculties fully em-
ployed. Frequently I felt, mentally and physi-
cally, quite exhausted. I was also affected with
neuralgia in the head, which often seriously in-
terfered with my duties. At night, my sleep was
restless, and of no recuperative benefit, and my
illness commenced to tell on me so seriously that
I consulted a medical man. He told me that my
system had run-down, and that I wanted a tonic.
He gave me some pretty extensive medical advice,
which developed into a large-sized bill. I would
not have had any objection to the bill had I re-
ceived any benefit; but I had not; so I tried a
second medical man, with no better result than
the consoling information that my evident state
of debility and want of tone required me to
take an extensive rest. This rest was, of course,
an impossibility. Noticing my indisposition, a
fellow - professional suggested I should try Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. I followed his advice, and
purchased a supply. After using these pills for
six weeks I found myself free from all signs of
debility and its attendant ills, and fully recovered
in spirits and health ; in short, ‘ Richard was him-
self again.’ ”
“ And you recommend them ? ”
“Yes, I do. If I see any member of my com-
pany showing the least signs of brain-fag or being
run-down, I recommend a course of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, and have never known them to fail.”

Racking Rheumatism!!!
A STARTLING ULTIMO CASE.
LAID UP FOR AN ENTIRE YEAR
A reporter recently visited Mr. Walter Tye, of
33 M ‘Arthur-street, Ultimo, Sydney, whose mar-
vellous recovery from a most severe attack of acute
rheumatism has been the subject of much com-
ment in that neighborhood. _ Mr. Tye proved to
bel a young man of great intelligence, and he
readily volunteered to give the following state-
ment of his illness and recovery :
“ It is some eighteen months ago that,after being
out in very inclement weather, I commenced to
suffer from aching agonies in the limbs and body,
and flying pains and stiffness in the joints ; and I
also experienced sudden chills, generally succeeded
extra work caused by moving from place to place,
for I have been a great traveller during the last
year or two. Anyhow, after my last journey I
began to feel very low and depressed in spirits ; I
could eat scarcely anything, and began to get
very thin. And as far as my color —well, I was
as yellow as if I had a severe fit of the jaundice.
An old friend of mine, who met me in the
street, looked me straight in the face and passed
me by without recognition, and she was posi-
tively shocked when I ran after her and
told her who I really was. I got from bad
to worse; the least exertion wearied me, and my
ordinary housework became too much for me. My
gums grew quite white, and my lips looked a
%
mi
V
*6
bluish-grey color. My sleep was restless and
disturbed, and I was so tired in the mornings I
could hardly get out of bed, and when I did I
used to lie on the sofa, feeling I hardly cared what
happened to me. I saw a doctor, who said I
needed a strong tonic, and I had several bottles
of physic from him ; but I grew no better. I was
in this miserable and hopeless condition when I
heard from someone who had been cured by them
about Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and I at once got
some. After taking a few I was agreeably sur-
prised to find a real and distinct change for the
better. I got rid of that miserable depression of
spirits, which in itself was enough to keep me ill,
and in a few days I was able to sleep the night
through without interruption. The Pills, too, gave
me a good appetite, and I began slowly to get
cheerful and fat again. My color came back, and
after taking six boxes I was completely cured, and
found myself as strong as ever. I shall always
remember my oure with gratitude, and shall, you
may be sure, recommend this wonderful medicine
to any of my friends who may have the bad for-
tune to suffer as I did.”

A Master.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The devil thatdwelt within him had stamped
its brand, undeniably,, upon his whole personality.
His face was pallid, to almost the hue of death ; a
pallor intensified by the curly, black hair, and the
eyes, so deep and fiery that one almost wondered
that they did not burn the high, slightly-arched
nose which kept them asunder. His thin, blood-
less lips were such that, when he closed them
•tightly, they formed only a livid, narrow cicatrice
‘beneath the black moustache.
He was passionless cruel adamant. None
.who had known him from his youth could remem-
ber that he had ever relinquished a design ; fore-
gone a revenge ; stooped to pity. Many a woman
could have declared him relentless, only that
women, after passing through the hands of such as
he, never speak—if they can help it. To him, a
woman was scarcely human; she was merely a
sentient thing, having no will, no power of
resistance where a man asked. Man signified
master, woman the slave, in his philosophy ; and
through his life he was true to his own faith.
He married ; he himself scarce knew why, unless
it were that he wanted someone ever at his hand,
upon whom to practise the essential tyranny of
his nature.
. To his wife he gave at first that kindness which
one might give to a captive bird, but with the
novelty departed the softness, and he became
■himself —pitiless and cruel. Often he regretted
that Nature had limited his inventiveness. The
schemes he could devise to harass and humiliate
the slave who bore his name were forceless com-
pared with his wish. That he had never beaten
her was solely because that would have been
coarse, and he abhorred coarseness. He must be
refined, even in his cruelty.
* * * *
- When the doctor brought home his newly-
wedded wife, folk who hurried effusively to
welcome her were chilled at their reception.
£?he was very tall and handsome, but she was
cold and haughty. Nothing seemed to move her
from her icy reserve. In her appeared no enthu-
siasfn, no feeling, no warmth. She might have
stoiffi to a sculptor for a statue of Virtue, and the
marble could scarcely have exceeded the model in
Chill tranquillity. This was the woman whocaused
the.Hivid, narrow cicatrice to move beneath the
black moustache, and the deep eyes to glow redly
over the high-arched nose.
Of his wife the man had wearied, and this
woiißm interested him. She was so calm upon her
pedestal of reserve that he coveted her as a child
might covet the stars, only that he knew he could
obtain her, and the cicatrice was very livid as he
maflb the silent determination to do so.
lii-the ball-room that night perhaps the lights,
the laughter, and the music moved him to volup-
tuousness ; perhaps the devil within him whispered
that this was as good a time as any.
The doctor’s wife sat apart, and he approached
her.
. have only one left,” she said, and she handed
him her programme. He wrote his name on the
gilded card, and sat beside her.
44 You looked lonely,” he said, his eyes on hers,
and his lips smiling.
44 1 am never lonely,” she replied, calmly. 44 1
have always myself—and my husband.”
44 Your husband ? He neglects you to-night.”
44 It is the way of husbands,” she said. 44 Your
wife is alone yonder, and you—are here.”
The corners of the bloodless lips came down im-
perceptibly, and the deep eyes glowed.
“My wife is doubtless glad that I am here —
rather than yonder.”
She was puzzled, and he saw it. He laughed
softly, and rose.
“ This is our dance,” he said.
44 Will you allow me to take you for some re-
freshment ? ” he asked, as the music ceased.
“No ? Then let us go out into the balcony. It
is very hot here.”
Outside, where the drooping greenery made a
pretty arbor in the lamp-light, he sat her opposite
him. For a time, she bore his gaze unflinchingly;
then something in his eyes caused hers to drop in
confusion, and a faint color flickered in her cheeks.
She half arose, as if to escape, but his voice
arrested her.
44 D0 you know that you are very beautiful?”
It was only a purring whisper, that seemed
scarcely to vibrate the air, yet she heard it dis-
tinctly.
“ My husband has told me so—often,” she said
quietly, her cheeks scarlet now.
44 Do you think of no one but your husband ? ”
Again the purring whisper.
“Excepting myself,” she replied, calmly.
44 You will, some day. You will think of me/”
44 You are insolent —or mad ! ” she said.
44 You will think of me,” softly, 44 because I love
you! ”
Her foot stamped passionately on the floor, as
she arose with an angry gesture to leave him.
With a quick movement he caught her arm, and
she was powerless.
44 1 love you, and I will win you. You think
you love your husband, but when you love me,
only then will you know What the passion is. No
man need fail with a woman. She must yield,
and you will yield. Will you yield now? Why
fight, when it is so useless ? ”
44 My husband will horsewhip you ! ” she cried,
passionately. 44 He will kill you.”
44 When you are mine, he will kill himself.”
He seated himself- She looked back at him as
she fled through the door. He was sitting half in
the shadows, silent, motionless, the thin bloodless
lips smiling sweetly.
* * * *
It was another night. The clock in the next
room had long since struck twelve, and the doc-
tor’s wife arose from the bed she had lain down
upon an hour before. She negligently drew on a
wrapper, and, taking the lamp, went into the ad-
joining room for a book. Her husband was absent
on a sick call, and she, solitary, was restless and
nervous. She had placed the lamp upon the table
and turned to get the book, when she heard the
door close gently behind her, and the soft sound
of a foot on the carpet. She turned quickly.
A man was leaning, quietly, up against the
door, his thin lips smiling, and his deep eyes gaz-
ing placidly at her. For a minute there was
silence; then a silver stroke rang through the
quiet house.
44 One o’clock,” he purred, very softly.
r ‘ You here ! ” Only fear prevented the words
from being a scream.
“Because I love you,’’ he returned, calmly.
“ Will you not sit down ? ”
44 If you do not go, I will call for help,” she said,
44 Very foolish,” he replied. “ That will makea
scandal—sooner than need be. ”
She sank, half-fainting, into a chair.
“Quite right,” he smiled; “what time to-
morrow will be convenient for you ? ”
“Convenient?”
44 Yes —-to come with me. I said you would
yield, and you will. Before Igo you will promise
to be mine before this time to-morrow night.”
She gazed at him, silently. From his pocket he
drew a paper and spread it on the table before
him.
“ I must have your promise in writing,” he said
softly, smoothing the paper. 44 Because otherwise
you might forget. I have drawn it out; you have
only to sign it, and I will go at once. Then, if
you break your promise, I will have to show this
paper—signed by you—to your husband, and then
—well.”
44 1 will sign nothing,’’ she cried, starting up.
44 You cannot force me.”
He smiled —went round, and placed the paper
quietly before her.
44 Sign,” he said, offering her a pen.
44 1 will not! ” she cried.
“Listen! Your husband will be here shortly.
What will he say if he finds me with you at this
hour? Very excitable man, the doctor ! ”
She sank down, sobbing, and then came a loud
knock at the front door.
44 Your husband,” he said calmly.
Her face lighted joyfully, and she struggled to
rise.
“No use,” he said, and taking a revolver from
his pocket he placed it on the end of the table
nearest the door.
The knocking was louder and impatient.
44 He is getting angry,” he said coolly. 44 He
will force the door in a moment.”
Then quickly he seized her arms and stooping
before her placed them over his shoulders. Only
there —he could not clasp them behind. His face
pressed down close to hers and he whispered—
-44 Thus I will hold you. When he comes in he
will be mad with rage. He will see that revolver,
and shoot us both. Do you hear ?—shoot us both !
Tnen he will go to the gallows, believing you had
betrayed him.”
Downstairs sounded a crash as of falling glass.
44 He has smashed the window,” the man said, re-
leasing her. 44 Will you sign?”
Her limbs were trembling, and a choking sound
was in her throat. He forced the pen between her
fingers, but they rested inert.
Hurried footsteps were heard on the stairs.
He seized her by the shoulders, and his grip
seemed to galvanise her arm to life. The pen
moved rapidly, and then clattered to the table.
The man smiled triumphantly, and was gone in
an instant. The doctor, entering immediately
after, found his wife senseless on the floor.
• • * •
The bloodless lips were at rest in the morning,
when their owner’s wife arose from his side. On
the dressing-table she saw a folded note. She
opened and read it, and sank back upon the edge
of the bed.
He was awake, and smiling at her.
14 You have read it, then,” he said. 44 1 placed
it there for that. . ,
After breakfast, he called her to him, ana
handed her a letter. ~ ~ T
“Take this to the doctor’s wife,’ he said. 1
have changed my mind.”
Rebellion was in her face. Carry his message
to the woman for whom he was going to ieave her .
She would not do it! . .
But she did—as she did everything he wished.
The letter ran “I am returning you your
promise. When I was wringing it from you, 1
scarcely knew whether or not I intended to bold
you to it. But you seemed so impregnable, and 1
was determined to conquer. I conquered, and 1
am satisfied. Bor the rest —well, I will stay with
my own wife for the present.”
P. T. FREEMAN.
He has arrived at last, and by-and-by a
common, ordinary pedestrian will be as rare a
thing as an up-to-date suburban councillor. A
Melb. firm now employs a man going messages on
pneumatic-tyred roller-skates. He gets over wood-
blocks and metal-roads in good style, too.
A house typical of the old convict-days is
now for sale —Macleay House, El izabetli -street,
Sydney, the old home of Alex. Macleay, N.S.W.
Colonial Secretary. It was built exclusively by
convict-labor at a nominal cost to the official
owner.
‘ 4 H,, ” concerning the late Toni Bracken :
It seemed my duty this morning to glance over the late
Tom Bracken’s volume of “Musings in Maori la nd, ’
scarcely having looked at him before. The volume
printed in Germany—must surely be in every way the
most creditable M.L. book to date. Prefaces by Grey
and Stout. I conclude from his writings that Bracken
was a Catholic, and spent most of his life in Australia.
His religion hampers his pen a little, but he has a
delightful freedom from the cynicism too characteristic
of the Australian poet. He dedicates his book to Tenny-
son. Bracken has no depth. He has read Shelley much,
and pleasantly emulates the descriptive parts of the Pro-
metheus, without ever touching one of those chords
which mark the poet par excellence. Bracken is just a
nice, gifted, silvery, readable writer. His occasional
pieces are much above the usual calibre of those things,
and may rank with such striking off-hand verse as Shirley
Brooks contributed to London Punch. They must have
hit hard at the time. One upon the death of the sympa-
thetic actress, known as Miss Fanny Cathcart, Mrs. Robt.
Heir, and Mrs. Geo. Darrell, has the charming idea of
commencing each verse with a line from one .of her
favorite parts. Bracken tried too many styles indeed,
almost all. No great poet has such variety as he affected.
The reader nearly always has an underlying reminiscence
of Wordsworth, Longfellow, Kendall, Gordon, or some
other of his masters.

A young woman, aged 26, residing in
Dunedin (which is Scotch and Calvinistie) recently
made application to the Mayor of that city to
have her fourth illegitimate child committed to
the Industrial Schools. Also, she applied to the
authorities of the Benevolent Asylum for an order
for admission to the lying-in ward, as she was in
hourly expectation of becoming an unmarried
parent for the fifth time. And his flabbergasted
Worship said that she ought to be put away in
the idiot asylum.

The Quiet City.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We came to the quiet.eity, where quiet people be,
By the shore of a shoreless river that turns to a
tideless sea,
We came to this silent city, where the silent people
lay,
In the year of Ever-never, in the land of Night-
and-Day. .
But lest, in the town enchanted, there worked o’er
. my Love its spell, - *
She wore in her hair a garland of the woven im-
mortelle ; . !
The pearl on her bosom glowing was not more
rare than she,
The rose at her heart that quivered might not as
lovely be.
But there burst no band of music, and no organ
-swelled sedate
As we crossed the arched entrance of that city’s
outward gate;
The sound of our steps but echoed thro’ its quaint
and narrow street,
The sound of our words which faltered, the sound
of our hearts that beat.
They dwelt on the quiet highway, yea, an hundred
thousand strong,
But they rose not up in welcome, and they came
not forth in song,
And there cursed no man at our coming, and there
asked no maid of our quest,
Though the flower at her heart had withered, and
a Hand to my heart seemed pressed.
We cried to the quiet townsfolk, we called unto
some by name, ",,
But none to our calls responded, and none from
his dwelling came.
We knocked, but they made no answer ;we wept,
but they heeded naught.
I said to my Love, “A marvel, and one with a
meaning fraught 1 ”
“ They are mute in this quiet city—mayhap they
are all away
In the Land of the Ever-Never, in the country of
Night-and-Day,
Or they speak in the Silent Language that maybe
the quick cannot hear; ” : ■ ’ ■
Then trembled my Soul as she whispered, “ Haste,'
Love, ere the night appear ! ”
Pale too were her lips as we hurried, but paler her
cheeks as she spake: ;
“ Dear Love, will they sleep tor ever ?• 7 Dear
Love, will they never awake ? )
Will We in the Time that is coming, the Time of
the Long Ever-more, , '
So rest as these Others are resting, so sleep with
the close-fastened door ?,
I said to my Love as we hurried, “ ’Twas written,
' sweet-heart,'An J d ’£ will be;
The river rolls backward not ever, but onward at
last to the sea.” r - ■ ‘
t " Love, cried, my Love in sore sorrow,
. “’t w.ere well had two streams never met,
1o mingl'e mad mirth' and sad music - to know and
to be—and'forget I ”
We passed through the streets together where the
quiet people lie
Under the rain and the sunshine, under the cloud
and the sky ;
Out where the day springeth never, out where the
; moon never glows,
Out where each man for his neighbor hath builded
nn ; strange, ho uses in rows.
We came from that quiet city where those quiet
people be; . ,
I turned to my Love and trembled ; her great eyes
turned to me.
Then poured we red wine together, then filled we
our glasses high,
And drank to the quiet people in silence, my Love
• . and I. ,'
E. J. BRADY.
Now that Q. Govt, has decided to prose-
cute Q.N.B. ex-directors and auditors, it would be
interesting:. to know upon what given material
Messrs. Pilcher and Sly, of Sydney, to whom the
case was submitted, based their opinion that no
prosecution could lie against the defendants.
The picture to be presented to the sub-
scribers of the English Art Society this year is a
specially fine engraving of E. A. Abbey’s noble
painting,- “ Richard Duke of Gloucester and the
Lady Anne.lt is a magnificent subject magnifi-
cently dealt with, noble in conception and superb
in composition. It is a picture that seizes the
attention as in a grip of iron, and holds it unre-
lentingly.' The treatment is eminently decorative
without ceasing for a moment to be remorselessly
realistic. It is undoubtedly one of the finest
effects in mediaeval dramatic art ever attempted
by Abbey—or by most other people.
To The Bulletin, from Tasmania
Ben Tillett showed his square jaw in Hobart the other
day, and was received with a plentiful lack of enthusiasm
by the men in moleskins, and a half-timid, half-tolerant
curiosity by the officially respectable. Between the two
icebergs Ben would probably have been frozen but for
his own internal Are and the warm welcome of a faithful
few. These latter demanded, and got, a mayoral recep-
tion for “ Aid. Tillett, L.C.0.” The sturdv 'little agita-
tor’s personality somewhat thawed the tallow-fats on the
local council, and they even “ hear-hear'd ” Tillett’s in-
teresting talk on L.C.C. matters; but when he spoke out
for the dernocratisation of municipal institutions, the
representatives of Proputty looked at the clock and
glanced furtively towards the door. Tillett’s frigid recep-
tion in Hobart is easily accounted for : Labor organisa-
tion is a dead-letter in that city—the only apology for a
union (the typos’.) has just gone down badly in a dispute
with the local evening print—and an agitator of Tillett’s
stamp is 100 years ahead of the average Labor thought of
the place. It was different on the W. Coast mining fields.
There the Miners’ Union welcomed the visitor with bands
and banners and packed meetings, and greeted him as a
well-beloved comrade. At time of writing, he was trying
to tear himself away from the hammer-and drill artists
for the purpose of having another shot at Hobart’s hope-
lessness per medium of a big free meeting. Hobart
being great on free entertainments, Tillett may count on
at least one decent-sized congregation to preach at.
LAND OF THE CROCODILE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Solomon in all his glory was not arrayed
like the Archduke Charles of Austria when he
boarded the Merrie England to call on Caledonian
Gov. Macgregor, of Papua. Never had Ormuz or
Ind showered on their kings more “ barbaric pearl
and gold ” than glittered on the attendant staff.
And they were received on the Merrie England
by a plain, broad-shouldered man in serge trousers,
a Crimean shirt, and a light twill coat. That was
Gov. Bill Macgregor, a modern sterling “ armed
soldier of Democracy,” in whose big heart there is
much of that old “ fierce native daring which
instils the stirring memory of a thousand years.”
A Dutch Admiral who had visited Macgregor some
time before presented himself in such dazzling
splendor that the Papuans were inclined to fall
down and worship him as a god ! And the Dutch
Admiral and the heir to the throne of Maria
Theresa both wondered if that plain man in the
Crimean shirt was really the Governor of British
Papua and representative of the British Govern-
ment.
* * * *
Next day when the Archduke and his staff
came ashore on a shooting expedition, they were
chiefly clothed in velvet, with capacious leggings
and silver-handled hunting-knifes. The lawyer-
vines fairly laughed as they lifted off the plumed
hats of these Nimrods, hooked them up by both
ears, or engraved cabalistic characters on their
gable-ends. The hunters swore in seven distinct
Austrian-empire lingoes. Next day they went
ashore in shirts and trousers and straw hats—and
got lost! The Austrians in camp rushed madly
round, and fell against each other, and tumbled
over logs, and yelled in the aforesaid seven dis-
tinct languages, and started platoon musketry-
fh’ing until the welkin was knocked senseless. As
darkness descended, and the Archduke had not
turned up, Mac quietly started with a lantern and
four Papuans, and found the prospective emperor
and his followers three miles away, stuck up in a
lawyer-vine scrub and preparing to camp ! The
Archduke observed that Diogenes Macgregor with
his lantern was the most beautiful sight he had
ever seen in this world.
* * * *
In the staff of no other potentate on earth
could the following incident have happened. It
represents the philological labyrinth in which the
Austrian empire is entangled. The Archduke
had shot a wallaby and sent it to be cooked. The
bearer spoke German ; the cook was an
Italian, and neither could speak the language
of the other ! The instructions got hope-
lessly mixed, and both parties grew angry.
Just as the German was about to bash the cook
over the head with the wallaby, and the cook was
going to retaliate with a saucepan, Macgregor
came to the rescue as interpreter, and Sauerkraut
and Maccaroni fell into each other’s arms and
wept! Just picture a British Governor, on
the savage shores of Papua, interpreting
between two members of the Archduke of
Austria’s own household staff. No wonder that
Czechs and Germans drag each other round by
the scalp locks in the Austrian Legislature !
* • * *
The Archduke’s chief joy in the primeval
forest of Papua was to sit on a log and eat straw-
berry jam out of a tin with the handle end of a
spoon ! He was pleased with everything and
everybody, and is evidently a polite and amiable
man whose policy of “tacit acquiesence” may be
fatal if applied to the empire he will one day be
called to govern.
* * * *
One of the labor - organisers who went
out to N.W. Q., previous to the strike troubles,
was a bit of a new-chum. He called on a squatter
near Longreach, and asked him if he employed
any cheap, labor. The squatter said he only had
t n aboriginal stockman, from the Condamiue!
Ah, well, said the organiser, who thought the
natives of the Condamine were Papuans, in the
care of missionaries, “you will be able to get me
his birth certificate.” The squatter fell all of a
heap into a.canvas chair, and next day the new-
chum organiser learned that Queensland gins are
not particularly regular in registering the birth of
their children!
* * * »
A comic drama at the mission station, at
the mouth of the Batavia River, in Cape York
Peninsula: Japanese and Malay pearlers land
within three or four miles of the station, and buy
native women with flour, sugar, and tobacco.
The women go on board the boats in the evening
and return to the shore next day. The mis-
sionary (Rev. Mr. Hey) decided to appeal to the
manhood and self-respect of the aboriginal men.
He told them they would be knocked on the head
m short order if they went to the Japanese and
Malay country in search of women ! Three days
afterwards, a band of Coen River myalls marched
up to the station escorting five Japanese prisoners,
with their hands securely tied behind their backs !
Five subjects of one of the great naval Powers of
the age, captured and inarched to a Presbyterian
mission-station by a rabble of naked savages!!
The five Japs fully believed they were going
to be killed and eaten. But the Coen
myall has a keen sense of humor, and the
desire for loot is strong within him. They
liberated one Jap, and sent him on board to bring
four bags of flour and a lot of sugar and tobacco
as a ransom ! Then the liberated Japs boarded
the lugger and sailed away in all haste, after
christening the locality the “ Coast of Devils ! ”
